O, son, since I have completed for you this book, which is the culmination of the science of medicine,  through its completion, and through its explanations, and through its clarifications, it seems to me that I should complete this for you with this treatise, which is a part of manual operation, that is, surgery.
Because manual operation is neglected in our region and in our time, almost entirely deprived, so that the science of it is in great peril, and its practice is lost; and there remains of it only some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which are mostly corrupted and thus lead to error and hesitation, so that its intentions are obscured and its strength and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I should revive it with the ordering of this treatise, following the path of explanation, clarification, and abbreviation; and so that it comes with the forms of cautery instruments and other surgical instruments, as is appropriate, through the addition of clarification, and through their preparation, which is a necessary matter.
But the reason why a skilled artisan with his hands is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is lengthy, and it is necessary for its practitioner beforehand to be trained in the science of anatomy, which is quite difficult, so that he knows the functions of the limbs and their forms, and their connections and separations; and he should have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, and their number and location; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their emergence.
And for this reason Hippocrates says that in name it is much, but in practice little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book; for he who is not knowledgeable in what we have said about anatomy is not exempt from falling into error by which men will die, as I have seen many such cases, for some are trained in this science and practice it without knowledge and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and reckless physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he incised some arteries of the neck, so that a great deal of blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who was elderly, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and it happened that a black slave who was under him suffered a fracture in his thigh; and the physician, in his ignorance, extracted the fracture, over the wound, with cushions and splints with tight binding, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he unbound it according to his wishes, and then he bound it again, and then he left it like that for some days and commanded that we not unbind the bandage; and it remained so until the thigh and foot were suppurated, and it resulted in loss (of the limb/life).
And I was called to him, and I prepared to unbind the bandage, and tranquility was achieved, and thus a lessening of his pains.
But however, corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated after some days, until its malignancy was magnified by its lord (i.e., the condition worsened greatly).
Which is why, because the burning cancer is full of melancholic humor, then it is not suitable that it be approached with iron at all, but that it be in a limb which suffers so that it is entirely contained (or, that it be in a limb that can be entirely removed).
And therefore, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be divided into two divisions: that is, into an operation which is associated with health, and into a work in which there is peril in the greatest circumstances (or, in the most serious cases).
And I have already explained this in every part of this book, in which operations come which contain error and fear; therefore it is necessary for you that you guard against this and leave that (i.e., avoid those operations), lest fools find a way to speak and dishonor you.
And therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with God's protection, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method, by the grace of God, leading to health and praiseworthy success, and leave the terrible diseases of difficult healing, and abstain from those things that cause you fear, so that wavering in your faith and in your reputation does not overtake you: for it remains more for your glory, and higher in the world and behind your holiness.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you be called a bad physician.
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter deals with the head down to the feet, the forms of instruments and cautery tools, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on suction and perforation, and on cupping glasses, and on wounds, and on the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on reduction and dislocation, and on the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and on the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I describe the qualities of the benefits of this cautery and its drawbacks, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and its drawbacks is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and a small part of their discourses suffices for me, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, whether with matter or without matter, except for two complexions, which are cold complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not suitable for a disease which is caused by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry disease should be healed with something that is hot and dry.
And I contradict those who say the contrary, that benefit is achieved through cautery in every hot and dry disease that occurs in the human body, because when you compare the human body and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the human body cold.
And I say, regarding this discourse, that experience has revealed this to me several times.
However, it is not fitting that this should come from someone who has already been exhausted and exercised in the chapter on cautery by prior practice, and who has understood the diversity of human complexions and the disposition of diseases in themselves, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the duration of those.
But in other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and humid diseases, because all physicians are agreed on those, and they do not disagree on the benefit of cautery in them.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and its goodness, is superior to cautery with burning medicine, because fire is simple, whose action does not reach beyond the member which is cauterized and does not harm the other members adjacent to it except with little harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches those members which are distant from it, and quickly brings about in the member a disease of difficult healing and quickly kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except to remove superfluities. And it is known to us by experiment and by long service of study with the art concerning the truth of the causes; and therefore I am excused from the length of discourse. And if it were not that it is not written in my book in this place to reveal to you this secret concerning fire, and the quality of its operation in the bodies and its expulsion by which it expels illnesses, with philosophical demonstrative discourse, which would be difficult to understand in your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree on the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, and they posit springtime as the best time.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by accidental timing is overwhelmed by the benefit which is obtained by cautery, and especially if the cautery is for necessary, strong and sudden pains, which do not allow for delay because it is feared that those pains might become worse, which is a greater harm than a small harm introduced by timing.
And this does not enter your minds, O sons, this is only considered by the vulgar people and foolish physicians, that is to say that for that illness which is cured by cautery, there is no unsuitable time, and they posit that consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they believe, for cautery is only in the place of a medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
However, cautery is superfluous over medicine because of the swiftness of its efficacy and the power of its operation and the greatness of its effect.
And it is possible that the illness may return at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of the illness, and the strength of the patient, and what is prepared in the patient's body from the aggregation of superfluities within, and what has been acquired by the patient through diet and similar causes; but if the illness to which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a limb with few superfluities and humors, as is cautery of a tooth for pain and similar cases, it is possible that the pain will not return, but this happens rarely.
And it is more common speech that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and this is a true saying, not in the sense that they understand it, for they believe that it is not a cure that profits with medicine or any other cause except cautery.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer methods of cure in certain illnesses, and those medicines do not help, and then at the end of the treatment, one administers cautery, and it helps in that case. And therefore, from this it is understood that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and many foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the evenness of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not scar, although that is not entirely true, for I have already proven this and found that it does not do so except in some bodies and not others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron, as they said; however, when you do cautery with fire with gold, it is not clear to you, because it is heated according to the quantity you want by the roughness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you apply too much heat, the gold melts from the fire, and some laminae fall into the fire, causing the craftsman trouble from this.
And because of this, cautery with iron is done more easily and more readily with precision of operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those from the head down to the feet, so that I might explain to the one asking what he wants from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization is applied to dampness and coldness on the brain; the accidents of which are two causes: that is, a cause of sudden and profuse rheumatic flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and excessive sleepiness, and toothache and sore throat, and universally any illness that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, sudden stroke (apoplexy), and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is this: first, order the patient to be evacuated with purgative medicine, cleansing the head, for three or four nights, depending on the strength of the patient, his age, and his constitution.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit between your hands on a low bench and place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm near the  [likely meaning:  a specific point near] the bridge of the nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that point, mark the spot with ink, and then ignite the olive cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place that [the cautery] on the spot marked with ink, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand is gradually constrained, and you revolve it.
And then lift your hand slightly and observe the place, and if you see some quantity of the head of the bone uncovered, like the size of a grain of barley, then lift your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron, or with another like it if this one is cooled, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the location and leave it for three days.
And then place on it cotton soaked in vinegar and leave it there until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the area with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And it has been said that when the wound remains open, it better discharges the arm, because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the elbow should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's substance is hardened/burned.
And then scrape away what is burned from the bone, and then let it be healed.
And others say that with the cautery, penetrate to the back, until it firmly penetrates the bone, until the bone is charred, and this is the manner of a small ulcer. And they believe that vapors from the head exhale from that place.
And let the wound be kept open for a long time, and then let it be healed until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men, as a method of fear; and leave that, it seems to me, better is health when it is; for the head is weakened by the solution of its continuity, as we have testified in the other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a cold nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and with that I use and with that I work for health.
Chapter two.
Concerning the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs throughout the head, and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches and cupping and purgatives and plasters and poultices and cauteries and unguents and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we recount and none of those things benefit him, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution by nature; and if it is not weak and finds much great cold, then cauterize that with another cautery a little below that one. And then cauterize that above the temple of his head with a cautery until the scab is formed. Incise the scab and let the bone be uncovered to the extent that we have recounted, and cauterize that with a cautery in the back part of the head, in the place which is called the vas - and this is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And ease your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient thereby finds much great pain, different from the pain of all the other cauteries of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place, because it is necessary that the cautery with which the temples of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter Three.
Concerning the cauterization of a not-ancient migraine.  (or, Concerning the cauterization of a recent migraine.)
When a sudden pain occurs in the head, or a spreading pain around the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the various diseases; and if nothing helps, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with an acute, burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and you crush it and bind its extremities on both sides. Then incise the location of the pain in the temple with a lancet until a place is made under the skin, so that it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a bandage for a continuous constriction, and leave it for three hours; then untie it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then apply a cotton swab soaked in vinegar until the area becomes inflamed.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner: heat a cautery iron of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail or peg, because its head is shaped like a nail, which has a certain curvature, and in the middle of it is a small eminence.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the flesh underneath is not burned — and move the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount of flesh that is burned is as much as half of it, due to the specific nature of the cautery, then lift your hand so that the artery beneath is not burned, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the area, and leave it for three days; and then apply cotton soaked in balm, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of the scalpel according to the cautery method, and be careful of cutting the artery in this specific, not-ancient, type of headache.
Chapter four.
On the cauterization of chronic headache.
When you treat a headache with what we have said in the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and sometimes the illness is such that what we have said about the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is insufficient, then you should heat the cautery knife until it is white-hot, after marking the location of the pain with a line whose length is half a finger, or about that; and place your hand there for a moment and press it until you incise the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be mindful of the continuation of the jaw, which is moved during mastication, and that the muscle not be injured and the nerve which moves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the guarding and caution regarding the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, for there is danger in its incision, and especially for one who is ignorant of what he does and is not studious and experienced; and leaves the operation to him at the beginning.
And I will come to the recollection of the treatment of accidental bleeding from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see from this illness that this cauterization is insufficient and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize it in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of arteries with a cautery having two blades, and this cauterization is better and more suitable.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5: On the cauterization of earaches.
When pain in the ear comes from cold and is treated with laxative remedies and with the other treatment which I have mentioned in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is what is called a "puncta," of which this is the form:
And then apply the cautery point by point, and let it be moved around the entire ear as a wheel is turned, or in the circumference of each part if the pain is located in a specific part. Extend the cautery from the root of the ear little by little after you have marked the place with a probe.
And if the cautery requires ten points in the whole ear, or however many, then treat those places until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
On the cauterization of a cough.
A cough which is cured with cauterization is only of those types which come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of illnesses.
Avoid cauterization of the type of cough that arises from dryness or from a spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of cough with leeches and purgatives and gargles, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected area, for softening only comes to the part that appears healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the upper ear, a little above the temple, and another in the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the little finger, and hold your hand with the cautery until half the thickness of the fingernail is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of knife, the form of which is shown above/previously described. However, it is more subtle if that is done slightly as you see; and it is necessary to cut it with a little less excess thickness.
Then treat the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have said, and your treatment does not help, and the patient has no fever, then cauterize them with four cauteries:  on all four corners of their head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And it is also cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; and it is better behind; and then let it be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the cauterization of forgetfulness done by flame.
So that large enemas and purifying pills are given to the patient at the beginning.
And then shave the patient's entire head and apply to their neck a plaster of cetre [a type of plaster, likely containing a specific ingredient] described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cautery. And do this according to the same order which I have already said.
And therefore if he is healed by these means, it is good; and if not, then make for him three cauterizations in the back part of his head, which descend from the uppermost part of the head down to the lower part of the neck. And place between each cautery the width of a finger, and then treat the cautery with what has been prescribed.
But if you add to it and the patient tolerates it, then apply cautery to the middle location; and if you want a greater addition, then cauterize that over the two horns.  (Here "horns" likely refers to a branching or spread-out location related to the initial cautery site).
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form given before.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
That you proceed in the purification of the head with herbs and with that which we have said; and then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery over all the horns of the head, and with a cautery over the back part of it, and with three cauteries over the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to find it and the patient tolerates it and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries over the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery until it is numb from the burning of it, and raise your hand. And then care for it until it is healed, and this should be an olive cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore it is necessary that you purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatments which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and can tolerate the expulsion of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, before that for many days, with the improvement of the diet.
Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on his occiput, and cauterize above each temple. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, as has been previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small/fine cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are corrupt humors and a thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cauteries which we have described in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is a superfluity inclining towards melancholia, and the patient's body is humid, then give him something to drink that cleanses his brain, according to what has preceded in the division (of treatments).
Then shave the patient's head, and then make a properly-made poultice for him on a circle (possibly referring to a bandage or cloth), and then place that in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright, and held on all sides (supported).
Then apply a liniment; and it is more worthy (better) to use old sheep's fat. And heat that over the fire with moderate warmth, which the said sufferer feels when it is applied to him. Then pour that into the middle of his head within the circle (bandage), and leave it there until it is cold.
And do this to the patient once a week, with his other treatment, until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize it making small punctures according to his need, but hold your hand with a cautery thus prepared: this is a kind of cautery which moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply that cotton soaked in butter or goose grease.
Chapter 12.
On the cauterization of water descending into the eye.
When the beginning of water in the eye is declared to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something to purify his head, and guard him from all humidities.
And make him sweat in a bath for some days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the beginning of the descent of water is in two eyes, or on one side if the beginning of the water is in one eye.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the occiput (back of the head); and let the cauteries be far apart in the width of the temples.
And beware of a flow of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see a flow of blood from it, stop it with some medicine, which is possible for you.
And I come with wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries placed separately.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears.  (This likely refers to long-standing wounds or sores, using "tears" in a metaphorical sense.)
When the tears from the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest area of the head externally, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we have recounted in the beginning chapter about water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it needs additional treatment, then cauterize it in all the area of the corner of the eye above the end of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (or nasal stench/foul smell).
When you treat this with what we have said in the section and your treatment is not effective, then you prepare and give the patient  decoctions to drink for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize him with a medium-sized cautery, with an olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize that with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauterizations above the two eyebrows, a little below the hair.
And be careful with the cauterization so that you do not burn it.
And of the clove-shaped cautery, this is the form/shape.
Chapter 15.
Of the cauterization for the softening/mollifying of the eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize that (area) above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries in each part, and they should be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too much with the cautery, but only to the extent that a third part of the eyelid is hardened/cauterized.
Chapter 16.
Concerning cauterization of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inwards and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inwards so that they prick the eye, cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, for cauterization is either done with fire or with a burning medicine.
If cauterizing with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave their eyelashes like those who cleanse them, until they are long and thick; and if they prick near their roots, then bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so that they are not injured until the eyelashes have grown.
Then, when they have grown and thickened, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid of their eyes with a probe, with a mark that is shaped like a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or in mallow mucilage on the eye.
And then heat the cautery, whose form is this.
And then cauterize over the mark that you made, little by little, in many times, until the surface of the eyelid which is like a myrtle leaf is burned, all properly apparent.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid already contracted and the hairs raised above the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with a poultice on it until the scar is healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if some matter returns through the hairs near their origin and the eyelid is softened, then perform the cautery again on that area, exactly as you did before.
But if the hairs are under the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is even, so that the hairs do not touch the eye.
But cauterize with a caustic medicine; this means that you instruct the patient to pull the hairs until they are elongated and even, and then a leaf-shaped piece of linen is applied.
Then take known soap and quicklime, one ounce, or something like that, and mix these ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; then spread this on the linen shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place this on one or both eyes; and place under the eye a cotton ball soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it lightly, but do not move it until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like fire.
And when you perceive that he feels the bite, then leave the medicine and move it with your finger; when the bite ceases, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then observe if you see that the eyelid is raised as it should be, relieved of the constriction by fire or incision, for then it is well; and if not, return to applying the medicine to the place where the medicine has not yet been applied and it does not darken until your work is even and the eye is contracted.
Then place cotton with a bandage on it until the scab from the cauterization is gone, and then treat it with healing ointment or something else until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to watch with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after a few days is softened and some hair grows in the eye, then repeat your medicine on that part of the eyelid which is softened, and exactly as you did in the beginning, and then bandage it until it is healed.
And this is the form of myrtle leaf. (Referring to a shape or method described previously, likely a shape for applying medicine or a bandage)
And know that the eyes of men are diverse in size, both large and small, and therefore your constriction (or perhaps contraction/adjustment) should be made accordingly.
And the path of righteousness will not be hidden from him who studies this art. (This is a metaphorical statement implying that proper technique will be revealed to the diligent student).
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have mentioned in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is not successful, then you must cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, and let a minister hold his head between your hands in such a way that his head is not moved or shaken.
Then place cotton soaked in egg white or gum arabic on his eye.
And then heat the cautery, whose shape is this:
which should be concave, like the shape of a cane or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cauterization is performed.
And if you wish, let it be penetrating to the other extremity. And if you wish, let it be sharp like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
Then place over it the cautery, which should be well ignited, and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not waver or the patient is disturbed, and then place the cautery on the granulation of the eye and cauterize it.
And so if at the beginning your cautery penetrates to the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then apply a bandage over it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which some ancients mentioned: approach the location of the fistula and open it, and then place in that fissure a thin tube, which is shaped like this:  And pour into it the quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand with a cloth with good retention, and the patient should not be moved so that the molten lead does not reach his eye; and it is necessary to place over the patient’s eye a cloth soaked in egg white or water, for the lead adheres to the location of the fistula and heals it with marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to make an opening in the nose and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose according to where it will return to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the cauterization of the cleft of the well (likely referring to a fissure or wound).
Often fissures occur on the lips, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat those fissures with what we have said in the divisions and your treatment is not effective, then ignite a small, knife-like cautery according to this form which is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the subtlety of a knife.
Then carefully place that ignited cautery into those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
Then treat it with cerates until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops and then breaks, and then becomes chronic, and pus flows and a fistula is formed, and you treat it and the treatment is ineffective, then you should ignite a cautery according to the size of the fistula, and then place that ignited cautery into the opening of the fistula, holding it with your hand until the heated iron reaches the bottom of it, and afterwards do this once or twice; and then treat it with what we have said about treatment until it is healed.
And so if the wound is excised and heals, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to expose the area and remove the corrupted bone according to what will be found in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the cauterization of the teeth and the softening of the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines and they don't help, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery—the shape of which will be appropriate for this—in such a way that you place the small cup (or shield) over the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, and hold it a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization a few times as needed.
And then let the patient fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth is fixed, the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt moisture is dried.
Chapter 21.
On the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or if there is cold or worms in it, and you cannot cure it with medicine, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with a hot iron, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with a hot iron is that you take a hot iron and heat it in a fire, and then take cotton and wrap it around the end of the probe, and then dip it in the boiling iron, and apply it to the painful tooth quickly until it is cooled.
And then repeat this several times until the power of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool or cotton in the aforementioned hot iron, and place that on the aching tooth, and place a glowing iron on top of that until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is that you use a small tube of brass or iron, and there should be some thickness in its core so that the intensity of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.
Then ignite the cautery, shaping it as needed, and place it on the tooth, holding your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times because the pain will be relieved either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, upon application of that cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good hot water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the method of cauterization.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the form of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
Concerning the cauterization of extrophuli (likely referring to a type of skin lesion or protrusion).
When extrophuli are caused by phlegm and cold humors and are not responsive to digestion by medicines, and you wish to digest them lightly, then you ignite the concave cautery, whose form is as follows: having two open ends so that the smoke escapes through one end of the cautery, and place that ignited instrument upon that abscess once, or more times if needed, until it reaches the base of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days, and apply cotton soaked in butter over it until that which the fire has burned is healed.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the cauterization of hoarseness and the constriction of the throat.
When humors gather in the pulmonary canal, especially when this is accompanied by coldness of constitution, then it is necessary that the patient be evacuated at the beginning with laxative medicines, and afterward cauterize below the root of the throat in the constricted place.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn the uvula, only a little.
And cauterize that with another cautery below the junction of the neck at its last vertebra. And let its cauterization be done with a nail, following the previous method.
And afterward, treat it with what was previously prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are caused by cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption (tuberculosis) and the illness is chronic, then cauterize with two cauteries on the clavicle, in the previously described places, inside and outside; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest, between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a nail according to the preceding form.
And if you wish, your cauterization may be done by puncturing with the cautery that is called a puncturing instrument, the form of which precedes in the puncturing for earache.
And let the punctures be made within those limits; and then treat it with the same remedies that have been described previously, until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs, for cauterization is done with this: with one application you can make three cauteries according to this form:
Chapter 25.
Of the cauterization of the testicle, which is the head of the seminal duct.
When the head of the testicle is dislocated due to lubricous humors and is not fixed in the moment of its return from its dislocation until it is in the place it is accustomed to return to, and then with a small movement it is dislocated again, as we have testified, then first you must reduce the dislocation; and the patient should be turned onto his back or onto his healthy side; and then lift the testicle which is inside the scrotum toward the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated inwards.
And then heat the cautery which has two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the testicle until it penetrates to the other side; and the figure of the cauterization will then be of four cauterized heads.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count, and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place cooked pork fat with salt on the cautery, and then keep the patient calm, and the member should not be moved for some time until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this [treatment] is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a nail-shaped cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened, the humidity is removed, and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
On the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors enter the stomach, exceeding its capacity and multiplying within it, and if you continue the treatments and do not improve, then the patient should lie supine on his back, extending his arms and legs. Then cauterize the area with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet, to the extent of one finger's breadth, using a nail-shaped cautery; and two more cauteries below on either side of this cautery, so that a triangular figure of cauteries is formed. Space these out so that they are not joined together, which would cause putrefaction.
And the depth of the cautery should be two-thirds the thickness of the finger.
And let the shape of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery in the form of a circle according to this form:  (Note:  This line indicates the presence of accompanying diagrams, which are missing from the text provided.)
And let the stomach be cauterized, paying attention to the fact that this cauterization is frightening: and it is so that you mark on the stomach points according to the amount you wish with a probe, and then cauterize those points with a cautery; and then treat it with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
On the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain arises in the liver due to coldness, humidity, or excessive flatulence, so that it is expelled from its normal fullness by superfluous egress, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it does not help, then the patient should be supine on his back, and mark with a probe three cauteries according to this form and this same quantity on the liver under the hypochondria, where the patient's decubitus is felt. (Again, this implies accompanying diagrams showing the location and shape of the cauterizations.)
And let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger, and let the cautery be according to the length of the right index finger.
And do not compress the area with the cautery too much.
And if the amount of what is burned is the amount of half the thickness of a finger, and no more, and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him lie down, having already extended his legs, and raise his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these areas with a knife-like cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And beware that you do not proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the thickness of a whole finger be burned so that the whole body is burned and it reaches the intestines, because the finger here is thin; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess of the liver with a cautery.
When there is an ulcer or abscess in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its tunic, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient finds gravity and pain without acuteness.
And if it is in the tunic of the liver, the pain is very acute and sometimes it tires the doctors in its treatment.
Therefore, the patient should be turned onto their side; and then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then ignite the cautery which is shaped like a hook, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire abscess is consumed, until it reaches the bottom, until blood escapes.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this form of cautery be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is extensive, and in whose hands many diseases have been known; but let someone with experience sometimes dare this operation, however, leave that to us, and it is better.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that cautery with the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy, according to this manner, is effective; and it is this: that you take of the long, dry roots of birthwort a root one finger-length long, as is found, and let it be of the thickness of a finger.
Then immerse it in oil and heat it in the fire, and then cauterize with one cautery in the place which is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the chin, slightly inclined towards the part which is below the beard, and with two cauteries in the place which is inclined between the fifth rib and the sixth, inclining slightly to the back parts; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another on the stomach, and with three cauteries on the back parts; and with one cautery in the place which is between the two shoulders, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary that you grasp the cautery with your hand, but rather let it be in the evident manner of the work. (Or: ...but rather let it be in plain view during the operation.)
And some ancients said that some men use a cautery of iron similar to a ray and ignite it, and place it in the place which is between the ribs, until it reaches the abscess and is purged of its matter, just as we have said in the abscess of the liver.
But this perforation with cautery is not without danger, for one may easily kill the patient in the place, forming a fistula which is incurable.
Chapter 30.
On the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen with what we have said of the treatment in the division and it does not improve, then cauterize it in three ways, all of which are correct. One is to make three or four cauterizations in a line according to the length of the spleen, following the shape of the liver cauterizations, the shape of which has preceded.
And between each cauterization let there be the width of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as that which we have described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not press your hand with the cautery; and the patient should be lying on their side.
And another way to cauterize is to ignite the cautery having two points, which we have described in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and raise the loin which is in the area of the spleen where the patient's left side lies.
And let your elevation, with which you raise the loin, be according to the length of the body, so that cauterizations are made according to the width of the body.
Then introduce the two points, very hot, into that space so that with them you penetrate the loin from the other side.
And then withdraw the cautery; and thus four cauterizations are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three points.
And then you must take care of the cauterization until it has finished discharging pus for many days, for this is more important than the other preceding actions in the treatment.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the cauterization of a dropsical patient.
Cauterization is not suitable for all dropsies, but only for a certain kind.
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment which we have described in the division and your treatment is not effective, then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries in the area around the navel, and with one cautery on the stomach, and with another cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and with another cautery over the stomach. And the depth of the cautery should be proportional to the thickness of the skin.
And leave the cautery open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not remove the cautery from the patient before it is necessary, for two intentions should be combined, and his healing should be hastened with helpful measures.
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its form is described above; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its shape is described above.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are inflamed with dropsy and are filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the dorsum of the foot, in the space between the little toe and ring finger. Raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; then raise your hand again and do not completely remove it, for the yellowish water will drain out.
The cautery is according to this form: Then make two cauteries on both thighs, and on each thigh a cautery should be made with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other below the middle of the thigh; and on each thigh two cauteries.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that any water may drain from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is cold and damp to the point that it weakens the virtues—that is, the digestive and retentive virtues which are in the stomach and intestines—and this is treated with methods of cure and it does not improve, and sometimes the patient suffers, exhibiting a weakening of the vital forces, then cauterize it with a large cautery over the stomach as previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and with four small cauteries in a circle around the navel, with a thin awl-like cautery; and with a cautery on the hypochondrium, above the spleen, with a large cautery, or with two cauteries.
And if sometimes the humors are excessive and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize it over the pubic bone and over the intestines, and quickly apply two cauteries near the stomach and near the groin, for this is a perfected cure of benefit from which you will not err.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic hemorrhoids.
When there are by nature chronic hemorrhoids, or one of thick cold humors or corrupt humors, and they are treated with what we have said in the previous section and your treatment does not improve, then cauterize with three cauteries on the most inferior part of the sacrum, small and triangular, and with a cautery above the navel, almost two fingers' width.
And if you suspect that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face inflamed, then cauterize that on the stomach with a large cautery as previously described; and with one cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter 35.
Concerning the cauterization of athel [likely a misspelling or archaic term, possibly referring to a type of growth or swelling] near their excision.
When you excise an athel, then you ignite the cautery which is shaped like a ray/sunbeam. Then insert that ignited cautery into that excised athel, and restrain your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood comes.
And do this once or twice, for if the atheyl are numerous, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and then treat those with that which you apply to them of ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on someone, it is slower in healing.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. Of the cauterization of fistulas which are formed naturally in the private parts.
When nature does not obey the work which we have recommended in its place, and the patient is frightened by it, let it be healed with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in the private parts, and it is old, it is corroded by pus and corrupt humors.  Therefore, what is most fitting is to probe it with a slender probe, and then know the quantity of its depth with the aforementioned probe.
Then ignite the cautery that is similar to a ray (or, a pointed cautery). And then place that ignited cautery into this fistula, according to the amount which the probe entered.
And repeat the cautery on it until those corrupt bodies are burned, quickly once or twice or three times, according to your necessity.
And beware of burning the nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes to the body of the bladder or to the body of the bowel, then beware of all those places; and do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy place and you think that it is not penetrating (deeper structures).
And then treat the area until it is healed.
And if the place is sealed, and the matter has been excised from it, and remains so for a long time, then know that it is completely cured.
And if the materials (infected matter) have not been removed from it, then know that it is penetrating, or that the bone beneath is corrupted, or that it comes from those causes, the memory of which will be found in its place (in the following discussion/context).
Chapter 37.
Concerning the cauterization of sores/ulcers.
When in sores pain arises from cold or excessive windiness, and because of this the patient's strength is diminished, then it is necessary that you cauterize that area over the sores with a cautery, over each sore with a nail-shaped cautery, the preparation of which has been previously mentioned.
And quickly cauterize again a little lower, so that three linear cauterizations result, and then they will be more effective in healing.
Chapter 38.
Concerning the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, such that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that area in the lowest part above the bladder, where the pubic hairs begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side, and with another on the left.
And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the walnut; and cauterize that area with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be a nail-shaped tool, as is appropriate.
Chapter 39.
On the cauterization of the womb (or uterus).
When cold and humidity affect the womb, and because of this pregnancy is prevented, and her menstrual flows are corrupted, and their onset is delayed, and pain occurs before their arrival, then the area should be cauterized with three cauteries in a circle around the navel, as we have said in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery above the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
On the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and the sign of this is that one leg is longer than the other when they are compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it protrudes.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient on the part of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the affected area as it is swollen, so that the affected area falls in the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the kind previously described for the cauterization of the stomach.
And if this cautery is not available to you, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be done according to the size of the thickness of the whole spine; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it is not effective, and this is ancient, then it is necessary that you purge the patient of thick humors with fetid pills or with artificial pills or similar; and then cauterize it.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
If according to two known methods, of which one is that you cauterize that area on the patient's hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of the thickness of a finger, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be on the middle point, above the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of the hip dislocation, so that it is a continuous circle and burns the thickness of the whole hip.
And this is the method of cauterization in sciatica when the pain is localized in the hip and does not extend to the thigh.
But if it extends to the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the spot to which the patient points as the location of pain; and with one cautery on the hip, four fingers somewhat below the left side.
And if the cautery is knife-like, let the depth of the cautery be according to the thickness of the hip.
And if the patient indicates that the pain extends to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery in three or four points, or more if needed.
And if the patient indicates that the pain is below the buttock towards the thigh, then cauterize there with a knife-like cautery.
And be careful in all cauterization that you do not use the cautery to the extent that you burn nerves or cause great injury, and that because of this you do not cause the patient harm, such as unconsciousness or convulsion.
And I have seen one and another of those whom I cauterized on the ankle, and I went too far in the cauterization, and the leg swelled until the tumor reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then a strong case of diarrhea occurred, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places at once, cauterize each according to this manner.
And some wise men say in the cauterization of the ankle, use a cautery of which this is the manner: make a circle of iron similar to a ring whose circumference is half a palm; and let the opening be according to the thickness of a date pit, or a little less.
And within that circle let there be another third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a walnut; and let those circles be open on two sides; and let the length of those irons be a walnut or two walnuts, and make in those irons properly prepared margins in the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the part of the ankle, the patient lying on their side. Therefore cauterize that with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave it for three days; then plaster it with a bandage and leave the wound open for several days; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
Says Albucasis: O, son, we have used this type of cautery little because of the fear of looking at it, and because we have found few who dare to look at it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and finds with it the place of it.
But cauterization with burning medicines is that you make two circles having a perforated armature in which to place the handle of the iron door; let the length of the walls in height be two nuts or something like that.
And if the space which is between each is the quantity of the thickness of the finger; and let them be open below and above, and let them contain themselves between them according to this form:
Then place it over the affected part of the hip, the patient lying on their side, and compress your hand over the part of the hip; and then take warm water and pour it between the two circles, and let the quality of it be with little fire gradually, and keep it for the amount of an hour, and let the patient suffer the biting and burning of it — for this produces a burning like fire — until the burning ceases.
Then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the warm water from the whole hip with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and place a bandage over it until the blackness of the burn is removed, and leave it for some days, and pus will flow from it; this is the final healing.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make for him a bath containing water, in the same way as it is done for the hip.
And this is the method of hot water, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, equal parts of each, and crush them and place them in a new pot, the bottom of which is perforated by a small hole, according to the quantity that will enter the ray. (The "ray" here likely refers to a specific instrument or body part; more context would be helpful for a definitive translation.)
And place under the bottom of the pot another glazed pot, and pour onto the alkali and quicklime some fresh water that covers them by a finger's breadth, and then compress them with your hand with good pressure, and let the sharp water descend from above into the glazed pot below.
And then collect all that water, and pour it over more fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be of a very strong acidity, which is used in many medical operations and in the cauterization of other members, because it acts like fire.
And some medicines with which cauterization is also done are:  honey,  vinegar, and lime with soap, mixed together.
And Gualia recounted from an ancient man the operation and cure with which pain in the hip and sciatic vein is cured, and highly praised the effectiveness of it, and said that it had no need of further treatment, and that it healed at once, as recounted, because he quickly entered a bath and was already healed of it.
And it is that fresh rue should be taken, and if fresh is not found, a fresh fig should be taken; and it should be well crushed with a little fat and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and bound on.
And it should be left on for three hours, or for as long as the patient feels the burning sensation.
Then put him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, expose him to the sun, for the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that it is not fitting that anyone use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
But already Dioscorides said that when the varicose vein is cauterized with goat's dung, it benefits him very much; and the cautery should be applied in this manner: wool should be taken and soaked in old oil, and placed between the toes and the ankle.
Then take dried goat dung and burn it until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it there until it is extinguished. Then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of burning reaches the ankle and relieves the pain.
And this method of cautery is called cautery of  goats (or "cow cautery," depending on how "vaquas" is interpreted - it seems to be a scribal error).
Chapter 42.
Concerning the cauterization of toothache.
Sometimes pain is in the teeth due to various causes, such as a fall or a blow, or excessive evacuation, and similar causes, due to the infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cautery is only suitable for that one type which is caused by the effusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after evacuation of the patient with purging pills and similar things,  make incisions on the tooth where the pain is, three lines according to its width, then mark with ink in each line five cauteries or more, according to the patient's tolerance and their strength.
And let the cauterization be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries with a medial nail-shaped cautery according to this form:
Chapter 43.
Of cauterization at the beginning of gibbosity (or hunchback).
Many times this illness happens to small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it occurs in their spine with elevation and movement.
And you find in the last vertebra of the back and neck that it begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And so when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize that with a cautery which is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in the whole part of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the circumference of the vertebra with a pointed cautery two or three lines. And let the points be close to each other.
And then treat the area until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered in a swelling that is caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
Concerning the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is customary for physicians to call this properly gout.
And therefore, if gout caused by cold is cured by a method of treatment and the pains are not removed, then cautery removes them.
And it is that you cauterize that near evacuation in the area of the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains rise into the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize that area on each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize that area, and do not press too hard with your hand and the cautery beforehand; let it only be about the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains rise into both knees, then administer the cautery which we have described in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands and it is called  suagra (likely a form of hand pain or arthritis), then puncture in the circle of the veins, as if revolving, puncture in those two lines.
And if the pain remains in the fingers, then puncture in the circle of the veins on each finger and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains reach the elbows or muscles in a few days, then cauterize them in every part of the affected areas.
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and the reception of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of fractures.
When a fracture occurs in the knees and the intestines protrude before the patella to the genitals, and this begins to happen, then you should order the patient to abstain from food that day and to use lenitive remedies so that the rupture descends from his belly.
Then hold the patient between your hands on his back, and instruct him to hold his breath until the patella or intestine is expelled or appears.
Then return it with your words and signs, gently pushing the rupture over the pubic bone, noting that its extremity should resemble a semicircle at the upper parts of the body.
Then ignite the cautery, whose shape is as follows:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not protrude.
And you have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion underneath; and let another servant be on their legs, and another on their feet, who holds their hands.
Then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and hold it there until you reach with it the bone of the pubis to the middle of the duodenum; and repeat this another time if you did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And observe carefully the position of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn the intestine, lest death or other great harm befall the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, do not complete your operation.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small, according to their size and measure.
Then care for the cauterized area for three days with a bandage until the burn scar is healed.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and wound are sealed/healed.
And it is necessary that you provide the patient with nourishment according to the length of their treatment, which will cleanse their bowels, and that the intestine does not protrude during the healing process.
And then, when he wants to get up after forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be lessened in work and in the fullness of food and drink, and in vehement voice, for when he uses this regimen of healing entirely he will be healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the belly; and if, at the beginning, you want them not to protrude, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that which is exuded from the wound be like the third part of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is not appropriate. and a line is placed across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a nail-like cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion. (or twisting; likely referring to a twisted intestine or similar)
When in some member, because of a fall or a blow, pain arises and persists for a long time and does not yield to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be appropriate to each member according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and its pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cauterization, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is customary for those pains that they are punctuated from one member to the next closest member, so it is necessary that you treat those with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes helped by cautery, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so, if you wish to cauterize someone, consider whether the leprosy is spreading and whether it can be cured with what we have said in the division, and if it does not decrease or remain stable, and you fear that the corruption will spread throughout their body and their entire complexion, then cauterize them on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and the second lower, between the forehead and the hairline; and two on the temples; and one behind, on the nape of the neck.
And finally, with the cautery, press your hand slightly inward so that a small impression is made on the bone, and the crusts are removed, so that the exhalation of thick vapors is relieved; and cauterize over the spleen as needed.
And if the leprosy is already widespread on the patient and manifestly apparent, then you should cauterize them with the cauteries mentioned for the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with a large one on the vertebrae near the buttocks, and one on the buttocks, and with two on the hips, and one on each hip, and with two on the groins and one on each groin, and with two on the knees, and with two on the thighs, and with two on the ankles of their feet; and cauterize them on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of their feet, and on the wrists of their hands.
And be careful of the nerves that are on the heel, so as not to burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and with another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.
And know that the more precisely you apply the cauteries, the more beneficial and helpful it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they would with other wounds, because their body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as is prescribed by the specifications of the instruments.
Then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness/stupor.
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes some numbness is cauterized which extends to the hands and feet, and to the vertebrae of the back, near the origin of the nerves which move that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not attempt this except for someone who is skilled in the anatomy of the limbs and the origin of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of white patches which are morphea (vitiligo).
When the white patches are old and you do not apply any medicinal ointments, then you cauterize it with a cautery iron a little, according to the size and nature of the affected area, until the whiteness and its color are relieved, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of barley and lentils with rose oil and arugula leaves, and with pigeon blood or adder blood, of each an equal part, and all mixed together, and apply a linen cloth over the area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you want to stop it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around its perimeter as it is developed/spread.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery iron in the middle; however, this is not my practice, for I fear that it will be ulcerated, and I have seen this happen several times.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized around its perimeter with a circle, as we have said, or with several cauteries.
Chapter 50.  (Assuming a Roman numeral system;  LI = 51 is also possible depending on the manuscript's numbering conventions.)
Concerning the cauterization of a boil/abscess.
When a boil is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the abundance causing it, or to the age of the patient, because he is old and decrepit, with little blood, or due to the season, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the boil; then cauterize around its perimeter with several small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the shape: and place that in the middle of the boil until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in a lower place, so that the body of the arm is relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
On the cauterization of corrosives.
A corrosive is a corruption that moves in the limb, which corrodes it like fire consuming dry wood.
