Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place that suffers cauterization with fire, then you should ignite very small, hot cautery nails, and large ones as is appropriate for the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it completely until you eradicate all the corrosion and none of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and place crushed sulfur mixed with oil on the cauterized areas until all the scabs are gone and all the corruption is eradicated.
And then treat it with a flesh-generating ointment.
But if after three days you see that the flesh grows healthy where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on whatever remains corrupt in those places.
And corrosion is sometimes treated with a sharp medicine, for that is a substitute for cauterization. But however, cauterization with fire is of more certain benefit.
And I have already described the treatment with sharp medicine in the section on incisions, and therefore use that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
On the cauterization of ulcers and other similar ailments.
Often, a disease called an ulcer develops on the parts below the foot, and it is a deep, penetrating sore that damages the foot; and cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, that is, cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is to heat a concave cautery resembling a vulture's beak, which is made of iron according to the size needed to contain the entire ulcer, and it should have fine edges.
Then place that heated instrument on the ulcer, and gently move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the ulcer until the cautery reaches the depth of the ulcer. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed eschar. Then plaster it with crushed wild mallows mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
Then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it does not heal, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you should apply cautery with water to these areas.  Take a small tube of bronze, iron, or vulture's quill, and place it over the ulcer. Then pour a small quantity of hot water into the tube, and hold your hand, gently moving it with compression around the tube.  The edge of the tube should be pressed into the root of the ulcer, and the hot water will find its way to the root of the ulcer. Let the patient endure the bite of the hot water for about an hour, then remove it, for the ulcer with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with those [ulcers] and then with the others until you have treated all those that are on the body.
Then treat those [sores] after the roots of the ulcers have been eradicated, with ointments that generate flesh.
Chapter 53.
Concerning the cauterization of tremors.
When someone suffers from tremors due to cold in the nerves, or from a cold fever, or from a similar cause, then you should cauterize them with four or five cauteries on the spinal vertebrae, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, using an olive-shaped cautery.
And his tremor will subside, and hasten the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 54.
Concerning the cauterization of accidental ailments of the body.
When in the body there are boils and pustules caused by thick, corrupted matter; then, at the beginning, when they appear, it is necessary that it be cauterized on the head of the fistula with a fine cautery made with myrtle wood, the end of which is heated with fire; or with long roots of erestologia; or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes a cautery is also applied at the beginning of its expulsion in this manner; and they are not believed, and the superfluity is broken up by doing this, and heals from it.
However, it is necessary that this be done near evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56. On the cauterization of a blood flow accident caused by an arrow wound.
Often, there is a flow of blood from an arrow wound which is incised near the wound, which flows outwards, or near the incision of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similarly, and it is difficult to stop this.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you should press with your hand on the opening of the arrow wound, and place your finger on it, tightly pressing it many times until the flow of blood stops under your finger, and it does not come out.
Then place in the fire small and large olive cauteries, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
And then take one of these, small or large depending on the location and the wound where the artery is broken,  and apply it to that vein quickly after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery there until the bleeding is stopped and restrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from among those which are prepared in the fire, and do not cease doing this one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that another ailment befall the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to stop it in those cases, especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or by extraction if it is not extracted, for when it is extracted the extremities are better burned and the bleeding is stopped, or that it be tied with thread or with a tight ligature, or that medicines whose virtue is to stop blood be placed upon it; and these should be tightened with a cushion with a decent constriction.
But that which administers a decent restriction with a ligature and with a tightening with cloths or by the application of cauterizing substances and the like, should not be applied except late.
But if this happens to someone and the physician or medicine is not present, then gently press with the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and apply great pressure to it until the blood is stopped from the principal part of the wound, and the finger should not be removed from the artery; and upon the artery sprinkle very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and consider whether cautery or medicine is needed.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their similars.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire and with burning medicine is suitable, and the occasions and causes of it, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one who seeks this will be aided, then regarding that which he will have asked.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter it is about the terrors that are mentioned in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because it is necessary that there be great caution in it, for in the operation which is in this chapter, many times it causes an evacuation which is with blood, with which life is maintained, through the opening of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of a fistula, or purification of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision of a stone, and similar things. And all of these are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in most of them, death results.
And I warn you that this should not befall you, in which there is hesitation, for sometimes it happens to you by the hands of men with many kinds of illnesses. And of them, there is some who becomes angry with his illness and death is light for him, and this is because of the vehemence of what he finds in his illness, and because of the length of his illness, and with the illness is a fear which signifies death.
And of those, there is one who gives you his possessions and says you are hoping for his health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to him who comes to you, of whom this manner is; and your caution should be greater than covetousness and your rapacity; and do not presume anything about this except for certain knowledge which verifies this to you, and to which a praiseworthy outcome follows.
And use in the cure of all your patients prognostication and indication of him to whom health is restored, for in that there is help for you in the acquisition of fame and memory, of praise and glory.
O, sons, may God inspire you with His understanding and may He not deny you direction and protection, for this is in His hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that what you ask for and what you need may be alleviated for you.
Chapter one.
Of the treatment of the water which is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often happens to children at birth, and when the nurse compresses the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it happens for a hidden cause which is unknown.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and all those whom I saw died from it, and thus it is, to my knowledge, a deadly affliction for them.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could no longer support himself because of the size of his head, and moreover the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity is either collected between the scalp and the bone, or it is collected under the bone on the brain.
Because if the operation is such that the humidity is between the scalp and the bone and the abscess is small, then you must make an incision in the middle of the head with a fissure in width, and the length of the fissure should be about two finger-widths so that the humidity can drain, and this is the form of the spatula:
And so, if the humidity is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there should be two incisions in this manner:
And if the humidity is under the bone, the sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress that area with your hand on the inside parts and this is not hidden from you. You must make an incision in the middle of the head with three incisions in this manner:
And then extract all the humidity from the incision; and then tighten the incisions with cloths and lint; and then bathe it above with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then untie the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little humidity until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore aim with the incision where the abscess and the accumulation of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and that a flow of blood ensues, and the patient dies from that flow of blood with evacuation of humidity.
Chapter Second.  (or Chapter Two)
When someone experiences cataracts or rheums in the eyes or feet, and this is chronic, and the treatment with medicines does not help, then the last resort is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise them, have the patient raise their head, and rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and then constrict the patient's neck with a cloth; and then observe where the vein pulsates; and the location of each, above the areas near the ears, is depressed, and is hardly visible except in some people.
Then mark the artery with a probe, and then incise each one with a sharp lancet on each side, making an incision reaching the bone, and this should be across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and sever it at the incisions above.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers, because when a vein is incised, the blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which spurts out frequently.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you must measure from the ear a space of three fingers, and then mark it with a probe, and go in as far as the bone.
And the amount of blood to be shed is about six ounces, and you should quickly let out more or less, and all this depends on the strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
And then examine the wound, and if any part of the splinter remains on the bone, then incise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And then bind the wound tightly with feathers and linen bandages, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrid humors and a quality in the tissues of the temples, or a vehement and similar old affliction, and is treated with the methods of medicinal cure and this does not help, then we must attempt in these illnesses the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or their cauterization as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hairs which are on the temples; and he should look for the artery appearing in the temple because it is shown to you by its pulse, and is hidden only in few men by the intensity of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the neck of the patient should be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the place with a rough cloth or evaporate the place with hot water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you scrape the skin with that [spatula] easily until you reach the artery; and then place an ointment there and extract it to the superior parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are below it, from every part.
But if the artery is subtle, then wrap it with the end of the oncia [a type of surgical instrument], and then cut a part of it according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and there will not be a flow of blood, for if they are not incised, the blood will not rise at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with cords, and do not worry about putrefaction before the consolidation of the wound and a flow of blood will result.
And then incise the superfluity which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or approximately another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone with a knife-cautery until its ends are incised, and it will be in place of that same work.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or is of a warm complexion; for cautery is one of those things that helps to dry up humidities, and therefore it is better in its effect.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you fill the place with old linen, and that powders are placed on it decently made, and then it should be cured with dry medicines causing the flesh to grow and with bandages until it is healed.
And if while you are operating another time there is a flow of blood from the artery, then you must take care to restrain that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the place with linen, and squeezing your hand until the blood is restrained. And if none of those things is at your disposal, then place your finger on the place until the blood is congealed, and bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and it is constricted as necessary.
And what is lighter in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and place each one, very hot, until they reach the bone and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between its extremities is elongated, because they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be equal. However, if it is less sharp than two knives, it is better, for if it is as sharp as two knives close to each other, and does not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since in those [cauteries] there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is contained within them and the flesh is cut lightly.
And this method is better in every way and gentler.
And let the space which is between the two blades be the amount of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter four.
Concerning the treatment of sharp and acrid watering of the eyes.
When there are acrid tears, and it is the flow from those veins which are on the forehead, and you find no cause for this in medicinal treatment, and sometimes the face of the patient becomes red and feels a crawling sensation on the forehead as if it were the crawling of ants, and the eyes are swollen, moist, so that the hairs around them are matted, and the eyelids are inflamed by the sharpness of the tears, the ancients had a method for treating this, and they called that treatment "spassa".
And it is that you command the patient to shave the hairs which are on his forehead, and then incise three slits in the forehead, equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure is two fingers: one fissure should be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little beyond the movement of the muscles which are in the lacrimal duct, and the third on the other side.
And beware of the incision of the arteries which are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jaws. And if the space of each incision is the quantity of three fingers [wide].
And if you have a prepared piece of sponge or dried cloths at hand, with which you will dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that [tool/instrument] the whole ridge which is between the two incisions, with the [surgical] knife which is above the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a curette, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that [instrument] against the flesh attached to the ridge, and its blunt edge against the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to that appearance of the ridge, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient quantity of blood has flowed, squeeze the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old linen, and put on those powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day untie the bandage and administer a great infusion of warm or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the treatment of tears due to cataracts and those descending into the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone suffers from acute and persistent cataracts, and sometimes the patient's eyes are thin and small, and their vision is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their skin falls off, and there is sharp, persistent pain in the front of the head and consequent sneezing, then know from these symptoms that the materials and cataracts originate from many veins proceeding from the inner parts.
And the best cure in such cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then incise a fissure in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be to the bone; and lengthen your cut from the temples which are bruised due to mastication), until the bone is uncovered.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two lips of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then bind it above with powders soaked in wine and oil so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind it and see that the abscess has diminished, then it is necessary to scrape the bone until new flesh begins to grow there, and then treat it with a drying regimen that causes the flesh to grow, as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and from them make a plaster and apply it to these wounds to promote the growth of new flesh.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of what falls into the ears.
Every cause that falls into the ears is one of four types, for it is either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone, such as iron or glass; or it is from a plant, such as a kernel or a bean; or it is a flowing substance such as water and sour wine and similar things; or some animal.
And therefore, when a stone or other similar substance falls into the ear, which does not soften or grow in the ear, and you place the ear in the sun, and if you see the stone, then instill some violet oil or sesame oil into it, and then proceed with its extraction, and make them sneeze with a potion, and plug the nostrils before the onset of sneezing. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and gently pull the ear upwards, for many times it is expelled by these means.
And if it is not expelled, then proceed with its extraction using the instrument called a slender epicyclic probe, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the probe, it is good; and if not, then proceed with its extraction using a thin hook with little curvature.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by this, then make a brass cannula and insert the end of the cannula into the opening of the ear and seal around the cannula with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open except the cannula. And then extract it with your breath with strong suction, for many times it happens with what we have recounted.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small quantity of ignited or prepared birdlime, which is used to catch birds, and place it on the end of a thin stick, and then carefully wrap it around that properly positioned cotton. And then insert that into the opening of the ear easily, and then dry the ear of its moisture.
And so, if it is not expelled by all the means which we have recounted, then you should aim to remove it before it becomes a hot abscess or spasm.
And the manner of removing it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to their strength, and then make this incision between your fingers and turn the ear to the upper parts, and make a small incision at the root of the ear below its cartilage in a well-prepared location. And let the incision be of a crescent shape so that it reaches the stone, and, the stone found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then immediately close the incision with speed, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause occurring in the ear is a growth of fat that is swollen, then administer its cure and extraction; but if it does not yield to such treatment, then take finely powdered litharge according to this method: and administer with that the removal of that growth which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and place it on thick cloths, and carefully extract it with forceps or with a fine instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thereby eased.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing as a first measure, and fill his ear with some old cotton, and lie down on the ear into which the water has entered; and if it is expelled by this, it is well; and if not, then take some heated smooth stones and lentils, about the length of a finger, and warm them a little in the fire, and let the patient insert one of these into the opening of his ear and jump on one foot on that side and strike it with another stone against the stone which is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water is not expelled in this way, take some paper or tow, and place one end of it in the ear and hold the other end to the fire until the majority of it is burnt.
And quickly place more tow there and do this several times until all the water is expelled, and extract it with a cannula as has been said above of the extracting stone.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small, like a fly or similar, cure it as we have said in the division; but if it is of a large size, which is evident to the senses, then administer its extraction with tweezers and hooks, and the cause of this is the lightest of all the other causes that fall into the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you cure it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your cure is not effective, then you should expose the ear to the sun.
And so if any cause of the worms appears there, then extract it with tweezers or with fine hooks; but if no cause appears, then take a cannula narrower below and wider above, of which this is the form: And insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it strongly, and do this many times, until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you should fill and seal the opening of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the cure of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and flat above.
And let it be such that it pushes what is in the concavity of the cannula, contained where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a rod and wrap its end decently with cotton, and then pour oil into the concavity of the ear, or juice of some herb, or whatever you want of these medicines into the cannula—and that is in the ear—and then insert the rod with cotton on top and press with your hand, with moderate compression, until the oil is expelled into the concavity of the ear and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is poured into the ear should already be slightly warmed by fire. And be careful that what you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the treatment of ear obstructions.
Sometimes children are born without perforated ears, and this also happens in some adults; an obstruction in the ear canal due to injury or by flesh growing there.  And this obstruction sometimes occurs in the opening of the ear canal, which prevents drainage, and sometimes in the outer parts.
And that which happens in its opening, which prevents drainage, is in the greatest disposition towards difficult healing.
And in that which allows drainage, you should expose the patient's ear to the sun and observe it. And then if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a fine spatula, of which this is the form:
If its end is narrow, with one side sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be two finger-widths wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or its canal, then remove it with fine hooks and incise it vigorously until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the opening of the ear, then take a thin, fine reed and heat it slightly with fire, and then insert it into the ear canal.
And then, if you have felt an obstruction with which the patient experiences difficulty, due to the severity of hearing loss, alleviate the pressure on it gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a linen cloth, according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for a few days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take some wool, dip it in warm water, and sprinkle it with finely crushed powder and use it; and if you see that the ear is already abscessed, then you must remove the wool and replace it with other wool which you will wrap in a plaster made with rose oil, until the abscess is completely reduced; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear, and use the other treatment which constricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which develops on the eyelids.
Styes which develop on the eyelids are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with your fingers, and incise them with a spatula from their roots, and then apply a poultice to the affected areas.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a thin, lentil-shaped cautery iron.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains in their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Many times there appears on the eyelids of the eyes some growth which resembles a swelling in its firmness and hardness, and because of its swelling it is called a swelling.
And it is an accumulation of thick humidity in the eyelid below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you consider then if the swelling is coming forth in such a way that it appears from the eyelid and is spread throughout; and the cause of it is slight.
Then make an incision on it lengthwise, and then peel it off entirely easily until it comes off, and then lift it up with tweezers and cut it off.
And if the growth is large, then you adjust it with sutures; and cure it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will suffer no harm from it because the ointment heals it and it will fill in.
And if the growth is leaning towards the inner parts of the eyelid beneath the inner surface, then lift the eyelid and lift the growth with tweezers,  even if you need to make an incision, and press it from all sides, and if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, this harms nothing.
Then, after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with something that causes it to fill in until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a growth on the upper eyelid.
A growth is a swelling which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and mostly in children, it irritates their eyes and causes them to have a continual discharge; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because of it, for tears gather there.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and the lower eyelids and eyebrows are moist, showing swelling and the appearance of a growth is evident in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel a growth there.
And one method of operation is this: the patient places his head in your lap, and you place a cloth between you and him to act as a support.  Make a circle of the appropriate size to encompass the entire growth, then place it over the growth and press it with your fingers all around so that the growth is concentrated in the center of the circle. Then make an incision into the center of this swelling with a pointed instrument, the incision being no larger than that made in bloodletting.
But in the operation it is necessary that you cut through completely until you reach the growth, because in most cases the growth comes out through the incision in the form of a white greasy substance.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers at the edges and roll it between your right and left hand until it is completely removed.
And be careful not to push too deeply into the incision so that with the instrument you do not reach the eye and injure it.
But if the growth does not appear at the beginning of the incision, gently probe a little further into the incision until the growth emerges; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in wine and vinegar, and place them over the area and bind them with powder.
And some crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision so that it dries any remaining moisture.
And then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the site, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
On the ways of touching the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes there are growths beneath the natural skin and are inflamed, then these harm the eye and cause various illnesses, such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and membrane, until this causes destruction of the eye, which is called a tactus of the eye, and it is cured in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with an acute medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery; or the tactus is made by incision and suture; or by cannulas as I shall describe.
And therefore it is necessary that you place the patient's head in your lap; then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, it is good; and if not, insert a needle into that part where the pus is beneath the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate upwards, and this should be – near the margin of the eyelid where the hairs grow – near the hairs, and draw the pus to the upper parts with the eyelid, and turn it with a ray.  (The "ray" here likely refers to a surgical instrument or a tool to manipulate the eyelid)
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid below the inflamed growths with a small, sharp instrument from the major lacrimal duct to the minor lacrimal duct; and then extract the pus and place small pieces of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then bind over the eyelid with a bandage, and let it be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape [of the bandage] should be according to the amount of swelling of the eyelid, for this varies in people, for some it is necessary to incise a large amount of the eyelid according to how much the eyelid is softened.
Then make an incision with the small instrument along the two lines that you have marked, beginning from the major lacrimal duct to the minor lacrimal duct. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, [let it be] as thick as a king's [hair/thread]. (This likely refers to a very fine incision)
Then place the hook in one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape it all, and then join the two lips of the wound with glue or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and stick what overflows from the threads on the two eyebrows with some sticky substance, if you wish to do so, and if not, don't worry.
Then leave the suture of the threads for three or four days, and then take care of it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without suture, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its aggregation; but suture is better.  Therefore, the ancients advised this [suturing].
But however, let there be diligence on the part of the physician, and it is a good operation, and let there be no fear in him.
And there is another way to treat [this], and it is to make a mark on the eyelid in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three hooks separated and joined according to this form:
But if this is not possible, to hold the hooks, and this is not appropriate, then take a needle on which there is thread and place that in the middle of the figure, and introduce the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust between your fingers the threads with equality, and then with those lift your hand with even elevation, raising the entire area over which it is marked as we have recounted.
And then join the two lips of the wound with a suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or your fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted, then it is necessary that you use on that what we have said of incision; but suture in each case is more skillful/artificial.
To bandage with canes is according to this method/form: and it is that you turn back the eyelid and cut the growth that is inside as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two thin canes or splinters whose length is according to the length of the eyelid and whose width is less than the width of the space/gap. And you have already incised at the ends of each part, where you have the threads/sutures.
Then carefully add each one with subtlety over what is superfluous of the eyelid of the eye, and bind the canes to each part with a strong binding, and leave it for some days, for the growth because of the binding will die and dry up and rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed/prolonged, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Then, when it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick/protrude.
And this is the form of two canes which you will make according to this form/model: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Chapter 12. Of the elevation of prickly hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and prickly hairs in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle, and place it on that fine silk thread and lion [possibly a type of linen or fine fabric;  the term requires further context for definitive translation], and then you adjust its ends, and tie each [end] in a very subtle manner. And let the length of the threads be the quantity of a palm [hand-span].
And then you prepare two other fine threads underneath it briefly, and tie the ends of those if you wish.
And then have the patient place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is illuminated, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the hair, adjusting it as needed, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it from above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair upwards and insert it into the alixati (likely a solution or ointment), and let there be one, two, or three hairs, but no more.
And then pull your hand with that until the hair is pulled out with the alixolati (likely a solution or ointment) in the eyelid, along with the natural hair.
But if the alixalati is pulled and the hair is not removed, you will draw the alixalati towards the lower parts with the bile that you have mixed with it, until the alixalati is removed from the upper wound.
And examine it with the vision of the eye, for then you will remove the alixalati and the bile, and clean and close each one and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is raised with that, or you leave it until it is lengthened to a certain space, and then you raise it.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which appears on the upper eyelid.
When a stye appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound or incision or cautery, and it is called lagophthalmos (leporina), and this ectropion or is natural or accidental, and is caused by the healing of a wound or incision or cautery, or by something similar to their bad healing.
The method of operation is that you incise that consolidation and separate its lips, and place between them a small piece of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not fitting that you use treatments which dry or constrict; for if you do this, the stye will return worse than it was before.
Use on it things that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice with the water in which mallow and linseed have been boiled, or diachylon ointment, which should be liquefied with some oil, and soak the linen cloth in it, and treat it with that.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you ensure, by every means, that it is not consolidated in a way different from its original form.
And this operation which we have described is nothing but a rectification of the stye so that it does not return to its previous form.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which is formed on the lower eyelid.
This stye, which forms on the lower eyelid—which is truly called a stye—is of two types: one natural, the other accidental.
But the accidental type is caused by a wound, or a cut, or cauterization, or similar things.
And the method of operating is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left lacrimal duct to the right lacrimal duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and sew it with the needle, and incise it with a wide spatula. And so if the eyelid's shape returns to normal, good; but if not, take a splint and place it in the slit, and with it reshape the eyelid, and stitch the two fissures on the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two fissures be two angles of the incision, which you have made so that they meet, and let an angle be formed between them so that when they come together, their shape is formed.
And this shape is the Greek letter: lambda.
Then remove the flesh on that side to the extent that the lower eyelid is affected, so that the eyelid may be restored.
Then join the separated parts and sew them with wool thread; and then treat them with what we have said of emollient medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if it is excoriated due to a fissure, suture, or cautery, then you should make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid, as previously described. Then separate [the wound] with a poultice between two lips, as we have said.
In summary, regarding the treatment of excoriation whether above or below, the operation should proceed as prepared in the manner of excoriation, which exists in many varied forms.
And the skillful surgeon always prepares himself in every possible way to restore the shape to its natural form, or close to it, and the patient according to what is appropriate for the operation and the instruments, in the best conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation happens to many people such that the upper eyelid is adhered to the white of the eye, and its natural movement and function are impeded, then you must insert the tip of a probe beneath the eyelid and lift it up, or extend it with an unguent.
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharply pointed, but rather blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and move the spatula so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision be such that it separates that adhesion easily, until the eyelid is returned to its natural shape and the entire adhesion is healed.
And then you put salt water in the eye with which you wash it; or distill into it some vitriol dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small splint, and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye, and then on the third day use consolidative remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types, for it is either nervous, and is similar to hard, thin horn; or it is non-nervous, similar to a congealed white humidity, which when iron touches it, or is forced to be taken with an instrument, is severed, and the instrument is not embedded in it.
And both types begin only from the larger tear duct, until it grows little by little, covering the sight and obstructing the light and movement of the eye.
But the manner of operating in its incision is that the patient should place his head on your lap, and then open his eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with a small, double-ended instrument and extend it to the upper parts. And then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or ox hair, or cow hair, or something similar, or strong thread, or double the end of the needle little by little and insert it into the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it to the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the edge of the nail which covers the pupil. And thus you follow it with the hair into the back of it.
And then you incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that through the incision of it there is no continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail, with only the forceps extended or with thread, as we have said, and then it should be scraped with a lion spatula, of which this is its form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then, after the incision, put a little crushed salt or finely powdered cinquefoil into the eye, and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with whatever is appropriate until it is healed.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is the forceps applied to it, then very often this type of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed as I have recounted in the division.
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the patient's eye and take a thin lion spatula of sharpened iron according to this form: with subtlety.  (or: delicately; with precision) And if you see that it is rarefied and is congested and rooted there, then distill from your still into the eye cinquefoil juice or rosewater or crushed salt, and occlude the eye until the next day.
And then repeat the operation on it until all that is there is removed, unless, on the contrary, a hot abscess forms in the eye, for then you leave that and cure the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it with reason until it is healed.
The eminence of the lachrymal flesh, if it is noticeable in the eye causing great harm, should be suspended with a hook; and incise it in part, and do not extend the incision so that you do not cut the flow of tears.
And then instill rosewater and cinquefoil juice into the lachrymal duct until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the incision of a growth and what is born of fleshy matter in the eye.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is born and grows until it covers the pupil, or approaches and presses on the eyelids, and strongly compresses the eyelids at the outer edges, resembling a balustrade rose.
But the operation is that the patient places his head in your lap; and then open the patient's eyes and remove all that flesh with suitable instruments for this, and hold it with forceps or tweezers; and then incise the flesh from the beginning until all is finished with the scalpel, and be careful of the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And let your incision with which you incise those things be with a spatula, which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which the hair is collected, according to how its form will come for this chapter.
Therefore, when your incision is completed and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt, or instill into it red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place a bandage on the outside of the eye with egg white until it is secured from a hot abscess.
And if some cause of inflammation remains and hot blood is observed, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and cure that abscess with whatever reduces that abscess, and then repeat the operation on it until it is healed, and similarly do with the fleshy excrescence which occurs in the eye of this kind.
But however, you should know that you should avoid all treatment of the eye, whether by natural or accidental means, operating with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that that member is able to withstand that operation.
Chapter XVIII.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is caused by red veins which are hardened over the eye and prevent the sight, and they weaken the eye over many days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and in it there is no other illness than pus; then you should collect that pus.
And it is that you command the patient to place his head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with an uncia or two, according to your judgment; and let that uncia be of subtle doubling according to this form:
or two unciae gradually in one piece according to this form:
And then let those veins be collected with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins be removed from the eye and be retracted.
And be careful that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is at midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation that you do not incise except those veins.
And when you have finished, then you distill into the eye verdigris or rue so that with its sharpness it corrodes what has been removed from the eyebrow.
But if this is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster everything with something that soothes his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is soothed and he is assured of a hot abscess.
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Concerning the reduction of a nasal fracture.
When you treat a fracture with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as previously described, and it is not healed, then there is no pus in it but fissures on the abscess before its maturation, and you must extract all the moisture which is in it and the pus until the bone is exposed.
And thus, when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a rasp, and it should be made of Indian iron, and its head should be round, like a button, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle sharpening (as it is in the following form).
And place that [rasp] on the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the place with drying and astringent substances.
Then if the place is consolidated and good flesh has grown there and the body of the sore is separated and remains for 40 days, and it is not renewed and is not festering and no accident happens to it, then you will know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is no other way than to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and according to this manner: that is, you uncover the second bone with an iron or with a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a rasp according to this form:
Let the extremity of the iron be triangular, and let its shaft be slender, tapering to the extremity, like this. (The final phrase implies a diagram would accompany this instruction)
And then place that upon the bone, and let that be near the lacrimal bone; and move your hand a little from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the rasp until it penetrates all the bone, and the patient will feel it because they will feel the air escaping when you move your hand.
Then restore the place with styptic and drying medicines, as you have been taught.
Then, when the flesh has grown and the place has hardened, the matter which flowed to the outside parts returns to the nose, therefore this is easier for the patient.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the reduction of the protrusion of the eye.
When the eye protrudes completely and is not injured by a wound or diminution, then it is necessary to relax the patient, and then to observe him, and then to apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification and suction for good evacuation; and then apply to the eye an ointment which is made of acathus, aloe, incense, and sarcocolla, and then firmly apply the plaster to the eye with many powders.
And the manner of the powders is that you take many lion cloths, and each cloth should be folded into four layers; and make many of these.
And then place those one on top of the other according to the amount that the eye needs. And then bind tightly over these with a strong cloth with firm pressure; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye tightly bound day and night.
But if meanwhile the ligament is softened, then it is necessary that it be very tight.
And then remove the bandage day and night, as we have said.
And if you see the eye already reduced, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the binding and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the incision of the prominence of the uvea tunic.  (or: Of the incision of the swelling of the uveal coat)
When there is a rupture in the uvea tunic, and it is prominent and protrudes beyond the eyelids like a grape, and the man's form is disfigured by this, and you want to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted: that you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from below to above, and then place another needle with double thread from the lacrimal side, and make that penetrate and leave the first needle according to its position; and then incise the place of the double thread and lift with that the part of the uvea in the circle of the needle, and tighten it strongly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and allow the thread to adhere to the uvea.
And then treat the eye with something that strengthens it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes collect from all sides with a circle of cloth the greatly swollen prominence within the eye, and then it should be pierced with a fine spatula that reaches the pupil of the eye, and drains the albuminous moisture, and the eye descends and enters immediately.
And then tighten it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of alconati (likely a type of eye condition or injury).
This illness, which is called alconati, is nothing but a humor which is located in the eye similar to descending water, but it is not that.
But the manner of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight seat, and then take his head in your hands on both sides and gently move it until you see the humor come to the lower parts, and then it should be dried and not descend, and light should be avoided, and the patient should see things as he used to see.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the humor is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a thin spatula and use it on the panniculus, wiping a little below the juncture of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the iris (towards the empty space), until the humor is expelled.
Then, when it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have previously boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then let it be treated with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of the water descending from the eye.
We have already described the types of water in the division and which ones should be punctured, explaining this according to their nature.  Then, that which follows [is for] healing.
Then it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands on a square [cushion] and bind his/her healthy eye, tying it firmly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of his/her eye, if the eye in which the water is located is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is the left one; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then place the end of the almond near the cornea, along the width of a ray, in the white of the eye on the side of the smaller lachrymal duct, and then push the almond gently and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the white of the eye and feel that the almond has already reached the empty space/cavity.
And it should be that the distance of the almond to the pupil is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the edge of the blackness, and that is the cornea of the eye; for you will see with that pupil with the vision of the eye through the clarity of the corneal tunic.
And then place the almond into the place where the water is, and then you draw it down one time then another, and then if the water descends immediately, then the patient sees that upon which his vision fell immediately, and the almond in his eye.
And then rest a little; then if the water has descended, then you remove it another time before the almond is expelled.
And then when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and gently rub it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the water and with that wash the eye inside; and then place outside the eye cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white and bind the eye with this.
But we and those of our time place over it, as crushed, with egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you upon entering the eye because of its hardness — for there are some men whose eye is very hard — then it is necessary that you take the instrument which is called alberid, of which this is the form:
And then perforate that joint with that [instrument], and do not insert your finger into the hole, but only make a subtle pathway for the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And for the completion of your work, let a darkened, comfortable sleeping place be prepared for the patient, where he may sleep on his back, and all movement should be forbidden.
And position him in such a way that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And let the healing occur according to its disposition within three days.
And then, release him from that dark room and expose him to gentle light, show him some things, and then return him to the treatment by the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that you do this at the hour of treatments or immediately after the infusion. Having said this, you must avoid this because the water can easily, by a great and vehement gaze,  [cause harm/be affected].  (The last part is difficult to translate precisely without more context.  The meaning hinges on what "reguardament" refers to here - likely some kind of intense observation or focus.)
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you must open the wound before the seventh day and treat it with something that reduces the abscess.
Then you blindfold him and place over his face a cloth through which his vision is regulated, leaving him for some days in the darkest room.
And then let him be moved from that room gradually and converse about his affairs.
And know that in this case the student is not excused from seeing some things, and then you should be bold in the operation.
And it has come to us from some of those who are in Artach, and some say that a fire was made in the Ayrac magdaam, a perforated place through which water was sucked.  (Note: "Artach" and "Ayrac magdaam" are likely place names or specialized equipment/locations, and their exact meaning is unclear without further context.)
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the types of magdaam, so that he who is ignorant of them may know them:
And they should be made of bronze or iron specifically, and their ends should be made with this triangular subtlety, the sharp end like this, which is the form.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of rotten flesh in the nose.
When in the nose various fleshy growths are born, of which some are similar to a very small scorpion, or to a polyp fish; and this illness is called polyp, for it resembles that fish in appearance. And from it is cancerous flesh, spreading, of a dark color; and from it is flesh that is not dark-colored, a lion's flesh.
Therefore, in that which is in these fleshy growths that is not cancerous or malignant, you should make the patient sit between your hands and place him in the sun, open his nostrils, and prick the growths with your nail. Then extract it outwards, and then incise what you have grasped of it with a thin, sharp spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; for if any part remains from which excision is possible, then you scrape it with one of the fine instruments easily until nothing remains. But if it bleeds profusely or is a hot, festering abscess, then apply what is necessary; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you incise and cauterize it until the bleeding is stopped and all the cavities are removed.
Then, near the incision, let there be poured into the nose, sharp wine and water or wine. But if the nose is open and a moisture flows from it into the palate, then know that it is healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is flesh corrupted in the deep bones of the nose to which excision has not reached.
Then it is necessary to introduce a linen thread, to which some thickness is added, and tie many knots in it, placing between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and then instruct the patient and introduce one end of the thread into the nose with a rod or any other instrument that is possible for him.
And after that is done, let him draw in its scent until it reaches the nasal passages, and it comes out through the throat, because often children in schools do something similar to this action, and it is an easy thing for anyone who wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end coming out of the nose and the other remaining in the nose, and then administer a mixture of ground meat with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then remove the thread, and place a poultice near the extraction of blood in the nose, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that is rotten in the flesh.
Finally, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for a few days until it is healed.
But if you need a more drying cure, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument, a head-purger, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or bronze, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small shell, and the opening similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the head-purging instrument should be flat and open; to which there should be a handle at the back, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or water that you want from the juices and the extractable substances.
Chapter 25.
Of the boils that grow in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a boil grows in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in a few days, to the point that it is easily observed.
And for this it is necessary that you incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate it entirely. And then place on the location either a cautery or a burning medicine which will stay in place.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened, firm, of a dark color, and with little feeling; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess; for many times he who incises this abscess suffers great harm from it,  the one who had it.
And if the abscess is of a light touch and not of dark color, and sometimes incision is possible throughout, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and treat the place with something that dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Chapter 26. Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when their continuity is broken by a wound or similar injury.
Know that when an accidental discontinuity occurs in one of these cartilages, you should put little confidence in that operation except in certain men.
Therefore, when such a thing happens to someone, consider if it is a fresh wound with its blood, and then you should align the two lips of the wound with sutures, and then treat it until it is healed.
And if the lips of the discontinuity are already separated, and each is healed, then you must scrape away each part of its apparent surface until blood appears. Then align the two lips with sutures and tighten them, and over them sprinkle dragon's blood and crushed herbs, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave it bound for two or three days, and then unbind it and change the plaster and leave it until the stitches are naturally dissolved; then treat with ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of suturing is that you close the discontinuity either with needles, as we have recounted in the suturing of the abdomen, or close the discontinuity with thread, as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things that come between the two lips in the inner parts.
Often there appear in the inner parts of the lips of many men certain hard abscesses resembling kernels, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you separate the lips and feel each of those hardnesses on all sides, and then, after the excision, fill the place with crushed sage until the bleeding is stopped, and then let it be washed with sharp wine and the place be treated with something that is styptic, until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh attached to the gums.
Very often a growth appears on the gum, flesh attached which the ancients called an albulus; then it is necessary that you suspend it with a hook or hold it with forceps and incise it to its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
And then place on the area crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for after cauterization it will not return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the treatment of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both inner and outer parts, and between the gums, rough, loose, and sometimes blackened crusts are attached, and they become citrine or green, causing corruption to the gum and making the teeth loose.
And then you should make the patient sit between your hands, and rest his head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those crusts and similar things appear, like sand, until nothing remains of them, removing the blackness, the yellowness, and the others until they are cleaned.
Then, if this (cleaning) has removed what was mentioned at the beginning of the treatment, it is good; but if not, then you repeat the treatment on them on the next day, and the second, and the third, until you reach the desired final result.
And know that teeth require many scrapers of diverse shapes and forms, according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the scraper with which the tooth is scraped on the inner part is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms; and these forms of scrapers should be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
That you cure the tooth of its pain with all skill and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that supplants its function when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance, wherefore its extraction is excusable.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is aching, for many times the pain deceives the patient and he thinks it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and aching tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operations of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which tooth is aching, and you know it well, then you should scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is loosened from the gum on all sides, and then remove it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, beginning little by little until it is completely loose.
And then apply strong, large forceps for good traction or firm grip, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold him well and he does not move, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not dislodged, then take one of those instruments, and place it under it on all sides with ease, and force its removal in the same way as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you should fill that crack with a cloth and bind it with the end of a thin rod so that it is not broken at the time of your tightening around it with forceps.
And you must open carefully with scarification around all parts of the gum and watch your strength so that you do not break it and a part of it remains, and return to the patient because the pain may be greater than the first pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and boldness concerning the extraction of teeth, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great diseases in men which are more difficult to cure, and their roots remain, all or some of them, or that they extract it with some bones of the jaw, as we have often witnessed.
And then, after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with hot wine or sour wine with salt.
And if bleeding occurs from this, as often happens, then crush some quantity of alum and sprinkle it on the wound, and if this alum does not help, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and fine tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be of long extremity, short and thick handle, so that they do not bend when you work with them.
And let them be like tongs with a thick handle so that when you squeeze with them they are not bent; let the ends be short.
And let them be made of Indian iron, precisely made with straight ends, and at the ends of these let there be teeth, of which one enters into the other and tightens by strong constriction, and sometimes the ends of these should be in the shape of the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* (that is, a stork), and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Of the eradication of teeth and the extraction of broken jaw bones.
When you must eradicate a tooth which is broken and some cause remains, then you must apply a poultice to the area for a day or two until the area is softened; and then introduce pincers or forceps, the ends of which are similar to the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork, the shape of forceps:
Let their ends be made like a file or rasp.
And if it does not yield to you thus with these forceps or pincers, then you must dig around the root and scrape away all the flesh and introduce the instrument which is called an *atali*, that is, a small lever, of which the form is this:
And let it be of short, somewhat thick extremity, and let it not be bent so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if by this the root is removed, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is a triangular end in which there is some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it should be triangular and of a more slender end.
And then you are aided by this instrument having two branches, of which this is the form, approximately the following: and with other instruments which precede in tooth scraping; for the work is easily done with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, and this is the form:
of the triangular, curved or short end, which should have some thickness, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
And also know that the instruments for loose teeth are similar to the other instruments.
But the wise and subtle artisan in the art finds for himself, according to what the operation and the diverse diseases signify to him; for there are diseases for which the ancients did not name or provide a proper instrument, because of the forms of diverse types.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken, or if it is possible, then it should be treated in its place with what is convenient for it from among the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of the roots of the teeth, and help yourself with a splint of which this is the form:
And let there be some thickness in it little by little, and let it be tightened or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone is completely set; and then restore the place with medicines suitable for this.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth are prominent, not according to their natural course, their shape is flawed, and especially when this happens to women, and especially to venerable old women; then you must consider: if a tooth is next to another tooth, and closure or alignment is not possible, then extract it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you incise it with an instrument of which this is the shape; and it is similar to a boar's tusk, and it should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp point.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be made in several days because of the hardness of the tooth, so that no other tooth is injured.
And if it is a prominent tooth suitable for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of which this is the shape:
And let the whole thing be of Indian iron and very carefully ground; and let it have very fine points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some scrapers.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and injures the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and it is filled so that the tongue is not injured, nor the speech impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and loosening due to a blow or a fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated it with styptic medicines, and your treatment has been ineffective, then, so that they do not fall out, contrive that they are fastened with gold wire and not silver, because silver turns green and quickly powders after a few days, but gold is never changed.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness according to the quantity that can fit between the teeth; and the manner of the binding is that you take the wire and pass the double of it between two healthy teeth, and then let it grow with two turns of the wire between two damaged ones, or if one or several, until the thread reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving to the point where you began, and tighten with your hand gently and skillfully until it is firm.
And let your tightening, with which you fasten the wire, be such that it does not slip from the roots of the teeth.
Then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and smooth those ends in each place with a file, and fold them over and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightened until it heals.
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and the arrangement of two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, as you see them tied together:
And when it is done that one or two teeth are returned to their proper places, and they are grown back as we have recommended, and that they remain fixed.
And this is only done by good and skilled craftsmen.
And sometimes some cow bone is adapted, and it is shaped like a tooth, and it is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is secured as we have said, and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the incision of the ligament which is in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which is under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation on him is that you open the patient's mouth, their head being in your lap; and lift their tongue, and then incise that nervous ligament according to its width until the tongue is freed from its constraint.
But if in that [area] there is any hardness or knot, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you make an incision and cut that across its width until you have incised the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the depth of the flesh and that you do not cut too deeply and cause a flow of blood.
And then wash the site of the incision with warm water or with vinegar or with cold water; and then place under the tongue a linen cloth; that is, a small cloth which the patient keeps under the tongue each night so that it does not heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess resembling a small tumor occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it is quickly enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting before the sun, and examine the abscess.
