And if you see it is dark or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is tending towards white, and moist, then you place the lancet there, and incise it with a fine instrument, and incise it on all sides; and if blood flows during your operation, then you place crushed vitriol upon it until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it is washed with verjuice and salt; and treat it with the other cures for this until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Concerning the extraction of an abscess from the two ears and of that which is situated in the throat and of other abscesses.
When a goiter develops in the throat, these are glands that are gathered together like cotton, which protrude outwardly and are called two ears; when you treat them with what we have said in the division and it is not healed, then you consider if the abscess is hard, of dark color, with little feeling, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron because of the flow of blood; rather, let it mature, and then either you will extract it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then it is one that should be incised.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already matured completely through rest or if it has diminished somewhat; and then make the patient sit before the sun, and their head should be in your lap, and open their mouth, and the servant, taking it between their hands, presses the patient's tongue down with a very fine instrument of which this is the form:
And let it be made of silver or brass, and let it be as thin as a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue with that and the abscess is shown to you, and you have gazed upon it, then take an onion and put it in the abscess, and pull it outwards as much as you can. Furthermore, if you pull out some matter from the tunics (layers of tissue), then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Furthermore, if you pull out some matter from the tunics (layers of tissue), then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to forceps, however its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be made of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula of which this is the form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner, and immediately after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, let him gargle with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar styptic substances until the flow of blood is restrained, and immediately treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those two [already mentioned], and incise those as I have said in the incision of the two sides equally.
And I myself once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat, which abscess was tending towards a dark color, with little feeling, which had already closed her throat, and the woman breathed with a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her food and drink of water, and she was close to death if she had lasted for a day or two more.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had formed two points from which it emerged through two openings.
Therefore I treated it with a fistula and inserted an instrument into one of the openings, and I pulled it, and a large portion was extracted from it, and immediately I incised it from where I understood the other opening was, and immediately I opened its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and immediately I inserted the instrument into that abscess.
And immediately I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore the throat of that woman was freed, and she immediately began to drink water, and soon she took food.  Therefore I did not cease to incise that abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess succeeded it in that place, which I incised until it was completely removed.
Then I took hold of it and cauterized her throat; thus it was stopped. And immediately I walked around and did not know what God had done regarding me in this.
Chapter 37.
On the incision of a wen when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is abscessed and long, then it is called a perpendicular [abscess]; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a bubo.
But when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and your cure does not succeed, and you see that the cauterized abscess is still present and is subtle, then you should incise what has grown round; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, of a dark color or black, and there is no feeling in it, then you should attempt its incision, for in that there is danger to the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner I have described, in its whiteness and length, make the patient sit and press down his tongue with an instrument that suits its shape, and then insert the incision in the uvula and pull it down and incise it with one of the two instruments which are described in the incision of the tonsils.
And you should not incise it except what is exactly within the natural limit without addition, for if you incise more, you will injure his speech.
And for the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of the tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use skill in cauterizing him without causing fear, and the method of cauterization is only with a sharp instrument; you should place the patient's head on your lap, and then press down his tongue with the instrument we have mentioned, and then take the liquid which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with that live coal, and do not apply it thickly or thinly, and with that fill the concavity of the instrument which has this shape:
Let the extremity of it be concave, in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on his side so that the saliva or mucus from his mouth flows out because of the internally applied medicine, so that nothing of it descends on his throat and harms him.
And then take your hand with the medicine, and press it on the uvula for the space of half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried by the action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and submerge the cotton in the medicine, and introduce the spoon with the cotton in a cannula from above so that the cotton adheres to that cannula, and do this as many times as is necessary until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it because it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
And if you judge it necessary to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and after the cauterization, wipe the area around the uvula with cotton soaked in wine, and cleanse with it what remains around it from the medicine, and let it be washed with cold water, and let it be treated externally with an embrocation, and internally with gargles until it is healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method more subtle than cautery or incision, for it is cured with fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, oregano, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile,  [and] similar herbs, and mix all these ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and let them be submerged in sour wine and boiled; and let the pot be well sealed with a suitable lid, and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which a concave brass instrument is placed in this manner; it should be made of silver or brass.
And let the end [of the instrument] be inserted into the patient's mouth so that the vapor may reach the uvula through the cannula, until the uvula is sufficiently fumigated.
And then repeat this several times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the appearance of an abscess, for it would increase the abscess too much, and you should not do this, but rather cauterize the inflamed area of the abscess.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a reed and attach a broken eggshell to the end of it, so that the patient's mouth is protected, because the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and that which adheres in the throat and of other causes.
Many times something adheres to the throat, such as a fishbone or some other thing.
Then it is necessary that you extract that which is apparent of those things, upon which your sight falls, after you have pressed the tongue with an instrument before the sunlight, so that that which is in the throat may be revealed to you.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very quickly the thing attached to the throat will be expelled by vomiting; or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root, or a piece of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sea sponge, bind it firmly with thread, and then let the patient swallow it, for when it reaches the place where the thorns are, it will extract them and they will be expelled. And moreover, take an eel, bind it with thread, and let it be put inside and quickly extracted; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly draw it out.
But if it is not expelled by those methods which we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be thicker than a small root, and at its end let there be a concavity which you may easily introduce into his throat.
And let him have his head raised up, and beware of touching his throat bone, so that it may happen to him, with that [instrument], to push the bone or thorn, or that the physician does it with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself introduces it, because of his knowledge of the location of the attached cause, and pushes downwards, and pulls his hand with the instrument upwards, all this according to the amount which is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and it does not come off, then consider it in the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed his tongue with the instrument which we have recommended to you.
Then, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with delicate, gentle movements.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this as many times (until the leech is dead and detaches itself from the throat), and abstain the patient from all water that day.
Then take a vial full of cold water, and open his mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not swallow anything from it, and stir the water from time to time with your hand, because the leech, as soon as it feels the water, falls off.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cyperus or with asafoetida using the instrument which we have recommended in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall out.
And another method of operation in the fumigation of it, is that you take a pot in which there are ignited coals, and the pot is covered with a cover in the middle of which is a hole, and the end of the instrument is fitted into that hole; and then let the fumigation come from above; and let the patient put his mouth to the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall out.
And if it does not fall out, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and do not drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps like those sometimes used, except that it has a certain curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is hollow, in which there is a roughening of the file, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the sum of the discourse concerning the opening of abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of many species, of many types, as you will remember from their description, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is by the type of abscess itself; and because of what we understand of the humors.
And the second way is according to the locations in which they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and each has its own judgment for operation.
And moreover, there are some abscesses which should not be perforated except near maturation of the pus within them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which should be opened when they are raw and not matured according to their completion, such as abscesses that are near the joints, because an abscess when it is near a joint, its being is prolonged until that which is in its circumference is digested, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore the cause of contraction of that member is made; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured, it will harm the principal member; or it is near the buttock, therefore perforate it even if not matured; and you should not perforate it raw, of incomplete digestion, except that which is near the buttock, so that the pus of it may not spread and be elongated to the inner parts of the buttock, and a fistula be made, or that it be made in its term that it may not be healed. But you must know the time of the perforation of the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when there is a lessening of the pain and the cessation of the fever, and also the diminution of the redness and of the pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know that the perforation falls in the place most below the abscess and from the pus coming from the healing of the eminence.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or of the feet, and away from the tendons, the cords of the nerves, the arteries; and finally, in all equal places in which there is no reflection; because in those that are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you delay its perforation until its digestion is completed according to what we have said; for if you perforate it before that, the course of its pus and much filth is prolonged, and quickly its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation, or many incisions, according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which it is necessary that the skin be incised and hollowed out when it is made like bread and is ripe, as happens in cases of swellings and buboes.
And some of those are incised with a three-angled incision; and others are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And there are those in which a round incision and a lunar incision are administered, and similar ones.
But that which is not like the aforementioned superficial abscesses, you must perforate with only a superficial or flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you must perforate it, and do not worry about extracting all the pus at that time; having extracted some of it, then pack the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger portion, and then do this many times according to its degree until it is all discharged.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in a weakened state, because the vital spirit is often depleted with the discharge of pus, and the patient dies very easily, and you must operate carefully on them.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, you must clean the wounds and consider whether the wound of the abscess is small, or a flat incision; then you administer lint or old linen to it; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you must place a wick in each opening until they unite.
And if a part of the skin is excised in the abscess or you have hollowed it out in the circumference, then you must fill it with old linen or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have extracted splinters or roots, without moisture; and tighten it until the third day; and then proceed and treat it with the ointments that are necessary until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and sour wine in which you soak a linen cloth, and apply this to the bleeding area many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer the treatment and cure and powders which I have recommended in many places in this our book and division.
But if it is winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you should mix powders in hot wine and oil, and apply those to the areas.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, and all this should be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and cleanse it and administer to its treatment what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction I have already discussed each abscess and how to treat it in specific chapters.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which appear on the back of the head.
On the back of the head, small abscesses are found, a kind of nodes containing membranes in their composition; and their appearance is varied.
And some are purulent, and those contain a moisture similar to slime. And some contain a moisture resembling dust, and similar to them; and some are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, since the extraction of them does not cause contrary effects.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is appropriate for this, which is called a probe, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is moisture, then incise it lengthwise with a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin the incision from line B to line G. Then, when the moisture is evacuated, you scrape away the skin that contained that moisture, and do not leave any of it at all, for often it returns if any of it remains.
And then immerse cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in salt water; and fill the wound with it and leave it there until the next day, for in this way you corrode all that remains of that film.
And then apply the cotton with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if it is needed, until you are confident that there is no trace remaining of that film; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you incise it according to this form: with an incision in the shape of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from every part, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane which contains it.
But if the arrow causes resistance, then do what I have recounted to you first.
And similarly, treat a petrified abscess with incision and cure as we have told you; and incision on a petrified abscess is easier, because it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes some of these abscesses, which are in the head in some men, have no moisture at all; the reason is that I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a dull, rough, white stone, which could not be cut, and if anyone were struck with it, it would hurt them.
But in other accidental abscesses in the head other than this one, this is not the chapter, such as the abscesses that occur on the heads of children and behind the roots of the ears, but the incision of all these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those in the lowest part of them so that the body may be relieved of the pus in the lowest parts.
And then treat those with what is appropriate for that cure.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42. Of the incision of scrophulas which happen in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck and in the armpits and in the groin; and sometimes they happen in other parts of the body.
And those which happen in the neck, and many are generated among them, and every scrofula of these is within its own tunic, and thus as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the types of these scrofulas are many, for of these some are stony, and of these some contain humidity, and of these some are malignant which do not obey cure.
Therefore, in that which you see of good disposition on palpation, of which the color that is manifest is close to the color of the flesh, and is soft in every part, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, it is necessary that you incise it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body, from line G to line B, and scrape it from every part, and extend the lips of the flesh with hooks, or with many hooks if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is most important that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the instrument not be too sharp, lest it cause you to injure your hand with the incision, or that it disturb the patient, and that you incise what is not necessary to incise; for if you should incise a vein or artery and prohibit yourself from your operation, then place on the wound crushed vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound becomes softened and begins to be suppurated. When the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then inquire with your finger if there is any cause of other small scrofulas, then incise those and cleanse them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, it is necessary then that you not incise that scrofula at its root. Beforehand, it is necessary that you tie it with strong doubled thread and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then care for it.
And if you incise the entire scrofula, then it is necessary that you adjust the two lips of the wound and suture it immediately, so that no superfluity remains.  (The "2" after *cuzas* likely indicates a footnote or a variant spelling/word.)
But if you see that superfluity remains because of the size of the scrofula, it is necessary that you intend to incise the root of it, and extract it, and administer suture and that which is said [above].
And that scrofula which contains humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and place the perforation so that it is at the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes that which remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound already cleansed, then you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the abscess accidentally occurring within the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the throat, but no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the writing/inscription/account of the speech/discourse of those (people/doctors).
But in men having a quinsy, the extraction from the throat must be avoided, because no benefit is gained thereby, since all the veins and the major ones of the patient are there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth or throat or both ears, when the illness is not in the windpipe, it is necessary to use the incision of the throat to avoid the loss which is made by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the throat below three circles of the throat's circles, or four with a small incision in width in the space between two circles, such that the incision is in the membrane, not in the cartilage. And this place is suitable for the incision, because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant.
And if the physician is timid, then he should extend the throat membrane with a hook, and then cut the membrane until he reaches the pipe/canal, but he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any of those.
And then incise the membrane as we have recounted; and the incision of the windpipe is indicated by the flame which emerges from it with what is broken from it and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Then, since the time is critical in which perforation is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound's membrane, and sew that only without the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that cause the flesh to grow until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the summary of the discourse which we have recounted, the exposition is nothing other than to see that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the throat of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and his breath is cut off, and some proceed to incise the epiglottis so that the patient breathes through the wound for some respiration, and is saved from death.
And for that reason I order the wound to be left open until the malignancy of the illness is consumed; and its malignancy lasts for three days; and then at that time I order the wound to be sewn, and cure it until it is healed.
And this which I have seen with my own eyes is that a servant took a knife and put it in her throat and incised with it a part of the lung's tube, and I was called to her cure, and I found the mucus as it is found in those who are dying, and I uncovered the wound and found the blood which had come from the small wound.
Then I was satisfied that she had not incised a vein or artery, and the wind (air) was escaping from the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And there was no harm to the servant except hoarseness in the voice, and after some days she returned to a better state of health.
And now I say that in the incision of the throat there is no fear (danger).
Chapter 44.
Chapter 44. Of incision on an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess called throat hernia is a large abscess according to the complexion of the body, and it is common in women.
And it is of two kinds, for it is either natural or accidental.
And the natural kind cannot be cured.
And the accidental is of two kinds, of which one resembles fatty nodes, and the other kind resembles an abscess which is formed by the knotting of an artery, and in this there is danger. And one should not presume to use iron on it except in the case of a small one. And you should probe and investigate it with your finger; and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you should incise it like nodes, and extract it with whatever it contains from the skin and with a small scalpel; and if not, then its entire being should be examined, and then treat the place with whatever is appropriate for healing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
On the incision of the types of nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already described all their types in the division (of chapters/topics).
And it is necessary that I recount again the difference between a node and an abscess, as it may cause doubt.
Therefore I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and vehement pain until the boiling of the superfluity is settled; and putrefaction is complete, then the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that leonine [lion-like, referring to its color or appearance] matter which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is made like a bladder, whether larger or smaller, and they are of two types, for either they are greasy and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division (of chapters/topics).
Therefore it is necessary, when you arrive at the treatment of a node, that you explore and investigate it at the beginning with an instrument which is called an intromes, according to how its form will be in the chapter which is near this one.
And the manner of examining abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will rotate your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated the depth/thickness.
And then place your hand according to the size of the abscess, and then remove the intromes and consider what has come out in its track/wake.
And if the running humidity, whatever its color, is expelled, then you will incise it with a superficial incision as I have said in the case of other abscesses.
And if no humidity comes from the track of the intromes, then know that it is fatty/thick.
And dry it up with an incision in the form of a cross, according to this form: as I have taught you, and suspend it with hooks, and peel the skin from all sides easily, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract that pus with the node.
But if it is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract all the pus, and this happens many times, and then you extract it piece by piece until nothing remains.
Because if anything remains, little or much, the node will return again.
And if you see that some small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and bind the wound, and place on it a hot poultice, and treat it with another treatment until it is healed.
And if it is a large node, then you sew the two lips of it and treat it with that which causes it to grow flesh.
But if a pulsating vein is present, or a non-pulsating one, then you cauterize and fill the place with crushed vitriol and leave it tightly bandaged for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood subsides; and then return to incising that which remains of the node.

Chapter 47.
Concerning the care of the breasts of men which are similar to those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are inflamed due to the effect of sleeping, and then the breasts are inflamed, and they resemble the breasts of women, and become purulent, white.
And to one who has this, it is necessary that he incise those with a lunar incision according to this shape, from line B to line G, and then excise the entire thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medicine, and then join the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat this until it is healed.
But if the nipple inclines downwards and is softened by its size as happens in women, then let it be split into two superior halves, with two incisions similar to a lunar shape, each contained within the other at the end of each, so that the larger line contains the smaller according to this form, from line B and line G. And then excise the skin that is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer that which we have said of the suture and powders which are necessary in the healing until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what should be incised due to the patient's unease and bleeding, then it is necessary that you fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of the perforation of abscesses which come below the nipple.
These abscesses which come below the nipple are a type of scrofula, hard, which contain a sac-like skin.
And of those, some contain humidity; and those which contain humidity should be incised with a lunar incision according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all that is in the abscess of pus is evacuated, then you fill it with old linen and leave it there until the next day.
And then the linen should be removed and it should be treated with a suitable ointment for this, until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the type of nodes and scrofula, then you incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula, similarly.
But if the wound is so extensive that it produces putrefaction, then apply cautery to it as we have previously described.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from an arrow wound or a vein.
When an arrow is broken, and the shaft which is on it is consolidated, then often an abscess occurs.  And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are from an artery or vein: for when an abscess is from an arrow, it is long and extends deeply into the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a kind of grating or creaking sound within it.
And that which is from a vein is round on the surface of the body.
And incision of this abscess is dangerous, especially that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large, therefore its treatment with iron (cautery or surgery) should be avoided.  And that which is in the extremities, or on the head, should also be avoided.
But concerning that which is there from inflammation of the opening of the arrow wound, make a long incision along the shaft; and then widen the incision with hooks and around the arrow, and free it from the tissues until it is uncovered.
And then place a needle there and make it penetrate to the other side, and bind the arrow with a double ligature in two places — which procedure I have shown you in the extraction of two arrows that were in the temples.
And then puncture the place which is between the two ligatures with a spatula until all the blood that is there comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer a treatment in which pus is generated until the ligatures fall off; and then treat it with ointments suitable for this until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess caused by a severed vein, then you should hold with your hand as much as you can of the abscess with the shaft, and then insert the needle under the place that you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread on it so that you extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with a good ligature, as I have told you in the ligature of a grape, in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert the thread with that needle under the whole abscess below the incision of the first needle, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until, when what is inside has come out, then you incise the superfluity of the shaft, and leave what is tied from it.
And then place on it a powder which you have submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1: Of the abscess which happens through the twisting of nerves.
Just as abscesses happen in an artery and in a vein, similarly abscesses happen in the nerves when they are struck or when there is excessive labor, and similarly.
And it is formed most often in the locations of the joints or of the heel, or in any place where the joints are bad.
And the abscess is serious, and it resembles the color of the other body, and it is in the worst dispositions of them, and when it is constricted with vehemence, the patient feels a kind of numbness.
And the abscess is not aggregated in the front of the body, and therefore it is under the skin, and it is bad in every part and does not go to the front or back parts.
Therefore, that which is in the joints should not be invaded with iron, for contraction will quickly result.
But that which is on the head or on the forehead, pierce the skin with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then take it with forceps and cut it from the root.
And if it is large, then you suspend it with a hook, and flay it, and then remove it; and close the wound with suture, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which happens in the area that happens in the abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies,  those things which are called fungals by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and the lips of these are turned inwards, and there are some that are small and some that are very large.
And I have seen one, and in his belly were two such things which were more like fungi, and there was no difference between them in whiteness,  [having] a slender root.
And the lips of these were turned inwards and split open, and humidity flowed from it constantly.
Therefore I incised them and found in one about eighteen ounces and in the other about six ounces.
And the operation in the incision of them is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the growth is white, humid, with a subtle root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and let cauteries be in your presence in the fire, because many times after the section of it much blood is expelled; and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, beware of incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind the growth with it, in the manner which we have said; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease to tighten the lead strongly around the growth, until it is severed and falls off by itself without effort.
But if the growth is of a thick root, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the belly.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
And then care for it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to incise a growth which is of a dark color, with little feeling, and of a slow growth, for that is a cancerous ulcer.
And I bring to mind a crab in relation to this.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the treatment of umbilical hernia.
Umbilical hernia is caused by many things; either by fissures of the umbilicus which is upon the belly, and from it the membrane or intestine protrudes as in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as has been said before, or by constricted wind within it.
But if it is due to a fissure of the umbilicus and protrusion of the membrane, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, then its location is as we have recounted, with greater variation; and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and returns readily; and it is easily felt there, and is greatly increased by the entry of a bath and strenuous work.
But if it is due to moisture, then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it grow.
And if it is due to blood, then the swelling appears with these signs, tending towards blackness.
But if it is due to raw flesh, then the swelling is heavy, large and hard, and is located in a specific area.
But if it is due to wind/gas, then its touch is soft.
And the operation on it is that you consider then if the swelling of the umbilicus is from arterial blood, or venous blood, or from wind/gas; then you must be prohibited from its treatment, because in that there is fear and dread as I have taught you in the chapter in which I have discussed swellings from artery and vein.
And if the swelling of the umbilicus is due to the intestine or the membrane, then you must order the patient to hold their breath, and be lying down extended; and then mark with a probe around the entire umbilicus, and then command them to lie between your hands on their back, and then surround with a broad spatula the circumference of the umbilicus over the area which you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the swelling to the upper parts with a large inch; and then tie around it with strong thread or with silk, and in a decent tying manner.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling over the ligature and insert your finger into that opening and seek the intestine; and if you have found it and secured it by the ligature, then you extract carefully and push the intestine into the inner parts of the abdomen.
And if you have found the membrane, then take that with forceps; and incise its superfluities.
But if it is an artery or vein that is involved, then surround that spot, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and put on them two strong threads and insert the two needles into the circumference which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, which you make penetrate; and then tighten the swelling in four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the extended flesh dries up and falls off by itself; or incise it when it is thinned and dried, and then cure it with what is appropriate of ointments until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the umbilicus is of flesh grown there or by humidity, then it is necessary that you conquer the swelling as we have said and extract the blood or humidity which you find there, and then cure it with what is appropriate and consolidate the place.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken, in the division, of the types of cancer and in what manner it is subject to treatment with medicines, and that the treatment with iron is to be avoided so that it is not ulcerated; and we have said that cancer is generated in the mother, and cautioned about its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, as in the case of cancer which is in the breasts or in the groin and similar members, to extract all of that, and especially when it is small and beginning.
But when it is old and large, one should not apply oneself to it, for I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen another who has succeeded in doing so.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you proceed and purge the patient, and draw off some black bile, and then bleed the patient if manifest repletion is found.
Then prepare the patient with a preparation in which the location of the operation is situated, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then scrape thoroughly on all sides with a sharp curette until no trace of its roots remains. And let the blood flow, and do not staunch it lightly, and press the areas, and wipe away the thick blood entirely with your hand or with an instrument which is available to you among your instruments.
But if you find an excessive flow of thick blood from an incision of a vein or an artery, then cauterize it until the flow of blood ceases, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a standard chapter numbering system)
On the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy of water, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites, and iron is not applied to the species of tympanites or to the species of anasarca, for that is mortal.
And when you have treated this species of tympanitic dropsy with medicines and your treatment is ineffective, then consider, for if the patient has already acquired weakness, or has another illness along with this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain and similar ailments, then beware of treating with iron, for there is danger in this.
But if you see the patient with exuberant strength, and with him there is no other illness but dropsy alone, and he is neither a child nor an old person, then the manner of his operation is that you lift the patient between your hands, and the assistant gently presses his belly with his hand, driving the water toward the lower part of the abdomen, and then take a lancet in this way:
Let it be two-edged, long, with a sharp point like a lancet; but nevertheless, let there be some shortness in it, little by little, so that in the operation with it you do not enter the intestine, and injure it.
And then consider if the origin of the dropsy is from the intestines, for then it is necessary that you, with the incision, move away from the navel by the amount of three fingers toward the lower parts, to the right of it, above the pubis.
But if the origin of the dropsy is due to liver disease, then your incision should be three fingers to the left of the navel.
And if its origin is from the spleen, then your incision should be on the right side.
And it is not fitting that your incision be on the side on which the patient wants to lie, lest the superfluities run to that weak place.
And then perforate the entire peritoneum with the instrument, and then insert it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the peritoneum and the omentum, and you should peel it back; and if it is the quantity of a fingernail or thereabouts.
And then perforate the omentum until the spatula reaches the empty place, which is the location of the water. And then extract that and insert an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or tin, which at its lowest part has a small hole, and on its sides there are three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its end is incised in the manner of a feather, thus:
And at its uppermost end is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then it immediately rests on the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in a moderate quantity, because if you evacuate more than is appropriate at that time, the patient will very quickly die from the resolution of their animal spirit, or you will cause a syncope which is a sign of approaching death.
But evacuate according to the quantity of its virtue and according to what the patient's disposition signifies to you by the virtue of their pulse and their well-being.
And then extract the instrument, and retain the water, the reason for which is that it is immediately retained by the chain which holds the hole that is above the cup, of which I have already told you to perforate it in that manner.
And then reinsert the instrument the next day if you see the patient tolerating it, and extract a small quantity of water, and do this for a few days cautiously, and beware of error so that little water remains.
But if you are afraid for the patient, and leave a large quantity of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and induce sweating in a bath, and expose him to the sun for a long time; and treat him with drying medicines until he is healed.
And cauterize the stomach, spleen and liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 55.
Of the treatment of children who are born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born from its mother's womb and the head of its penis is not perforated (or the opening is narrow or not in its proper place).
Therefore it is necessary to treat it with perforation as soon as it is born, with a very fine probe, according to this method:
And then place a thin lead pin into the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when it wants to urinate, it should be stimulated and it will urinate; and then return the pin.
And if you do not place the lead [pin], it is possible that the urine, which goes to that place, will not allow it to close.
But that [wound] whose opening is narrow should be treated with a lead pin, as we have said, for several days until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its proper place, it is said, the cause of this is that in those who are born, the opening is below the tip of the penis, so that they cannot urinate properly until they raise the penis with their hand upwards; and this is not [a matter of] generation, because the semen cannot reach the mother in a straight line, and it is a very long-lasting illness.
And the manner of the operation on him is that the patient should be placed on his back, and then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with strong traction, and incise the tip of the penis at the site of the foreskin with a sharp scalpel, like a feather incision, or shave a little of it away, so that the middle of it becomes similar to the head of the penis and that the opening falls in the middle, as it should be.
And be careful in your operation about the flow of blood, for this often happens; and stop it with something that absorbs the blood, and treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Concerning phimosis, which occurs in the foreskin at the head of the penis, and of corruption, blackness, and adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the penis.
This phimosis often occurs on the penis and is an excess of fleshy growth.
And of these, one is malignant and the other is non-malignant.
For the non-malignant type, you should suspend it with a fine thread and cut it away until it is completely removed; then, apply to the wound a pledget soaked in Egyptian ointment; and afterwards, treat it with diachylon ointment until it is healed.
And if the phimosis is malignant, of a dark color, then you should apply cautery to the incision and the root of it.
But if the phimosis is on the prepuce of the head of the uncircumcised penis, and one part of the phimosis is inside the prepuce and the other part is outside, then you should remove the part that is inside first until it is healed; and then you treat the outside part, because if you treat both at the same time, there is a risk that the prepuce will be damaged.
And if you observe in the genitals and prepuce blackness and corruption, then you should remove everything that is already blackened and that is beginning to corrupt or is already corrupted; and then apply to it a poultice of honey, which is mixed with crushed and sifted pomegranate rind flour and powdered herbs.
And then treat it with another appropriate treatment until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
because cautery is beneficial for two conditions at once, that is, for bleeding and for a wound when it is corrupted.
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you should insert a lead cannula into the meatus of the prepuce so that the patient can urinate through it.
In the adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis (and this adhesion only occurs in one whose prepuce is healthy, and circumcision has not been performed on him; and when adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess) it is necessary that you scrape with a blunt spatula until the adhesion is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate these exquisitely, then you should scrape with a spatula some of the head of the penis and the prepuce, and this is because the prepuce, when thin, is very easily perforated by its slight thinness.
Then separate the prepuce from the head of the penis with a very thin linen cloth, which has already been moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
And then it should be treated with styptic wine until it is consolidated (healed).
Chapter 57.
Of the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to those circumcised.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and since it is especially performed on infants, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest path leading to health.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not describe circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience.  The reason is that I have found most of the surgeons and barbers using circumcision and cleansing with a lancet and with forceps; and they use ligature with thread and incision with the fingernail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with forceps, with ligature with thread, than cleansing with the fingernail, because many times the membrane escapes higher up, because the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, and it is easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with cleansing which is done with an awl, and is digital, which shoemakers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not secured, because it easily enters the hole [of the penis].
But in the operation with a fingernail, it easily rusts and corrupts your operation. And the foreskin of a child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need for cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with forceps and ligatures with thread, the goodness, experience has shown me, because the forceps are of proportional incision, for this reason that the sharpness which is above is the same as the sharpness which is below; and therefore as you press your hand, two sharpnesses are proportional, and you incise according to a comparison at one time; and a compression is made of the thread with a form containing the foreskin of the penis completely, with which no error at all happens.
And the manner of the first operation is that you make the child, especially if he is among those who understand a little,  to remain still, so that you do nothing but tie the thread to his penis only, and leave it there until the next day.
And then make him happy in every way possible that he can receive to his understanding. (Or: ...that is possible to his understanding.) And then make him lie between your hands, prepared and still; and hide the forceps in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them or any other of the instruments.
And then put your hand to his penis and blow on the foreskin, and pull it back until the head of the penis is revealed, and then cleanse it of what is added to it of dirt.
The shape of the forceps for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let the extremities be non-cutting and the others be cutting, so that the branch of the nail does not protrude, and let the length of the two points be like the length of two handles equally. (This refers to the design of surgical forceps, specifying that some parts should be blunt and others sharp, and that the overall length should be balanced.)
But the error in circumcision is that you very quickly remove the entire inner foreskin, or a part of it, after the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary that you extend that [foreskin] with your fingernail before the suppuration of the area and that you incise it evenly.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then grasp it with forceps and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the inflamed penis, then you should leave it until the hot abscess subsides, and carefully incise it, and cut it as you are prepared to do.
And be careful of the head of the penis, for that will not harm him.
And then treat it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised beyond measure from the scrotum, and you attend to the parts above, this will not harm him.
And furthermore, treat it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
Concerning the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction from a stone, or by congealed blood, or by pus, or by flesh, and similar to those things which we have said about the methods of treatment in the division.
And if it is not dissolved with what we have said, and the cause is vehement in the patient, then it is necessary to administer extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver and be subtly long and concave, like a bird's quill, with a thinness of a long ray, in the likeness of a palm and a half, to which there is a thin tube at its head.
And the manner of drawing out the urine by this means is that you take a doubled cloth and tie at its end wool or cotton with a good binding; and introduce the ends of the cloth into the part below the syringe, and cut with the scissors if any superfluous part of the wool remains, but introduce it into the cannula with force. And then grease the syringe with butter and egg white.
And make the patient sit on a chair, and rub their bladder and penis with moist oils, olive oil and rosewater.
And then introduce the syringe easily until it reaches the root of the penis; and then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; and then push the syringe inside that; and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels it has reached the empty place, and this is not done according to this order, but the passage goes in a tortuous way.
And then extend the cloth with the wool little by little, because the urine will wet the wool.
Then extract it and make a cure with urine, and use a syringe; do not cease doing this and use the syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Concerning the method of irrigating the bladder with a catheter and similar instruments suitable for this purpose.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or blood clots in it, or putrefaction is constricted there, and you wish to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument which is called a catheter, and this is its form:
except that its extremity is blunt, that is, closed. And in that (extremity) are three holes: two on one side, thus it goes.
At the beginning is a concave place, in which is what is pushed in, and it should be according to the quantity that fills it, without addition, except that when something is drawn from the humors with it, it should be drawn, and when it is expelled, it should be drawn with it, and when it is expelled long, as the catheter does with which naphtha is inserted in sea battles.
Therefore, when you want to extract humors from the bladder, insert the extremity of the catheter into the humor, and draw with it what is pushed to the upper parts, because the humor is drawn into the concavity of the catheter.
And then insert the extremity of the catheter into the urethra as we have recounted in the syringe; and then expel the humor with what you expel, because that humor comes to the bladder immediately, and the patient feels that.
The form of the fine catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is its form:
And let it be made of silver or brass, of which the upper end should resemble a small feather, and below it a canal inside, smoothly.
And then take a goatskin bladder, and place in it the humor that you wish to inject into the bladder, and then tie it between two [objects] with a strong double thread ligature, and warm that humor over the fire.
And then insert the end of the catheter into the penis.
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid with gentle pressure until the patient says that the liquid has already reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it; and pierce it all around its circumference; and then insert strong thread into those holes, so that it is reinforced; and then grease the circle so that it is greased like a clyster, after you have placed in it what you want of the humors, oils, and waters.
And then you tie that to the instrument and do with it as you have done with the bladder, and express it with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have already mentioned in the division the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and their signs.
And we have discussed the stone in which one operates with the hand, and incision.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating this with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that stones generated in the bladder mostly affect children; and among its signs is that the urine which is expelled from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs his genitals, and often cries out in pain, and is restless; and with this he expels much stool.
And the cure of children from this is easier until they reach fourteen years; and the cure is difficult in the elderly. But in young people it is moderate because it is between those [extremes - referring to easier cure in children and difficult cure in the elderly].
And of that [stone] which is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is contrary to that [i.e., more difficult].
Therefore, concerning the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning that we purify the patient with a clyster which removes all the filth which is in his bowels; for this filth sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone through its examination.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet up and shaken and massaged downwards so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or that it jumps from its place sometimes.
Then make that  (presumably a patient support or positioning aid) between your hands, prepared, and his hands should be below his thighs, so that the whole bladder is made to incline downwards.
And then search for it and everything else around it (the stone).
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space, then you must immediately attend to it with an incision.
And if it does not completely fall below your touch, then it is necessary that you anoint the index finger of your left hand with oil, if the patient is a child, or the middle finger if he is a grown young man, and insert it into his anus. And search for the stone until it is below your finger. And return it little by little up to the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards to the place where you made the incision.
And command the assistant to press the bladder with her hand; and command another assistant with her right hand to extend the genitals upwards, and with her other hand to hold back the perineum which is below the genitals, in the region where the incision is made.
And then introduce a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: and cut what is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be above the stone, and your finger in the anus, and let the incision be transverse, so that the external incision is wide and the internal narrow according to the size that the extraction of the stone allows, and no larger, because very easily by compressing with your finger, which is in the anus, the stone will be expelled through the incision without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and jagged, which makes their extraction difficult because of this.
And of those that are like a nut, and are round, the extraction is easily done.
Therefore, if it is one that has angles and edges, adjust it in the fissure.
And if it is not thus expelled, then it is necessary that you push on it, or that you tighten around it with a decent instrument, the end of which is like a file with which you press on the stone, and it does not escape; or that you introduce under it a subtle instrument with a curved end.
But if you cannot [reach/work on] it, then enlarge the opening a little, and if you want to stem the flow of blood, constrict it with crushed vitriol.
But if the stone is more than one, then at the beginning you place your hand at the opening of the bladder, and then incise it; and then gently push on it; and similarly proceed if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is unwise to incise it with a large incision, for it causes one of two things to the patient: either death or continuous urinary incontinence, because the area is not completely healed.
But administer its expulsion until it is expelled, or work on breaking it with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Therefore, when you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with aloe and dragon's blood powder, and bind it tightly. And place over that a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be calmed.
Then lay the patient supine on their neck, and do not untie the bandage until the third day.
And when you have untied that, bathe the area with water and much oil, and then treat it with diachylon ointment and basilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound there occurs a hot, burning, and corrosive abscess, and of such an appearance as if blood were frozen in the bladder, and urination is thereby prevented (and this is known by the passage of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains, it provokes corruption of the bladder and putrefaction thereof.
Then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and with a poultice of whatever is suitable for its healing until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the entire treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is lodged there, and thus urination is prevented, then treat it with what I recommended before you resort to incision, for many times because of this treatment incision is required; and I have proven this, and it is that you take a heated iron perforator, of which this is the form:
of triangular extremity, sharp, set in wood.
Then take bile, and bind with it the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the perforator reaches the stone. And revolve the perforator with your hand against the stone little by little, and you will enlarge the perforation until you make it penetrate from one side to the other; for then the urine will be immediately released.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outside of the penis, because it is broken, and it will be expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this cure is not suitable for you for some reason that prevents it, then tie one bandage below the stone, and another bandage above the stone. And then incise upon the stone in that penis between the two bandages. And then extract it. And then untie the bandages and clean the congealed blood which is formed in the wound.
And it is not necessary to bandage below the stone except to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other bandage above only because, when the bandage is untied near the extraction of the stone, the stone returns to its place and closes the wound.
And for this reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the bandage above, that you elevate the stone to the upper parts, or that it returns thanks to your action, and so closes the wound as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
Stones are rarely generated in women.
But if a stone is found in any of them, then the cure is difficult, and is forbidden in many ways.
One reason is that women are very easily made virgins again (i.e., their hymen can easily reform or be mistaken for intact).
And the second is that you will not find a woman, whether chaste or married, who willingly exposes herself to a physician.
The third is that you will not find a woman who well knows this art, especially the manual operation.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for stones in women is distant from the location of the stone itself, therefore requiring a deep incision, and there is fear in that.
But if necessity compels you to this, then it is necessary that you take a female physician who is well-skilled, and few such are found.
But if you are deprived of that, then seek a cautious and skillful male physician, or present a matron well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who knows some part of this art.
Therefore, present that woman, and command her to do all that you order her, from the initial examination for the stone;  the reason for this is that if she observes whether the woman is a virgin, then she must put her finger in her rectum and search for the stone.
Then, if she finds it, she should grasp the stone with her finger, and then command her to incise over it.
But if she is not a virgin, then command the matron to insert her finger into the vagina of the patient and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder, and compress it with good compression.
Then, if she finds it, she should gently draw it from the opening of the bladder towards the lower parts with the sum of her strength until it reaches the root of the thing.
And then incise over it near the midpoint of the vagina, towards the root of the thing, wherever it is stopped in her, and center the stone at that point, and her finger should not be removed from the stone while grasping it.
And the incision should be small at first; and then insert the tool into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then adjust the incision according to the size that she knows the stone will be removed from.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches.
Therefore you should know how many types there are of them so that you can signify what you want about them.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the place and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, and then return to your operation until the stone is removed.
And see that you prepare with you the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you are aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been incised, then place powder on the area and bind it tightly with powders and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will quickly die. And then care for the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the blood has subsided after a few days, and the area has healed, you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62.
Concerning incision for a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white sheath which is below the genitals' scrotum containing the testicle, and is called the sheath.
And sometimes it is in the proper membrane which nature has prepared for the testicle, so that it is enclosed as if it were another testicle; and it is the scrotum between the white sheath which we have mentioned, and this is only cartilage.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it happens due to a blow inflicted on the genitals.
And this moisture has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or red, or black and foul-smelling, or watery white, and this is the most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white sheath which we have mentioned, then the swelling is round, tending slightly to length, like the shape of an egg, and it appears above the testicle, because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper membrane, then the swelling is round with part of the testicle and therefore one believes it to be another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the covering of the genitals and the white sheath, then it falls below the scrotum.
And if you want to know the color of the moisture, then you examine the color of the moisture in the swelling with a probe, the shape of which has been previously determined; and according to what has appeared on the probe, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to its treatment with iron (cautery), then it is necessary that we order the patient to be bled if that is possible for you, and cleanse his body.
Then let him be supine on his back on something a little raised, and place many cloths beneath him.
Then sit on his left side, and order the assistant to be on his right side, who should extend his hand from one of two sides to the covering of the genitals and to the part of the navel.
Then take a broad spatula and split the covering of the genitals from the middle, lengthwise, almost to the pubic bone.
And let the incision be made straight, equally distant from the line that divides the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the underlying white fat.
And scrape that [fat], and observe, and be careful not to cut it; and your scraping, with which you scrape it partially, should be suspended, and more subtly scrutinized with the scraping, according to the amount possible for you.
Then perforate the water-filled cyst with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the cyst upwards; and do not touch the covering of the genitals, and incise the cyst in the way that is possible for you. And incise it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, for if you do not exquisitely scrutinize its incision, there is no certainty that the water will not return.
But if the egg [cyst] protrudes from its covering during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the cyst, return it inside, and then join the two lips of the covering of the genitals with a suture, and then treat it like other wounds until it is healed.
And if you find the egg [cyst] corrupted by another illness, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels which are on the suspensory ligament, and extract the egg, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the fluid is collected in two separate parts, then know that there are two hernias; therefore incise that part as you have done the other equally; and if it seems to you that it is one operation, then you do it.
And then you wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place on the outside other wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the genitals and the area of the abdomen; and place over it folded cloths and powders, and bind those on top with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then two cloths should be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and the ends should be made with which it is pressed with cotton or damp wool, and they should be attached with a tight binding to the inside of the breeches, and one of the ends should reach from the buttocks to the thighs, and the second should reach to the groin area, and two ends should be on the two thighs and two other ends on the two ankles, and the whole binding should be adjusted in the inside of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and that you incise with it the skin of the genitals, and it is ignited as we have recounted until the white cyst containing the fluid is uncovered, and then you take a cautery of this shape: and it is similar to the Greek letter.
And then pierce the cyst with it, and ignite it until all the moisture is expelled.
And then spread the cyst with tweezers, and scrape it with tweezers, and scrape it with a thin, ignited cautery; and incise it as much as possible until you eradicate all of it; and beware that the fire does not touch the egg.
And know that this operation is safer and involves less blood loss, and better than an operation with incision.
But if an abscess forms contrary to expectation during the incision or cauterization, or there is excessive bleeding, then you must treat whatever problem arises, with the treatment appropriate for it, as has been said.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its horror, then administer a perforation as I have described; and this is when you are certain, from what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should have the patient sit on some raised seat, then perforate the abscess at its lowest part with a wide spatula, and make the perforation according to the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough that all the water drains out.
Then place a cotton cloth over the area and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water drains out. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is resistance to the drainage of the water, at that time you should perforate it: and this is done only because the white pannicle in the opening of the wound creates resistance and prevents the exit of the water; then you should put into the wound a probe or instrument with which water is extracted from dropsies, the form of which is described before; or apply a small pressure to the opening of the wound.
And know that if the water returns and is collected again within six months or thereabouts; and when it is collected, then it should be perforated in the same manner, for the patient will thus pass his days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
Know that in the incision of this hernia there is danger leading to death in most cases; therefore, in my view, its removal and being saved by that [method] is better.
