And I am remembering the operation on that and the types of abscesses.
Therefore, I say that abscesses arise in the genitals which are composed of [certain parts], and this is caused by many things: either by an acute, acrid superfluity which is poured into the genitals, or by a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly is a lapified abscess, of which the color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by a knotting of the arteries, and by inflammation of those, so that its remembrance is perceived, or by inflammation of the veins.
But that which is from inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we press it with our fingers; and it should not be touched.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any cause from compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that an incision be made upon this kind [of abscess] and upon other flesh; and it is so that you incise the skin of the genitals, and then extend the testicle upwards, and extract it from the white sheath, and free the suspensory ligament, and ligate it, and incise the suspensory further until you free it from all parts of the scrotum.
Therefore, if the testicle is already consolidated with those inflamed tissues, then it is necessary that you extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between any part of the sheath, or between that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the place of the adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you incise all that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Therefore, when your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of a scrotal hernia.
A hernia is an abscess enveloped by some covering similar to a boil, with softening of the testicles, and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, it is necessary to be mindful of what preceded it.
However, we must describe the operation as the ancients did.
And I say that you should have the patient sit on a raised stool; then gently push the suspensory of the testicles downwards; and then hold the scrotum with your fingers along with the vessels that are attached to the penis; and have another assistant hold and firmly stretch those out.
And then incise with a sharp, broad lancet with a transverse cut directly over the vessels until the vessels are exposed.
And then scrape the entire area as we have instructed you in the extraction of arrowheads that are lodged in the temples.
And then insert a double-threaded needle into those [vessels], and tie it first at the place where you made the incision, and tie it again at the other end. And then incise those [vessels] in the middle with a straight cut along the length of the body, and extract whatever is accumulated of foul and corrupt humors.
And then treat the wound with the same treatment as other wounds in which you desire healing to be engendered, so that the vessels which bleed from one of the two genitals may be cauterized, for this is not harmful.
But if you cut all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed, or that the testicles be tied off along with those vessels so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they be atrophied, or that they be tied off so that there is no help for them.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a separation that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen on the side of the inguinal rings of the abdominal wall, causing the intestines to descend through that rupture.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these two types occur for many reasons: a blow, a jump, a shout, the lifting of some heavy object, or similar.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs gradually over a long time, and not suddenly; and it is an inguinal swelling that extends toward the groin because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, is that it initially causes severe pain suddenly; and it is a different swelling appearing below the groin, and this is because of the protrusion of the intestine and its body outside the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is protruded along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" is likely a transcription variant or a word specific to this dialect/text and may not have a direct modern medical equivalent.)
And sometimes it is caused by flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and it is retained there, and this causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of this type of illness, using iron (i.e., cautery) is dangerous, therefore it should be avoided in this case.
And the method of operating on this is that you command the patient to return the intestine with his hand to its cavity if it obeys its return, and then it should be wrapped around its neck between your hands, and raise his legs.
Then hold the cord, which is the spermatic cord, upwards, and incise the spermatic cord lengthwise; and then place hooks in the two lips of the incision as you need to open the incision with them.
And if the incision, according to the amount that is possible for you, allows the testicle to be expelled from it.
Then scrape away that skin which is under the spermatic cord until the hard white tunic is uncovered on each side.
Then you insert your finger into what is the testicle, into what is between the white tunic, which is under the spermatic cord, and the second tunic, and break up with it the adhesion which is in the part behind the testicle.
Then gently pull the spermatic cord upwards with your hand, and with this, stretch the white tunic upwards with your left hand, and elevate the testicle with the tunic to the edge of the incision; and instruct the assistant to pull the testicle upwards while you completely dissolve the adhesions behind with a complete solution [i.e., completely loosen or separate the adhesions].
And check with your fingers that there is no bowel enveloped in the hard white tunic. But if you find anything there, then push it back down into the abdomen.
Then incise in the area below the spermatic cord, through an incision from which blood and pus will flow, just as we have described above.
Then apply wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have described (and sometimes the white tunic is cauterized, which we have mentioned near the incision to prevent bleeding, because this often happens) and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed with warm water, and keep it there until it falls off as we have said.
Then treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
On a Windy Hernia (or,  Concerning a Windy Hernia/Ventosity)
In this hernia, I have not seen anyone who has been bold enough to treat it with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done as we have recounted in the hernia that is with blood; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on that with the patient's consent; and then dry the area around the abscess with something that opens it, until the vessels collapse.
And cure the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the disruption that is made in the inguinal region (groin).
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and swells the area.
And it does not allow the intestines any cause to descend into the genitals; and if it does, it is little; and it returns in all hours (at any time).
But if the time is prolonged with this, a fissure is made in the inguinal canal until the intestine descends into the inguinal canal; and this happens because of the extension of the inguinal canal which is in the groin as we have said, which is caused because the inguinal canal is stretched, and then it is softened and the area is inflamed and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery, as its narration has preceded.
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner: and it is that the patient lies on his back between your hands, and then dry the area of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with incision across the width according to the amount of three fingers.
And then tighten the white inguinal canal that is under the scrotum until it is uncovered (or exposed).
And then take the ray (presumably a surgical instrument, possibly a cautery) and place it on the prominent part of the inguinal canal, and press it towards the front of the abdomen.
And then sew the two prominent places on the end of the inguinal canal ray (instrument), and join one of them to the other with a suture.
And then remove the end of the ray (instrument), and do not completely incise the inguinal canal, nor extend the wound, nor anything else like that, as I will let you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat it with the treatment for wounds, and if you have incised the fibers, cleanse it and restore the wound until it is healed; because the inguinal canal is constricted and the prominence of the rupture is not aligned (or reduced).
And cauterization is better in this place, because it is closer to health (i.e., a more effective and less risky treatment).
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the swelling of the testicles.
Many times the swelling of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and this hinders their proper functioning (or development).
Then it is necessary for the one who is entrusted with his care to make the patient lie on his back, and to incise all the swelling which is softened above the natural swelling; and then to adjust the two lips (of the wound) with a suture.
And if you wish, sew up at the beginning the excess of the softened swelling with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and then care for it with the treatment for other wounds until it is healed and the stitches fall out.
Chapter 69.
On castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, but because of this I am obliged to mention it in this book; and its mention is necessary only in two ways, one of which is so that it is within the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it, so that he knows its treatment should it occur; and the other way is that many times we have need of the castration of other animals for our benefit, such as horses, goats, pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by abrasion, or by incision.
But in that which is done by abrasion, the method of operation is that we cause the animal to be bathed in warm water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. And then take them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear under the touch.
But in castration done by incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and grasp the scrotum with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory ligament, and incise over each testicle with one incision so that the two testicles are exposed.
And then incise them after you have skinned each one. And do not leave any material on those of the cyphus (likely referring to a wound or ulcer), except the fine cyphus which is on the vessels.
And this method of castration is better which is done by crushing, because crushing quickly leaves no trace of the testicles, so the animal does not desire copulation.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
On the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two kinds: one is where there appears in the space between the testicles, that which is between the two testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva in which there is hair.
And sometimes urine flows from that opening which is in the space between the testicles.
But in women there is one type: and it is that the vulva above the pubic bone is like two testicles of a man, somewhat smaller externally, like a man's prepuce and two testicles.
And the cure is of three types: two for men and one for women. And it is necessary that you incise those adhering flesh until the impression of it disappears, and then that you treat those with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second type in men, from which urine flows which is in the space between the testicles, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Concerning swelling and prominent flesh due to causes in women.
A slight swelling is readily attributed to natural causes until it is pressed and examined; and sometimes it is magnified in some women to such an extent that it spreads, as in men, and extends to the genitals.
Then you should hold the excess swelling with your hand or with a bandage and incise it.
And do not overdo the incision, especially near the root, lest a flow of blood ensue.
And then treat it with wound treatment until it is healed.
But a fleshy growth is flesh that grows in the mother's womb until it fills it, and it soon protrudes externally, resembling a tail (and for this reason some initially called this "tailed disease"); therefore, we must incise it as we incise a swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
Concerning the treatment of gout.
Gout is when the woman's vagina is not perforated, or is small. And this is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a preceding illness, and that is caused either by improperly formed flesh or by a thin or thick septum.
And it is formed either in the front of the mother, or on her sides, or in her upper part, or in her lower part, and it prohibits conception, pregnancy, and childbirth, and very quickly it prohibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by observing the womb; if the obstruction is apparent, it is straightforward. But if it is not apparent, then you search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin septum, and it is close to the two labia, then you cut and break it.
And you should place powders on the two labia, and then place your index fingers of both hands inside them.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then forcefully spread the two labia until that thin septum is broken and the obstruction is opened.
And then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area, and keep it moist throughout the day so that the incised area does not heal.
But if the septum is thick and dense, then you must incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to fleshy growth, then lift it up with a hook, and incise it.
And have with you medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and enses, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not close up too quickly.
And keep it there for some days and apply some dry lint.
And then it should be treated with the other consolidation treatment until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the birth of other fleshy growths, and then it is necessary to incise in this manner. But if this is due to a cancerous abscess, to the cancerous abscess which is in the mother [presumably, the breast or womb], no counter-irritation should be applied with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and of a boil and red tumor that occurs in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the orifices of many veins, from which much blood continuously flows; and when hemorrhoids are old, they become boils.
And I have already described in the division the types of these [hemorrhoids] and their symptoms, and I will say thus which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and boils, when they are in the womb and do not appear externally, then in those cases, treatment should not be done with iron [i.e., cautery or surgery].
But those which are at the orifice of the womb, upon which sensation falls [i.e., which are externally palpable], those are the ones that are cured.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cold bath and then excise the boil with scissors, or with a rough cloth, and incise them at their roots.
And then sprinkle upon those [wounds] to staunch the flow of blood, one of the incisive powders without biting [i.e., causing further irritation], such as acacia and similar things.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place them in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumac and pomegranate bark and oak bark and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this and apply it to his back.
And if the blood is stopped, good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice or in comfrey juice or in  mallow juice with old wine and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Concerning the perforation of a fistula occurring in the mother.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the mother, such as cancerous abscesses, stony abscesses, ulcerous abscesses,  abscesses with swelling, herpes, hemorrhoidal fistulas,  fissures, abscesses in the thighs (or similar), and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses, their types, their signs, and their cures in the previous section.
And it is necessary that I explain in this treatise the hot abscess which occurs in the mother, when it is among the abscesses that collect pus; and if you wish to know in what manner the operation is performed with iron (a cautery).
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute, with throbbing, phlegm, and fever; and if it becomes red and inflamed to the touch, then do not attempt perforation, but treat it with remedies that promote maturation until the severity of the symptoms subsides.
Then you must make the woman lie down on a couch that has two supports; and recline her on her back and adjust her feet to the lower part of her abdomen, and her thighs should be spread, and place her arms under her knees, and bind each with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife should stand on the right side and provide the instrument with which the opening of the womb is opened, the form of which will be described in the procedures that follow.
And when you wish to apply this instrument, you should place the tip of the instrument at the neck of the woman's womb; and that which enters the womb should not exceed the neck of the womb and harm the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the neck of the womb, then you should apply powders to the texture of the neck of the womb so that the entry of any instrument into the neck of the womb is prevented.
And you should place the turning device that runs on the instrument on the upper side, and let the assistant hold the instrument and rotate the turning device until the opening of the womb is opened.
And when the wound appears, and is touched by hand and is soft and wet, then you should dry that wound with a clean sponge.
And when the pus is evacuated, then you should place in the wound a piece of cloth soaked in rose oil or green oil, in which some astringent has been added; and place the compress in the section at the neck of the womb.
And place outside the womb and over the vulva clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then let it be treated for about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed in such a way that it is rinsed with water and honey, and then with water in which the root of lily or of birthwort has been boiled, and then returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is hidden inside the womb, then you must be prohibited from treating it with iron, taking care to do what we have said in the division. (The "division" likely refers to a previous section of the treatise.)
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner living embryos should be treated when they emerge in an unnatural way. (or... when they present abnormally)
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the form of natural childbirth by its signs, and it is that when you see the woman straining downwards and desires to smell the air, and moreover is relieved by what is above her of the birth, and hastens the emergence of the embryo, then you should know that this birth is according to natural form, and it is the emergence of the child head-first, and the afterbirth with it, either suspended or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see those signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily because when it descends head-first, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed of those superfluities with a good cleansing.
But that which is other than this birth is unnatural and unfavorable, for when the embryo emerges feet-first and its body is emerged before its head and feet, and one of its hands, or its foot, or its head and one hand, or its belly, and it is quickly turned on its neck—these are similar to the unfavorable presentations.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and skillful in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what skill is needed in them so that it may be signified by this and be established upon this.
When the fetus has emerged head-first through natural expulsion, and this is difficult for the woman and the birth is difficult, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then have her sit, or have the women support her, and evaporate her genitalia with cooked fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small, thin spatula between her labia and with it she tears the secundine, or she scrapes it with her fingernail until it removes the moisture within. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be given a clyster of mallow and fenugreek mucilage with anise.
And then, during the clyster, order her to be compressed and make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then you must reduce them gently little by little. But if it does not obey the introduction, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs, and then shake the stool on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she does not fall from the shaking.
And if the hands are not returned and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the remnants of the fetus, or tie the body to its hand with a cloth and extract it gently, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus is positioned on its feet, then you must push it upwards, and then gently turn the fetus, and then grasp it, and then take one of its feet and extend it little by little.
But when it has entered the vulva, then you command her to strain, and make her sneeze forcefully, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then you return the fetus little by little until you place it in its natural position, for it will then be expelled with ease.
And if it is prevented by everything we have recounted, then take mallow and dissolved gum, and pound everything in a mortar with good force, and then apply this to the vulva of the woman, and the lowest part of her belly. And then make her bathe in lukewarm water, up to her hypochondria (the region below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts of her are softened, then give her a fumigation of myrtle and apply it to her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and then make her sneeze and hold her mouth, and lift her up, and press the lower part of her belly with gentle compression, for the fetus will be immediately expelled.
Thus of the fetus, laterally holding one of its hands. Let it be managed by reduction of the hand, but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her hips with violent shaking, and then administer pessaries which I have recommended made of mucilage.
And if childbirth has occurred, then make her sit on a stool, and then work on the reduction of the hand, and grasp the fetus according to its natural form, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain herself until it is expelled.
Thus of the fetus, over the neck, its hands spread out, and its face is towards its mother. Let the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then gently turn it, and let it come out easily.
And when it is grasped, then place on its neck that which we have recommended of oil and mucilages.
And then instruct her to cough gently, and when you have done this, then make her sit on a stool and instruct her to lean on her right side, and then instruct her to strain; and make her sneeze with a suitable [remedy/method]. The prepared fetus exits sideways.
When the mother's suffering lessens in this manner, observe, and then you incise the secundines with your fingernail until it is emptied of its moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended around its face and neck, then you incise it at the umbilicus, so that it may not be retained and die.
And then gently push the fetus inwards until you place it as it should be, and tell the woman to strain a few times, and it will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
The birth of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare and seldom happens; but there are reasons for this in nature, and they do not live.
And count those, even if they are multiple, as one pregnancy, and they are separated by a membrane, distinguishing them by the ligament in the umbilicus of each one. And sometimes they are expelled as if it were one fetus by easy natural birth, and sometimes they are expelled by an unfavorable birth, as we have recounted.
And if some of their births are difficult and arrive at what we have said about unnatural presentations, then you treat those as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and skill, and with patience, and is resourceful according to every presentation as she is prepared for by her skill in bringing about health.
A remembrance of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and even ten or more are formed in the mother, and it has been verified by me that one woman aborted and expelled six, and another ten, all formed, which fact was established by the measurement of glorious wise men.
And all this must be in the knowledge of the midwife, because it easily appears similar to that, and therefore she must oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
On the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medication and it has not yet been expelled, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and is not afflicted by any of the conditions which cause loss of life to be feared for her.
Then the woman should be placed on the bed on her back, with her head inclined downwards and her hips elevated.
And the assistant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
Then you should moisten the mother's vulva with moist oils and with mucilage of mallow, and fenugreek, and linseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient places for this: then you should consider if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, and then you should grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or at the neck, or at the mouth, or at the palate, or under the chin, or at its clavicle, or in the places near the ribs, or under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is feet first, then at the heel, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary that you hold the umbilical cord with your right hand, and place its curvature in the space between the fingers of your left hand, and gently insert your hand and grasp the umbilical cord in one of those places we have mentioned, until you reach the empty cavity.
Then place and apply another umbilical cord, or a third if necessary, so that there is equal traction, and there is not extension only along its straightness.
Rather, it should be pulled evenly on all sides, so that its removal is eased, just as it is done with a tooth before its extraction.
And it is necessary in this that which is between them that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some of her fingers with oil and expertly insert them and loosen that which is retained with them.
Therefore, if part of the fetus is expelled, then the umbilical cords should be moved to a higher location; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
But if one hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then extract it until it is entirely expelled, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you should proceed with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or a constriction occurs during its emergence, or there is accumulated water on its head, then it is necessary to insert between the fingers a spiny spatula and split the head and extract the water, or to break it with an instrument which is named "alundat", the form of which will be shown with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which follows this one.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: then it is necessary to incise it with an algematu, or to grasp it with an alunda as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is out but constricted by the clavicles, then it should be split so that the fluid which is in the feet is drained.
But if it is not complicated, nor resists emergence, then you should incise the head immediately as much as you can.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscessed or dropsical, then you must perforate it so that the fluid within drains out.
But if the fetus is feet-first, then its extraction is easy, and its passage to the mother's birth canal is easy.
But if it is constricted around the belly and feet, then you must extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and you must incise the belly and feet so that what is within them drains out.
But if the other limbs are extracted and the head remains, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's birth canal is open, then she inserts her hand into the uterus, and searches for the head with it, and pulls it with her fingers into the mother's birth canal, and then inserts one or two fingers inside, and pulls it out with them.
And if the mother's birth canal is constricted by a hot abscess that harms her, then it must not be disturbed; instead, it is necessary to administer a perfusion of moist, oily substances. And administer plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is lying on its side, then, if possible, it should be turned: and what we have said should be administered as in the case of a living fetus.
And if this is not possible, then it should be incised in pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that no part of the afterbirth be left inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer what we have said concerning the treatment of the hot abscesses that might occur in the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and soon after that, she became pregnant again, and again the fetus died. A long time ago, an abscess occurred in his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and it began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied plasters of strong attraction to him, and I drew out a bone, and after a few days, I drew out another bone; and I was very astonished, since there should not be bones in the abdomen.
And then I considered that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I cleansed the wound, and extracted many bones from it, the woman being in good condition, and she lived in this way for a long time, still discharging a little pus.
And I did not consider this to be strange, but rather because in this there is skill and help so that the skillful physician with his hands administers the cure.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of incision with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a constrictor in which the edges are joined, in which joining there are two incisions at the two ends of two shafts. But this incision should be more subtle than the constrictor, and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each shaft be about two fingers, and the margin of each about one finger, and the length of each is one and a half palms; and in the middle space between the two shafts are two fastenings of the same wood, which are already fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the womb so that it is opened with them when you turn the two prescribed incisions.
Another form similar to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, in the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity are two additions, like those you see, and the length of these additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you wish to open the womb with them, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her legs hanging down, that which is between her thighs open. Then open your hand with the instrument, just as you do with scissors, according to the amount you want to widen the opening of the womb, until the woman does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
The form of the instrument with which the fetus is expelled:
The form of the instrument with which a large head is countered (or, dealt with).  The "2" likely indicates a variant or a second type of instrument.
And it resembles forceps which have teeth at the end, like a saw, and sometimes it is made long like forceps, in this way, like a saw, which has teeth like the teeth of a saw with which it is incised and countered (or, dealt with). Form of a small instrument; or, A small instrument's form.
The form of a hook having a spine (or point).
The form of a hook having two spines (or points).
The form of a spatula having two ends with which the head is severed (or cut).
The form of two broad spatulas.
All those instruments are used, and the more the types of those are multiplied and prepared by the artisans, the easier their operation is and the more valued they are by men because of their quantity (or number).
And do not neglect any of those instruments, whatever they may be, that are prepared for you, for the necessity of those is not excusable (or, their necessity cannot be avoided).
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should order the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze forcefully, and press your hand over her mouth and nostrils, and if by this it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then you take a pot and pierce a hole in its lid and put herbs inside it, opening the mother's mouth, such as rue, chamomile,  Arabian gum, cassia wood, and St. John's wort (or goldenrod). All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that over the fire.
And then place a reed cannula in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's womb (or inner cavity).
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it is not expelled, then you instruct the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or mallow poultice, and then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it. And when it has been found, then take it and extract it little by little. But if it is attached to the womb of the mother, then she should insert her hand as we have described, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it little by little as recommended, so that the mother does not collapse from the vehement traction. Furthermore, it should be moved to the right and left sides easily.
And if the mother's mouth (cervix) is constricted, we have already recommended treatment with sneezing and with herbal oil medication.
But if it is not expelled by all the methods we have recommended, then be careful not to tire yourself with repeated attempts at extraction. However, what has come out at the mother's mouth (cervix) should be tied to the woman's thigh, and then cauterize it with ointment and tetrafarmaco; for this will cause it to fester over a few days and it will dissolve and be expelled.
But when it festers, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then fumigations suitable for this should be administered.
But some of the Ancients already tested fumigation with hellebore and dried figs.
Method of instrumentation by which a woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and the afterbirth:
Let it be made of glass or bronze.
And let the thin end be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation be placed on the embers of the fire.
And let her hold that until the fumigation is consumed and then another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are very narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that narrow passage with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
And then place on it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then it should be treated with what we have said about the treatment and the lead [presumably referring to lead-based ointments].
Of the treatment of fistulas that develop below. (or "Of the treatment of fistulas which appear lower down.")
The fistulas that develop in the lowest part below are knotty and thick, which develop near the anus externally or in the space between the parts.
And it is one fistula or several.
And since that knot is old and discharges a watery white humidity or subtle pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because of what comes out of them through discharge and flatulence; and sometimes the patient suffers from discharge, and worms easily come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the space, into the bladder, and into the urethra; and some penetrate into the hip joint and into the buttock.
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating into the anus from one that is not penetrating, is that you put your finger inside the anus, and insert a thin probe of bronze or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor does anything come out of the opening, neither discharge, nor worms, nor flatulence, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then turn your attention to its treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the urethra, then its sign is the presence of urine in it, and it is forbidden to use that place for anything until the area is healed with medicine.
But if it penetrates into the joint of the hip or into the bone and causes pain in the surrounding area, then its sign is that the probe penetrates as far as the bone if there is no tortuosity in the bone, and pain occurs in the surrounding area; and that it always produces suppuration; and that no medication works, nor does it heal with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the treatment of these is the work and vanity of foolish physicians, on which it is founded.
But in that which is not penetrating and not chronic, healing with iron is hoped for, according to what I will recommend to you, and this I have already proven.
And let the patient lie between your hands on his back and raise his legs upwards, so that his thighs are inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe reaches.
And if the patient feels pain around the anus, then you should insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe itself penetrating through your finger, beyond what you feel between your finger and that abscess or flesh, then you know for certain that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, for there is no healing in it, as we have said.
However, some say that it heals in some men slowly.
And the medication by which help is expected for him is that you ignite a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been previously described, and insert that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those fleshy, fistulated parts resembling a bird's feather quill are cauterized.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen until all the cauterized flesh sloughs away, and then treat it with flesh-generating and consolidating medicines, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it will not heal at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate beyond your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or abscess, and sometimes the fistula is in such a way that it is only superficial at the beginning, then dry the beginning of the fistula, and you will see it drying with the probe, and it will be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the end of the probe arrives, and through the probe it is freed and cauterized.
And then cleanse those fistulated tissues which resemble a bird's quill and leave no trace of them behind.
But if a flow of blood sometimes comes between you and your operation when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and lightest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire achieves two good things: that is the cauterization of those affected tissues, and the stoppage of bleeding and drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed fabric until the place is free of pus, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate firm flesh.
And these are medicines in which there is stypticity and desiccation; when then it is restored, it is healed and we do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the rectum and is far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and examine it with that.
And so if you feel the fistula and between you and it there is a veil of fat or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation there except by way of trust and hope.
Which requires administering one of three methods: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that you can introduce the poultices and the cure is near; and do not proceed much with incision so that you do not cut the muscle containing the rectum, and that happens to the patient thus without their will; and then treat it with what we have said because it will heal very quickly; or lengthen that veil and clean that pus until you dry it near the rectum, as we have said, either with a probe or with another instrument with a sharp end, and then cleanse those fistulous tissues which are in the section where you can with cauterization; and then strive to make the restoration of the whole wound with incision, and if the orifice above is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the rectum, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the rectum near the surface of the body with the curvature and in the margin of the rectum, then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle for preparing hides in this shape:
and insert it into that fistula wrapped in five threads or around them; and then insert a probe with thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it penetrates into the margin of the rectum more inwards, then you pull the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the rectum; and pull out the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for a day or two, and when you incise the thread more into the flesh, tighten it well so that the tissues are incised which are between the two ends of the thread and that they fall.
And then treat the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, except that it is very deep, because it is not for you to do this so that you do not incise the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The form of the pointed spatula with which the fistula is opened: its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not incise what is unnecessary, as sometimes happens.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows during their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for either they are between the buttocks, similar to charcoal, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of these, there are small and large ones, and blood constantly flows from them, and there is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are, according to the majority, of little humidity, from which a citrine liquid or a little blood flows continuously, and their color is according to the constitution.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and strain until the anus opens and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you should grasp and lift them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, and then incise them at their roots.
But if the hooks cannot hold them because of their humidity and softness, then grasp them with a rough cloth or cotton or a pledget of wool, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle some hot powders on them near the incision, so that they act in place of cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatments until they are healed.
And if the anus does not yield to opening, then  stimulate the patient with a small, mildly irritating enema, and wash it with that water which is in the anus and which will gently cause it to open when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and you should grasp them with your fingernail, or grasp them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, you must suture as follows: take waxed thread and insert it into a needle and then pull the hemorrhoids together at the upper ends, and make the needle penetrate the root on the other side, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, and it should be laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong suture, and then tie the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one of them unsutured, so that the excess blood may flow from it.
And then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place that on the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointment or other treatment until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then you must scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are moist and the crust above is removed, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are healed.
Then remove them with more forceful scraping than the first time, until they bleed a lot and are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that *alamermar*, that is, a nail (ingrown toenail), is nothing but a red nodule according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a clove, and it is found throughout the body, and especially in the parts below the feet and in the fingers, and because of these, pain occurs in the area.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the nail be incised, and held with forceps or a hook, and be eradicated from its root; and then treat it.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was described in its chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round eminence on the surface of the body, and it often appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is to excise and incise it, or to bind it tightly with a thread or a hair so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine (caustic).
But a mole is a small, rough or fistulated, large eminence on the surface of the body, growing mostly in the hair, and when friction encounters it, it is felt like an ant bite, and it is found all over the body.
And its cure is that you take a cannula of goose quill or vulture feather, and let it be strong, and place it over the mole so that the circle of the cannula contains the parts of it; and then rotate your hand with the cannula so that you incise what is in the circumference of the mole, and place it in the depth of the flesh, and eradicate the mole from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a brass or iron cannula according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn out to a thinness, blunt (that is, not perforated), wrapped so that the pressure on it may be lessened and its rotation facilitated.
And if you wish, cauterize the mole as previously described in the cauterization of a boil in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the instruments that are used to administer the chrism (or, more generally,  ointments/medicaments).
Sometimes chrism is made of silver, or lead, or molten or hammered brass; and sometimes it is made of these instruments, small and large, according to those to whom it is administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose buttocks are fragile or painful, the instruments for them should be small.
The form of a large and a small instrument (cautery):
The head of the cannula above, to which the pouch is tied, should be wide in this manner; and there should be a distinction where the pouch is tied, as you see.
And the lower extremity of it, which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt (that is, not open) and very smooth, tending towards subtlety; and on one of its sides there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the width of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays and a little larger.
And let the pouch, in which the medicines are placed, be of animal bladder or sheepskin, and its length should be one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes as it is rolled up; and let the space which is between the holes be the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong threads of raw silk, about ten threads, or thereabouts; and attach the pouch with this; and when the pouch is placed in position, tie it tightly at the head of the clyster with two distinct strong ligatures, and then cauterize each hole with this.  But the method of cauterizing with this is that you fill the pouch with medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then let the patient lie supine on his back, and raise his legs and place under him a cushion upon which are placed men or a sheet; and then anoint the extremity of the clyster with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or the like; and then insert it into the anus easily, and then the attendant should take the pouch and squeeze it with both hands and with force until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the clyster and the patient lowers his legs.
And if the medicine forces him thus, let him keep it as long as he can; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
But if you apply this clyster in cases of diarrhea and the evacuation causes pain, then you repeat the clyster until he is cured.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very thin and soft, of the kind with which infants are given clysters.
Concerning the cure of wounds.
I have already said in the division on the cure of wounds what was appropriate to the book, and I say thus the cure of those with hand and with medicines according to what is fitting.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what wounds are made with and according to the place upon which the wounds fall.
And the causes by which wounds are often made are thus:  a stone throw, or an incision from a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar things of many causes.
But the wounds that are in the locations of the body are thus: a wound which falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the thigh, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the cure of certain wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or something similar, and it has only grazed the scalp and is a large wound, and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then you must take care and immediately phlebotomize the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and extract blood from it according to the patient's strength, unless some reason prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has issued from the wound is slight.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and from heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his diet be reduced, and let him not approach meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs in it, a lint cloth soaked in plain rose oil or in wine in which a styptic has been dissolved.
But if it is certain that a hot abscess will form, then place on the wound, if it is fresh, its own blood and the powder which I shall shortly mention, before the air has altered it.
And if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is caused by a sword or similar weapon, and its edges are not joined together, then join those edges with sutures, according to the method of suturing that I shall describe.
But if the wound has exposed the bone and hangs, and there is not much tissue holding it but only a small ligament, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in the place of the bone, and instruct the patient to eat foods that are thick and easily digestible, such as broth and similar foods until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood might occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you search for the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you incise it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity compels you to do what we have said, then you cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is slight; therefore you sprinkle this powder on them, for it consolidates them before the air alters them.
And the manner of making the powder is this: take one part olive oil, two parts dragon's blood, three parts quicklime; let it all be finely ground and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and let it be tightly bound until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with bandages and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is like this, unless it becomes inflamed, for the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes only heat does this, when you do not have olive oil and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary that it be applied to them and they are closed, and they are recent wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially if it is in summer, then this powder is not suitable, but rather treat those wounds with what we have said, so that you apply some digestive ointments to them, or that you apply powder made from barley flour with water and honey until they produce pus; and then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then you extract it with forceps.
And I will say the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a simple wound of the head.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are cut, then if it is a nerve, there is no other remedy than to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as vitriol and similar substances, because they are harmful to nerves and contract them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and their continuity is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is cured with mild medicines, such as washing the wound with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or plain oil, and applying a poultice after washing with fresh water and other softening herbs in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or draw together its lips, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep, or pus has accumulated in its lowest part, then perforate it at the lowest point.
But if an artery has been incised in the wound and bleeding ensues, then separate or dry it, or tie it, or cauterize it if necessity compels you to do so.
But if the vertebrae of the neck have been incised, or some of them, and the veins of the neck are unharmed, then you should join the edges of the wound with sutures above the spinal column and do not touch that; first equalize it and restore it to its natural shape, and then bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for a few days; and then treat it with ointments which have styptic and healing properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has damaged the bone, then clean it, and if there are bone fragments in it, extract all those that you can; and what you cannot manage, leave it until the wound suppurates, and is separated, and the inflammation is reduced.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle on it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that which will be recalled in its chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if there is a discharge of air from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is recent with its own blood, then you place on it the powder at the first hour, and do not bind it tightly so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but place on it attractive ointment, and unbind it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not at hand, then you place old linen on the opening of the wound so that it draws out that which is of the humors; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that that which is in it flows out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and it has not caused the patient spasms or tremors of the heart or constriction of the throat, and you see his other good dispositions, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with cauterization and with other treatment until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore treat that according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a cut by a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it with what is prescribed for stitching, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has caused damage to the bone, and pieces have been cut from it, then you cleanse the wound and try to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound is suppurated, so that their extraction is facilitated.
And for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the treatment is the same as what we have said.
Of wounds of the abdomen.
The indisposition which happens in the belly is either great, or small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, and it is such that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not take care of their replacement immediately, because it opens and swells, wherefore their insertion is made difficult.
Therefore the best rupture is moderate, because in that one the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in the two species previously mentioned.
And know that the suturing of the belly is according to four methods, but two sutures are common which are suitable for the suturing of other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures proper [to the belly] communicate with other wounds, but however they are more proper in the suturing of the belly.
And I recount to you each one with exposition and explanation.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has come out through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed by the coldness of the air.
And therefore when this is so, then it is necessary that you warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or that you boil it in water with squill, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with it until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it is done with something that has astringency, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And so when you see the inflammation to be resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marshmallow have been boiled, for by this the replacement of it is eased with little difficulty.
But if the replacement of it is hindered despite this treatment, then incise the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is proper that there is no incision, and there is no need to dry it because of necessity and weakness.
Of the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part not-sharp, and the end subtle, and not with the subtlety of a spatula, rather somewhat blunt.
And so when the wound is enlarged and the intestine is outside, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form, in its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and is in the lower part of the belly, then it is necessary that you make the patient lie on his back, and raise his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you raise his head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the belly, then always keep your attention and consideration on this, so that the part in which the wound is, is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, apply what you have prepared, and then have the assistant place his gentle hands between your hands, who will hold the entire rupture with his hand and adjust its lips, and then uncover from that side to that side so that the suture is somewhat more certain.
And this is the common method of suture: first, take the needle, or several needles according to the size of the wound; then leave the needle's end from the ring finger of the wound, and pull the needle, making sure not to put thread in the two ends of the stitch, with two ends of the thread, which are under the stitch of the inner parts until you make it penetrate that part; and you will already have added the two ends of the stitch and the two ends of the thread, and that makes four rows [or layers].
Then tighten with double thread in the circle of the needle, and sometimes on both sides together, and then bring together the two lips of the wound by decent approximation; and then leave the width of a finger and insert another needle there, and then tie that one with thread as you tied the first needle; and do not cease doing this with the needles if necessary until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And let the needles be of medium size between thickness and thinness, for thin needles very easily lacerate the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the stitch, and therefore they should be of medium thickness and thinness.
And it is not fitting that the needle be inserted too deeply into the two ends of the stitch, so that the flesh is not lightly lacerated and the wound opens before its consolidation.
And the sutures should not be too long, lest the wound be hindered from healing.
Then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient during their sleep; and you will place over the suture finely powdered herbs from all parts, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave those there until you know that the wound is healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient in small wounds, because in their suturing one or two needles or thereabouts suffice.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you sew together the four ends, of the wound and two ends of the lip, at once, with a needle around which is wrapped thread of equal fineness and thickness.
Then, when with the needle you have penetrated these four ends, return the needle to the same place from which you began, so that the drawn thread falls from the highest part to the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth suture with which cloths are sewn together.
And place between each suture the width of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture I sewed up a wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than a palm, and two palms of intestine had come out from the middle of the wound, and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he is healed; and afterwards he lived for many years, and is preserved in his faculties.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle, for I did not cure him with ointment, because it was in a place where ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I placed on the wound old linen twice a day and assiduous washing with honey water until he was healed.
But I will describe two kinds of sutures proper, according to the account of Paraula (or possibly a name similar to that).
And it is that you begin with the suture of the gut, and introduce the needle from the outside to the inside.
And then, as the needle penetrates the gut and that same laceration, according to the width and length of the whole body, leave the end of the thread in which you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inside to the outside at the other end of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again in that same end of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is on this side, and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inside to the outside.
And make it penetrate with that penetration with which you make it penetrate the thread at the end of the wound which is on his side until all of it penetrates.
And then begin on that same side and sew it with the end of the thread on the other side, and extract the needle from the gut which it penetrated, and then return it to that gut and sew the end of the thread which is on the other side with this end of the wound; and extract it from the gut which is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this once and then again until you sew up all that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a skin suture, with which you sew skins, so that you sew the gut once from the side of the end of the gut, and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.
But the second proper kind of suture, which I call, say, "Gualia," and this is spoken of by him and recounted by him, is that you sew it according to the resemblance with which some physicians sew it, so that you join each part to its corresponding part conveniently by nature; and apply the end of one side and the end of the wound to the other end of the side, and this is done as I have recounted to you.
So that you place the needle in the end of the wound closest to you on the outside part, and make it penetrate the inside part in that sole place, and leave two ends of the intestine; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside in the two ends of the intestine.
And then turn each of those, and make each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part in the other two ends of the wound which are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common, light suture, and it is the suture of which I have previously made mention.
This is the discourse of Gualia.
And moreover, some say the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is, that you insert into each of these a thread, and begin with the insertion of the needle from the side that is towards you, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew hearts similarly.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the belly.
But the judgment of the medication of a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
The reason is that when you cleanse a fresh wound with its own blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestine and sew it and dress it properly, then place incarnative powder on it.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then place on it some ointments that are at your disposal until they form a hard scab and the threads fall out and are consolidated with cyprus and myrrh; and then treat it as the treatment of other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have those medicines at hand, then immediately apply, as soon as it begins to suppurate, old lint, and change it twice a day, as you have been taught, until it heals, for you will not need other medication in many situations when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will spread infection to the principal members, then you should submerge lion wool in good quality tempered oil, or in rose oil, and place it in the area around the armpits and under the arms.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in the intestines, for this often happens, then cauterize it with warm black astringent wine, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and has made a penetrating exit into its cavity.
And know that a wound in the large intestine is easier to heal than one in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive complete healing from a wound; and this is due to the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of their size within it, and because of the subtlety of its substance, and because of its proximity to the nature of the nerves.
But if what comes from the wound is the web [likely referring to the omentum or similar tissue], and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your skill with which you reduce the intestines similarly.
But if it has passed into the space and has already turned green or black, then bind it tightly with a bandage over the blackened area, so that bleeding occurs, because there are veins and arteries in that web [tissue].
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and let two ends of the bandage hang down from the part below the wound, so that the traction of the [injured] tissue towards the falling of the web and emission of the arm of the wound may be relieved from you.
A recollection/reminder of wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it can be restored in some men, because I saw one who had been wounded in his belly by a lance thrust, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and had formed a fistula, and from it issued gas and flatulence.
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his recovery, but I continued to treat him until he was healed and the place was consolidated/healed.
And I, after I saw the place consolidated, feared for the patient that some accident might befall him in his belly; and no such accident occurred, and he resumed his usual diet of eating and drinking, and then entered a bath and exercised himself according to his abilities.
And some experienced men have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be bound in this manner: that you take ants with large heads; and then adjust the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants, its mouth open, over the two lips of the wound; then when it bites those and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not dissolve.
And then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease to do this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the whole wound.
And then reduce and sew the wound; and let those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and let no harm befall the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be sewn with fine thread which is made from the intestine of an animal, passed through a needle; and it is that you take the end of these intestinal threads, and let it be well scraped, and then tie the end of it with the thread, wrapped, and then insert that thread into needles and sew with that the intestine, and reduce that into the disruption.
And this method of suturing with ants is only according to a way of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, because then it is only a reduction, and not a complete healing of it.
Of the treatment of a fistula and fistula.  (Note:  The repetition suggests a distinction might be intended, perhaps different types of fistula, or fistula and a related condition).
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus constantly and is not excised, then, in whatever member it may be, is universally called a fistula, and we call that a fistula (or zukem).  (The repetition and slight difference in spelling suggests a possible dialectal variation or a synonym.)
And a fistula, according to truth, is a hardened, white nodule, with which there is no pain, and which has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and for this reason it is called a claw-like ulcer, that is, a claw.
And in some men it is moist, which discharges pus; and quickly the moisture is excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and is little, and is coarse, and is subtle. (This describes the variability of the discharge.)
I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of them and their treatment with medicines in the division (of the treatise).
And it is necessary that I make known to you the treatment of a fistula, which is done by means of a surgical operation with the hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body; for there are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the joints composed by composition, such as those of the hand and foot; and those that affect the proximity of the main member and its similar parts.
Therefore, that one of these fistulas, which is according to this manner, is among the illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive treatment, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
And if it is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its healing, and want to reach its knowledge, then take a probe of bronze or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, and search for it with that.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a subtle lead probe, because lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily bent along the tortuous path.
And if the fistula has very horrific contents, it is not possible for you to examine it with a probe.
