Then, cleanse the orifices through its orifices, because the moisture by which you cleanse it goes through the other orifices and cleanses them.
And then inquire carefully, in whatever way possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves here, and if the depth of the fistula is far or near, or is it a fistula which has many orifices and is situated there; do all this with the utmost of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your compression with the hand on the area, and similar signs and accidental causes to the abscess.
And when you have ascertained all these things with true judgment, then proceed to the treatment with confidence: and it is that you consider then if the fistula is close at hand or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you.
Then you dry the fistula as previously described, and remove what is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and that which has grown in the orifice of the flesh, or of cartilage and the like, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it in the front as much as is possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; and then apply cauteries wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce them into the depth of the fistula, which you have grasped with an instrument.
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not prohibit any cause from a vein or nerve or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with that which will cause it to grow flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not reached the total eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again and meticulously scrape it and cleanse it with all your strength, and then restore it.
And so if it is healed, good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which has not been reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to pull it out, then pull it out with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then meticulously remove all of them, and do not leave any behind with your strength.
But if some of the moisture causes you difficulty, and there is no fear or mistake in their incision, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then apply something to the wound that will fester the wound, and leave it until it has reached the area around those bones of flesh and ligaments, and keep the wound from healing, and keep the incision you made during the operation constricted.
Having said that, if you are afraid, place on it a cloth soaked in crushed rue with oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, for when those ligaments fester, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction is eased.
But if it is a large bone, such as a leg bone or thigh bone or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp only from behind until the blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and incise everything up to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it heals.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh so that you may use this as an example and help in your operation.
There was a young man, about thirty years old, who experienced pain in his thigh, and from the inner parts, pus rose to the surface within the body until the pus reached the thigh; it was a matter of an abscess with a large swelling, and there was no external cause for it.
And then time was wasted with it because of the doctors' mistakes, until the abscess was opened, and much matter flowed from it. Its treatment was then delayed until the leg was fistulated, and many orifices were made in it, all discharging pus and the body's humors.
And then many doctors treated him for about two years, and none of them were skillful in the art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those orifices in great quantity; and the man's body was quite emaciated, and his complexion was jaundiced.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and the probe reached the bone.
And then I quickly examined all the orifices and found that they reached to all parts of the leg.
And then I carefully incised one of those orifices until I uncovered a part of the bone, and I found it corrupted, and it was already rotten and corrupt and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted, to the extent that it had penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sawed away what was exposed and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I judged that in the bone there was only that corruption which I had excised and sawed, and I had already removed it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicine for a long time, and it is not healed.
And then I again incised and uncovered the bone a second time over the first uncovering, and I found continuous corruption in the bone.
And I sawed away what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was frustrated in making its cure and restoration, and it is not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and did not cease to incise the bone little by little, piece by piece, and I forced its restoration, and it was not restored until I had incised about a palm's breadth of it, and that I extracted with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicines, and it is lightly consolidated and healed.
And it is not necessary to iterate the operation on it and sectioning of it except for the disposition of its fragility and for the scarcity of its tolerance and its fear of death, because syncope afflicted him at all hours due to superfluity of evacuation.
And therefore he is healed by a complete healing, and there is nothing in place of the hard bone flesh; and its disposition in his body is made good and its strength has returned; and he is restored to his dispositions and experienced no impediment in walking nor harm in any matter.
But if it is a prominent bone in place of the flesh already removed, then it is necessary that you cut that according to this manner: and that is that you take a ligature and bind that to the extremity of that prominent bone, and command someone to extend that upwards; and place that woolen ligature thicker than the first ligature; and then tie that over the flesh that is beneath the bone, and extend the extremity of it so that you draw the flesh to the parts below, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board decently beneath the bone, for when you do this, it is not forbidden that the saw should not cut the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be done over the corrupt place little by little, because if the corruption is in the concavity of the bone and does not appear in its outward appearance to the senses, then force the sawing of it another time.
And if the bone is corrupt and not prominent, but one part is continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or in part of it, then you uncover the flesh on all sides completely; and then place wood underneath, and then saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the location of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupt joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no reason for it but ingenuity/skill.
And if the corruption is in the bone of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, you must investigate the corruption in whatever way it appears to you, and you must open it, and clean it according to any disposition which is possible for you, and with each tool it should be rectified for you, when it does not harm you by vein or nerve.
And know that the incisors and saws for incising these bones are many, according to the location of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and their subtlety and size and smallness, and their hardness and rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument convenient for that operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the type of instrument of which you should make use; for with great study and long experience in the methods of this art, and witness of many illnesses, you will then have for yourself what is suitable in instruments for every illness.
And I am giving you in the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will keep as simple tools with which you will proceed to your treatment which you will test against others. Form of saw:

Concerning the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
Extremities may suffer [from corruption] due to intrinsic or extrinsic causes. If, after having treated the corruption with medicines, the treatment is ineffective, or if you see the corruption spreading through the limb without any apparent cause, then you must incise that limb up to the point where the corruption reaches, so that the patient escapes death or the greater harm of limb loss.
And the sign of this [condition] affecting someone is that the limb turns black as if fire had burned it, or the blackness spreads until the corruption reaches the limb and spreads throughout the body; therefore, treat it with incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the impact of some poisonous thing, such as a sea scorpion, or a viper, or a shrew, and the like.
And if the corruption or injury is in the extremity of a finger, then you should incise the finger and not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.
And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you should incise it from the base of the hand, and not allow the corruption to spread to the forearm.
And if it affects the arm, then incise the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that case the patient's death is certain; and to administer any other treatment beyond that is a waste of effort.
And similarly, for the foot: when corruption affects the toe, then you should incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the heel of the foot, then you should incise the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you should incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy for him but resignation, and to leave the patient to die.
And the manner of incising or amputating a limb is that you should tighten a ligature on the lowest part that you wish to incise, and tighten another ligature above the location; and let an assistant extend a ligature to the lower parts, and let another assistant extend a ligature above; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures, thus spreading it open until all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be incised or amputated.
And it is necessary that a linen cloth be placed on all parts so that it does not touch the healthy area, and that the patient may receive a suitable dressing and hot poultice.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while operating, then carefully cauterize the affected area, or apply some blood-stanching powders; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot from a burn that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption first occurred in his toe until it affected the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, because he saw the affliction spreading through the limb with such vehemence that he was overcome by pain and burning, and he incised that foot up to the joint, and it healed.
A long time later, the same corruption occurred in the finger of his index hand (pointing finger).
And then he came to me, and I tried to repel that excess with what I applied to his hand in the form of medicines, to cleanse his body; and the excess was not repelled and began to spread to another finger until it affected the whole hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, but I did not want to do so, hoping for the repulsion of that excess, and even more fearing the incision of his hand might prove fatal, because the patient's strength was failing.
And after he left me, he returned to his land, and word reached me that that man prepared and incised his whole hand, and he was healed.
And I have included this account so that it may be helpful in cases of this illness, and that it may serve as a guide on how to understand and treat it.
Concerning the treatment of sinuses and the nature of clyster administration with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in fleshy members and the space of the abscess is extended until it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken open and perforated, and the pus flows out, leaving a space empty like a vessel, and the skin covering it is like a thin cloth, and it has not reached a state of further corruption, nor does it affect the bone, nerve, or ligament, it is called a sinus, and not a fistula.
However, if the space is extended so that the corruption affects any member, then it is called a fistula, and not a sinus.
And its treatment is to incise that skin completely, just as it is swollen, especially when it is thinned and made like cloth, and ensure that it does not adhere to the location due to its corruption.
But if you suspect that the skin will adhere and that the corruption has not progressed further, and there is a thinning of the skin, then you treat that with a clyster.
And you consider the sinus, and if it is large, and the pus has a foul odor, then you should clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine which is made of oil, sour wine, and resin, taking equal parts, and these should be mixed in a vessel and heated over a fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
Then take what is necessary from this and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with this, and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then extract that with a syringe; and do this for as many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with ash water, when this medicine is not available to you; and you should take the ashes of vines, and put water on them; and then strain this and clysterize with it until you are satisfied that the sinus is clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then clysterize with water and honey in which crushed resin is mixed, and clysterize with wine and honey mixed, for it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes it adhere to the sinus, especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are satisfied that the sinus is clean, then clysterize it with something that will cause the flesh to grow there.
And you should take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or clysterize with other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the orifice of the sinus is narrow, so that the clyster nozzle cannot be inserted, then gradually widen it with a small iron instrument, and place a [small, heated] probe wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment inside until it is widened.
And similarly, if the orifice is wide, then bring its lips together with sutures and leave a space into which the clyster can enter without difficulty.
And if the orifice from which the pus flows is located higher up, then drain it at the lowest point so that the pus flows downwards, because when the pus is trapped deep within the sinus, the flesh will not grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to drain the sinus into the lowest possible point as desired, then make sure you prepare the limb with a preparation which allows the pus to flow easily, as is within your ability, ensuring that nothing obstructs the drainage of pus.
But the cloth that is over the opening of the sinus, change that every hour so that the pus that collects there can drain out. And so that you may know if the pus that flows from it is much or little, or if it is digested or undigested.
And with this, you should examine the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment continues in this manner, and you see that the matter discharged onto the opening of the sinus has dried, or is about to dry, then on the next day or the third day, loosen it; and when you loosen the dressing, change the cloth to another and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus nearby, a subtle virus emerges, then do not worry about its adherence, for its healing is approaching, because there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and others in whom the opposite occurs.
And if from the sinus, for many days, undigested pus has emerged, then know that the sinus will not close; and if the closing of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust the drying properties of the medicines; and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their effect, use dry ones, such as diachylon ointment,  when there is an excess of added moisture.
And the best remedy with which it is cured is to take diachylon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and add to it old, well-aged wine equal in quantity to its substance, and carefully mix it, and use this.
And it acts similarly in that case, when there is no pus: diachylon ointment, honey cooked until thickened, and use this; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, grind everything and powder it over the honey while it is on the fire, and then apply this to a cloth and bind it over the sinus; or apply it with the honey that you have cooked until it is thickened, and powder it on a cloth and bind it on.
And sometimes, aristolochia longa, roots of lily of the valley (it is lily of the valley), and flour of barley and centaury are administered with it; use these simples or compounded, grind them, sieve them, and powder them over the honey which has been mentioned; and use this.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the bone, and it has spread to the bones or to other hard parts or ligaments; then you treat it with fistular treatment, as described above.
Of the treatment of paronychia, and of the treatment of a worn nail, and of the addition of a digit incision, and of its section.
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of a finger of the hand or foot, and easily spreads to other fingers; and as it is prolonged and its treatment is less valued, it becomes an abscess of hot pus, or it is corrupted, and pus forms until it corrodes the root of the nail, and easily corrupts the whole of it, and easily its corruption penetrates to the bone until there is a foul odour, and the extremity of the finger becomes swollen, and its colour becomes dark.
Therefore, when you have this with what we have said in the division and your treatment is not effective, then it is necessary that you incise with an iron all the superfluous matter that remains of the nail and immediately cauterize the wound there, because cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail, empty already, compress the flesh at the underlying parts, and begin to prick and hurt it, then it is necessary that you place a thin probe under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and push it up and incise that flesh easily, and place upon what remains of the flesh corrosive and cauterizing medicines until it is all consumed; and then treat this with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption already penetrates the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and extract it, for a bone that is corrupted will never heal.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption spreading into the finger, then you incise it at one of the joints, according to what has been previously mentioned, and immediately treat it until it is healed.
But if the nail suffers from percussion or friction, and there is intense pain in it, then you must first phlebotomize the patient, and immediately cut the nail with a sharp instrument with a transverse incision from above downwards, and take care that the incision does not reach the flesh that is under the nail, for you will cause the patient intense pain, and it will be the cause of the generation of superfluous flesh in the area; and then treat this area with something that relieves pain.
But a finger that is born in the hands of some men is very quickly fleshy all over, and very quickly bony, or very quickly grows a nail.
And some of them are born at the root of a joint, or their growth is in some of the finger joints. But that which is born in some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born in the joints is very quickly painful.
And of those that are born fleshy, the incision is easy, and it is that you incise that one below its root with a broad instrument.
But of that which is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its excision.
But in that which is born on a finger between the joints, it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a circular incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then treat it until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between their joints, whether by the birth of a man thus born, or by the consolidation of a wound, or by burning with fire, or similarly, then it is necessary that you cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not lightly consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as necessary.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you cut that consolidation according to what is prepared and suitable to the shape of the member.
Concerning the incision of vices, these are varices (varicose veins).
Varices are veins, and they are twisted, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and most commonly in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and porters.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
But the treatment of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be raised and completely extracted.
The incision is done in this manner: first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick, feculent blood of the leg is resolved, and then bind the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh to the part below the knee with a bandage, and then incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision, and then raise the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from above downwards, until the quantity of blood you wish to obtain has been released,  a quantity which will remove the patient's malady.
Then bind it up, and instruct him to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But the elevation of them is according to this manner: you who raise the patient's leg, if there is much hair on it, then place him in a bath and bathe his leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are distended; or, if a bath is not available to you, have him perform vigorous exercise until the limb is warmed.
Then incise the vein along its length, either behind the knee, or at the lowest point below the ankle.
Then open the vein with an instrument and scrape it on all sides until it feels sensitive, and it is from its appearance that one sees that redness tending to blackness.
And then, when you have opened the vein, you see that white which is like a cord.
Then you place a leech below it so that it is elevated and is drawn to the vein, and suspend it with a golden hook.
Then make another incision near that incision, three fingers' breadth away, and scrape the vein from the part above the vein until it appears.
Then elevate it and incise it at the back section near the heel, and then extract it and elevate it until it comes out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until it is all extracted; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you to be extracted and elevated, then introduce a strong double-threaded needle and tie it and extract it, or introduce a leech below it and rotate your hand on all sides until it is drawn out. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is made difficult for you to elevate it and it becomes harmful to the patient through that damage.
And when it is all expelled, then place on the wound site wool soaked in rose wine and olive oil, both unmixed and mixed, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having a tortuosity in which there is a wrapping around the parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you incise it at each part of its tortuosity and at the places of its wrapping, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it entirely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not made as sharp as the hooks, nor is it of a sharp extremity, so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of a thick flexibility, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein by its thinness; therefore it has superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little humidity, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And it is quickly generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, scabies, and maggots are generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the onset of this vein is that a vehement inflammation occurs in the leg; and then the area is blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, which resembles the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachm to two drachms, and bind it, and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as any part of it emerges outwards, wrap it in lead and bind it. And if the growth is prolonged, then you should incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise its root until it has emerged completely, because if you incise it, it is retracted and enters the flesh, causing an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled completely and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein it has emerged in some men to a length of five palms and fingers, and it has already happened to me that one of twenty palms emerged.
And if it is incised during its treatment, then insert a probe into its opening and perforate it with a long hole into the body, until all that is in it is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the place for some days with medicines; and then treat it according to the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices appear in it, and a branch emerges from each orifice.
And therefore treat it as we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Concerning the incision (or cutting) above worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine sickness (or disease).
This illness is called in some lands near us bovine illness because it often happens to cows, and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and it goes throughout the body, ascending and descending, and is shown by the feeling of its movement from limb to limb until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is by the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, snakes, earthworms, and ascarids happen in the belly (abdomen).
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it goes in the body and is elevated to the head, and has reached the eye, then very quickly it enters it and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure this and extract it, then it is not done except by its movement and appearance to the feeling (sensation), and then it is necessary that you tightly bind what is above it and what is below it with a strong bandage and constriction, and then cut over it and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you place a cautery with fire over the place until it dies, and the greatest feared cause is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see it has reached the head near the eye, then bind above the eyebrow with a strong constriction, and then cut over it and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use purification of his body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and guard himself from foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the illness nafir (or a similar ailment).
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in certain limbs, and then is moved from limb to limb.
And I have already seen that, as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small swelling in the basilic vein of the arm, and after it had remained for an hour, I saw that swelling moving as if a worm were climbing upwards, but more lightly than a worm, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and remained there for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then it occurred to me that it was moving throughout her body, as I saw; and I was greatly amazed at its movement and its speed from limb to limb. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb. But I never saw it with my own eyes as in the woman.
And I did not think it was anything other than that because the woman was old, with a dry body and exposed veins, and in this [condition] that windy movement appeared.
And it is necessary that it not appear, according to this treatment, in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on it lightly and dry it until that constricted wind is released, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, then you treat it with bodily exercise and with things that expel winds, and drive them out with fetid pills and with pills of serapium and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they land, for their diversity is such that there are those with large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and those that have three angles, and those that have barbs; and those that have feathers.
And of the signs of the brain, it is when an arrow falls into it and penetrates the bone, and emerges from the cranium which is over the brain, for it causes sudden vehement pain, and scotoma (blind spots), and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of understanding, and vomiting of bile, and the speech is quickly cut off and the voice is destroyed, and a white humidity resembling pus emerges from the place of the wound, and pus from it similar to the watery discharge of flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, are that it is near the left nipple, and one feels as if it were stuck in something hard, and not in something empty; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope; and therefore know that death follows without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are thus:  frothy blood emerges from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are swollen, and the color of the patient is changed, and he breathes with great difficulty, and desires the intake of cool air.
And if the arrow falls into the diaphragm, which is in the chest, the pain is then near the lower ribs, and great swelling is produced with vehement pain and with sighing, and not all the muscles of the limbs are affected.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes out of the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the abdomen, and is stuck in it, there emerges from the wound some pus, or the intestine, which is ruptured, and there is no hope in its cure without extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow falls into the bladder, and urine emerges from it, and some matter proceeds from it to the external parts, and very quickly great pain comes over the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle (furcula), and the hip, and the leg, and similar members, are saved according to the best dispositions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you that I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may signify by this the treatment for yourself.
The reason is, because in a man an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye at the root of the nose, and I extracted it for him from the other side, below the lobe of his ear, and he was healed, and no harm happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which fell into him in the lobe of his eye, below the lower eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and I found only a small extremity with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with an iron head in which there were no barbs.
And the Jew was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and this is the type that has barbs; and then I incised over it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I was subtle with it, and then I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and therefore, since thirty days had passed and no change had occurred in his condition, I incised over the arrow and extracted it, and he was healed, and no harm befell him.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his back, and the wound had healed over him, and after about seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman into whom an arrow fell in her belly, and the wound healed within her, and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman found no hindrance in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his face, and the wound healed and the arrow broke, finding no great pain there, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the prince of the king's man, which had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already declined a little to the right side, and the entire arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his cure about three days after the arrow's penetration.
And then I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; and then I examined it with a fine probe, and did not feel it; but he felt a prickling under his right ear, and then I suspected that this prickling was caused by the point of the arrow.
And I plastered the area with a plaster which has attractive and digestive properties, trusting that the area would suppurate so that the location of the arrow would be revealed to me, so that I could operate on it.
And no significant event occurred at the site indicating that the arrow had reached that location; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no incident occurred; and the wound was healing meanwhile, and the patient desisted from its extraction for many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and recounted this to me.
And then I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with a probe I explored it, and found the tip of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I adjusted the application of the sharp medicine to the wound until the tip of the arrow became visibly apparent to me, and I spent time with this for a period of four months.
And then, since the wound was enlarged, and it became possible for me to insert forceps into it and remove it, and I did not succeed in this way, then I did not cease to refine and devise a method for it, until one day I managed, with ingeniously crafted instruments, to grasp it with forceps, specially made, as the form of these will be shown at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored the wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, and I restored it and the wound healed, and the patient was completely cured, and I did not harm him in any way.
And I recount to you the manner of extraction of certain arrows so that you may apply this rule and understanding to that which I have not mentioned, for the details of this art and its definitions are not fully comprehended by narration, nor are these contained in books.
And skillful artisans compare the small with the large; and that which is present with that which is absent; and they find new operations and new instruments because of the unexpected occurrence of strange causes in their art.
Therefore I say that arrows are not removed from the members into which they are embedded except in two ways: either by traction from the place where they enter; or by the opposite from the other side.
And that which is extracted from the place where it entered, is an arrow penetrating a fleshy place, and is pulled and removed.
And if it does not yield to extraction at the time of discovery, then you must leave it for some days until the flesh around it softens, and the extraction and its removal are eased.
Similarly, if it is embedded in bone and does not yield to extraction, then leave it for a few days and repeat the attempt with traction and movement each day, and it will eventually be removed.
And if it still does not yield after a few days, then you must drill around the arrow in the bone on all sides with a fine drill bit until the space around the arrow is widened, and then remove it.
But if the arrow is hidden in the body and cannot be felt, then probe for it with a probe; and if you find it, then extract it with instruments suitable for its removal.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the projectile, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then incise the wound to widen it so that the arrow can be extracted.
And if it is held by barbs, then carefully free the flesh in which it is embedded by every means you can; and work on this, if you cannot otherwise free the flesh, by breaking off the barbs and twisting them until they release.
And when you are removing the arrow from wherever it is lodged, then use twisting with your hand and forceps in every way to make it yield; and proceed with great care so that the arrow is not broken, making its removal difficult.
And if you cannot remove it immediately, then wait a few days until the surrounding tissues soften, and then try again, for it will then be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then use the methods of treatment described in the relevant chapter.
And be careful not to cut a vein, nerve, or tendon, and employ every possible method to remove the arrow; and do this with gentleness, calmness, slowness, and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And therefore, make the arrow straight so that the patient's position corresponds to the position of the arrow's entry wound, for this is most beneficial. But if this is not possible, then use whatever methods are available to you, given the circumstances.
But if the arrow is protruding from the opposite side, or if some part of it is already projecting outwards, or if the arrowhead is noticeably swollen due to the feeling of the part above the deep tendon and sometimes that prominent part; then you should cut around that; and the incision should be according to the size that the forceps can grasp; and then pull it out, for it will easily come out in this way.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then you should twist the blade against it until the arrowhead enters the bone, and widen the opening, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is still inside, then push it through.
But if the shaft has fallen off and you want to administer a push, then introduce, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that the concavity fits onto the tail of the arrow, and then push it through.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then push it through with a strong instrument entering it, for the arrowhead is relieved by it.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then you should cut away all the flesh in which the poison is, if this is possible for you, and then treat it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow has entered the foot, or the belly, or the bladder, or the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut around it, then cut around it and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and extract it, and then sew the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
Form of forceps with which arrows are pulled out:
Let the ends of them be like a bird's beak, which are made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they do not let go of it.
And of these, many types are made, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the size of the wound, and the tightness of it.
like a ray, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased and is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the customary number is thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; that is, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called alhasisain; two veins that are in the two apparent temples; and two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called aspicientz; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein that is at the tip of the nose; and two guidegi that are at the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins that are called algelic —; and there are two veins that are under the tongue. But the veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of those there is one cephalic, and it is on the wild side (of the arm); and the people call it “the vein of the head”, and it is a blind vein, and it is a median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilic, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people name that one “vein of the whole body”, and the basilic is the vein which is located on the inner side of the arm, and it is named “citillar”, and the common people name it “vein of the belly”; and the funis of the arm, and it is located above the cubital fossa, and it is that one in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the elbow by manifest appearance; and the salvatella, and it is the vein that is between the little finger and the ring finger, and it has two branches there.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of these is that which is below the bend of the knee on the outer side; and the second is the saphena, and its location is below the leg on the inner side; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the outer side; and in the leg there are other veins similar to those.
The benefit of opening the veins near the ears is for chronic catarrhs, migraines, headaches, and old, bad ulcers of the head.
And the manner of bleeding those veins is as I recount to you: that is, the patient's head should be raised, and the patient's head should be rubbed at the occiput in the location of both veins with a wide cloth with good friction; then the patient should tighten their neck with a bandage until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed places on the head; and seek them out with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those with your finger, then bleed with a lancet there.
Then you take the lancet knife, which is sharp, and you insert it under the vein in the fossa until the blood flows. Then raise your hand with the vein and fossa to the upper parts and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two joined fingers or thereabouts; and let blood flow as much as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And already and previously at the beginning of the book, the incision of these and their cauterization has been described.
But of the two arteries that are in the temples, bloodletting is helpful for chronic and sudden severe migraine, persistent ophthalmia, and a flow of acute superfluities effused into the eyes.
And the way to perform phlebotomy on these is as I recommend to you:  constrict the patient's neck tightly until the two veins appear to the touch by manifest appearance and their pulse is felt under your finger; and then you bleed with a lancet.
Then you raise the skin above the vein at the upper part with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy instrument underneath, and raise the vein up; and incise it just as you have done with the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to your need.
Then loosen the constriction of the patient, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then place cotton and powder on it and bind it tightly above with strong constriction, and leave it until it is healed.
And already the recollection of them, and their incision, and the drawing [of blood] has been preceded in the beginning of the book.
Therefore, the benefit of phlebotomy of the frontal vein is for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein in old facial diseases and for migraine and rigidity and foul ulcers.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is as I recounted to you; that is, that the patient constricts his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is named a lancet, of which this is the form:
Place the prominent point which is at the head of the lancet upon that vein and strike it with a hammer or with something else in that manner, and let the blood flow according to the quantity that you wish; and then loosen the constriction of the patient and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a lateral phlebotomy, however it is not fitting that the phlebotomy be of acute extremity as are the other phlebotomies, rather it should be of a blunt extremity, and phlebotomize it according to the transverse, because the bone is near and would easily break there the phlebotomy when it is subtle [thin].
But the benefit of the two veins that are at the lacrimals of both eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for rigidity and swelling in the eyes, and for diseases of the face.
And the manner of the phlebotomy is that the patient should constrict his neck with a bandage, and then phlebotomize those veins, and you should be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made transversely, a little lengthwise with a small lancet, because in that place there is no flesh; and if the phlebotomy is thin, very quickly its tip is broken.
And then make the blood flow as much as is necessary for you, and place cotton over it, and bind it for one night, and then unbind it.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and vehement headache, and for diseases of the face and migraine and rigidity that occurs in the face, and especially when it is chronic.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is that the patient should constrict his neck, and then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a long, thin lancet and insert it into the middle of the nasal bridge in a straight line, because the vein is not palpable there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
So that you gradually withdraw your hand with the lancet and allow the blood to flow according to your need; and then bind it for a night, because it is lightly restored.
But the help of phlebotomy of the two jugular veins is for constriction of the throat and the beginning of leprosy and many illnesses and impetigo and bad corrosive ulcers, as well as herpes.
And the manner of phlebotomy of these is that the patient should tighten each one at his neck with a bandage; and the practitioner should stand above the patient's head, and the patient should be sitting on a stool.
And then phlebotomize the vein lengthwise with a wide lancet little by little; and then extract blood according to a measured quantity or according to what you see is necessary; and then do likewise to the other vein.
And then untie the bandage and tighten the two veins with a moderate constriction so that the patient is not suffocated, and leave it until morning because the wound is healed.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the temporal veins helps small abscesses and pustules of the mouth and corruption of the gums and bad ulcers and fissures, and bad ulcers that are in the nose and around it.
And the manner of phlebotomy of these is that you make the patient sit before you, and tighten his neck with a bandage, and then roll back his lips and look for two veins, of which one is on the right of the lip and the second on the left; and the demonstration of each is by the blackness of each of both, and this is more so because in the area of those are small black veins; and then incise each one with a cutting incision.
But if you have doubt, and you do not know which is which, then you should incise the larger of those, and the most obvious of those.
And do likewise for the two veins that are in the upper lips.
But of the two veins that are under the tongue, the benefit is for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein for throat affections, for diseases of the uvula, and for diseases of the mouth.
And the manner of phlebotomizing those is that you hold the patient between your hands facing the sun, and elevate his tongue, and look under the tongue, on one side you will see a vein and on the other side another vein, and the color of each is tending towards blackness.
And then phlebotomize each one; and be careful not to overuse your hand in phlebotomizing those, because under those are arteries.
And the veins that are phlebotomized in the neck are those which run customarily in all men, and the phlebotomy of them is done in two ways: either they are opened with a lancet or olive-shaped phlebotomy instrument of fine point; or they are incised with a cupped phlebotomy instrument, and it is called an alnessil, and this is its form:
Let its width be thus as you see, and it is suitable for opening concave, full, prominent, thick veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotomy instrument is of lesser width and more subtle extremity, and is suitable for phlebotomy of subtle veins that contain thin, clear blood.
But the benefit of phlebotomy of the basilica vein, which is one of three veins: for it draws out the blood of illnesses that are below the throat and neck, and of what affects the feet and abdomen.
And it is fitting for the phlebotomist, when performing this phlebotomy, that he carefully guards against this and be very cautious, for below it is an artery, and if he errs and is imprecise in the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will cut that artery and cause a flow of blood; therefore it is necessary that he not phlebotomize it with a sharp phlebotomy instrument, but rather with a cutting instrument like a lancet.
And if the basilica vein does not appear in manifest form, then it is necessary that you leave it and that you move to another, or that you seek one of its branches; or that you phlebotomize at the end of the arm instead, for that is manifest; and incise it with a lancet-like phlebotomy instrument as we have said.
But if you wish phlebotomy in the same place, then before binding the arm, you must touch the location until you find the location of the pulse, and then mark it with ink; and then bind the arm and incise the vein with a concave incision using a lancet-like phlebotomy instrument as we have said; and be careful that the incision does not fall into the space of the artery's location.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, swelling in the place you have marked with ink, then that swelling is a swelling of the artery, and therefore avoid that place.
And if you see the blood flowing from the phlebotomy like a child's urine, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is arterial blood; and then you should observe and place your finger on it for a long time; and then remove your finger, and if the bleeding is stopped (for many times it is stopped), then you should tighten the arm and leave it.
And watch the patient for movement, and let there be concern for him, and do not annoy him for some days until he is healed.
But if the bleeding is not stopped, and you see that you do not have medicine present for this, then you should incise the artery, if it appears to you; because its extremity is constricted, and the bleeding is stopped; or that you take bark from a fig tree, and pound it, and take one half and bind it tightly over the vein's location with a descending ligature and with powders until the next day.
And then if the bleeding is stopped, it is good; and if not, then treat it with that which was previously mentioned in the recollection of the place of the powders that stop the flow of blood.
And the stopping of his blood is difficult in many situations because of the small size of the wound and the possibility of binding the arm.
But from the dark vein, phlebotomy is helpful to draw blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; for this reason, because it is composed of branches of the basilic and cephalic veins, as we have said.
And it behooves the phlebotomist to be very careful in his phlebotomy, for below that is a nerve; and if he inserts the phlebotomy lancet and finds the nerve, stupor will befall him, and its healing is difficult, and perhaps he will never be fully healed. And this nerve often appears to the senses; and if it is hidden from some men and is subtle and not shown, then it behooves you to use the phlebotomy instrument with which you perform phlebotomy with a slanting incision, and avoid the nerve with your skill.
And if the vein is between two nerves, then you should open the vein lengthwise.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and helps with eye diseases.
And it is especially fitting in this case, if you wish the phlebotomist to puncture with an olive-shaped or myrtle-shaped phlebotomy lancet; because it is the safest of all veins, for there is neither artery nor nerve below it.
However, it is very quickly inflamed in some men, when it is not phlebotomized at the first puncture, but that inflammation does no harm.
The first of these is that you should know that phlebotomy is administered only for the preservation of health and for its hope and the prevention of illness, and that phlebotomy should be performed in one of the veins that are in the elbow, that is: cephalic, median cubital, and basilic; and that phlebotomy should be performed at the beginning of spring, on an empty stomach in spring and autumn, and in winter near mealtimes, when signs of repletion appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the third day or the first, after three hours of the day have passed.
But phlebotomy, which is administered, is more when it provokes convenience and necessity for it, whether by night or by day, at any hour and at any time.
And it is necessary that a child not be phlebotomized until he is fourteen years old, and a pregnant woman should not be phlebotomized except from the fourth month to the seventh, and old women who have passed sixty years should not be phlebotomized.
And if someone wants to be phlebotomized in any way, it is necessary that he cleanse his bowels before phlebotomy with a cleansing enema, if he has much retained waste in them, so that the veins do not draw the superfluities into the phlebotomy from the bowels, which are putrid, and that they not harm the principal members.
And let not patients with nausea, thirst, and indigestion be phlebotomized, nor those recovering from illness or drunkenness until this is remedied in them, nor patients with dropsy, and moreover, phlebotomy should be avoided during colic, vomiting, and abdominal pain, and during times of excessive coitus, labor, exercise, fasting, or vigils, and all causes that weaken the strength, whether corporeal or animal.

And let the constriction be moderate, for if the constriction is uneven, or if the constriction is excessive, then it prevents the flow of blood; or if it is too loose, then it prevents the proper outflow of blood.

Then the phlebotomist should anoint his fleam with a little old oil; and then he should place the index finger of his left hand on the vein, below the place where he wants to perform the phlebotomy, so that the vein moves slightly, and the percussion is avoided, because some veins are like a fleeing cord during phlebotomy, and those that are full of wind, when you place the fleam on them, are contracted against the fleam, and the phlebotomist fails; and the fleam does not open the vein, and if it opens, it is too tight.
Therefore, the phlebotomist must be skilled and attentive to all these matters.

Then repeat the phlebotomy if necessary.
And if the opening is small and the blood flows subtly, and you fear that the desired quantity of blood will not come out, then repeat the phlebotomy in that same place, accurately and precisely adjusting the opening a little, and do this carefully before the place becomes infected, because in many men the place becomes infected from a small opening.
But if you see that it is already infected, then do not repeat it there, for no reason excuses you; and place on it some part of an olive pit, for it relieves the body of blood. And it is better in this place than oil and other oils.

And it is necessary to do this near the exit of blood from that vein, along with it or from another vein.
And if some blackness or greenness remains in the place for some days, it does not harm; and if you wish, place on it some aloes and myrrh dissolved, or some mint juice or calamint, or something similar.
And many times abscesses and swellings occur after phlebotomy of the basilica vein.

Because if the blood is black, then you let it flow until it is red; and similarly, if you see it thick, then you let it flow until it is thinned; and similarly, if it is sharp (or acrid), until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full and strong and needs bloodletting, that the phlebotomy of the vein be widened and the phlebotomy be wide. (or: ...and the incision for phlebotomy be wide.)

And the best way to administer phlebotomy is that the section (incision) be transverse, not a puncture.
And this manner of phlebotomy is safer from blood flow and nerve injury, and is more laudable and safer than phlebotomy according to latitude and longitude (i.e., a lengthwise or vertical incision).
And to one who is accustomed to fainting during phlebotomy, it is necessary that you treat him before phlebotomy with a little bread infused in warm myrtle wine or simple oxymel; and extract the blood in three or four times.

But whoever wants to bleed his arm and shed his blood a second time, and his body is strong, then let him do so about seven or nine hours after the first phlebotomy.
But whoever wants to draw blood from his body away from the part to which it declines (or flows excessively), it is necessary that he be bled on the second or third day.
But that body in which there is much already heated blood, and it is acute and causes fever, it is necessary that a large quantity of blood be shed from him at once, and the opening be enlarged until syncope occurs, provided that you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy; and that you place your hand on his pulse below the bleeding site, so that death does not result instead of syncope, for this often happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent.

And the vein called "rope of the arm" is phlebotomized instead of the cephalic or basilic, when they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them.

And if the flow of blood is impeded, then put the hand back in warm water and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your objective.
But if you are in the summer days, then you are excused from putting the hand back in warm water, and it is more appropriate to place a bowl of blood in warm water in wintertime.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all other veins, because beneath it there are no pulsating veins nor nerves.
And phlebotomy of the little vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver diseases.

Concerning cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping instruments are made of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping that is without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And cupping instruments, which are administered with scarification and bloodletting, are applied to fourteen places on the body, of which one where cupping is applied is the occiput, the back part of the head; and the middle of the neck on both sides together; and cupping of the chin below the mandible, below the mouth; and cupping of the two shoulder blades; and cupping of the loins, which is above the end of the buttocks; and cupping of the two elbows; and cupping of the two armpits; and cupping of the two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not diminish the strength through them, as it does with phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity calls for cupping due to illness or because it is continuous, we administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at any time.
Therefore, when you see any of these conditions, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we order that cupping be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it is applied it relieves heaviness in the head and that which is settled in the eyes; but this should be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not advisable to use it on one who has a cold complexion, or in whom there is a catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a person.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to use it on the elderly, and on those whose heads are afflicted with cold illnesses.
But to the one upon whom this is continuously applied, forgetfulness is caused; and therefore you should instruct the cupper to place his hand a little below the cupping vessel, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
But cupping with alkehel (likely referring to a specific type of cupping or medicinal preparation) is in the place of phlebotomy of the dark vein (vena fusca) and the basilic vein: and for this reason it benefits asthma, and the constriction of the lungs, and the rupture of a lung instrument (perhaps a reference to a lung injury or instrument), and cough, and repletion (fullness/congestion).
And it is necessary that the alkehel cupping vessel be slightly raised, for if it is placed low, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping with alhagdahan (another specific type of cupping or preparation) benefits accidental pains in the head and ophthalmia (eye inflammation), and migraine, and suffocation, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper not to press his hand on the scarification, lest he injure the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits canker sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar oral diseases, and is in place of phlebotomy of the alar veins (veins near the lips).
But cupping that is done over the two shoulder blades benefits caphatan (likely a specific illness or symptom), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two inner parts of the thighs benefits as much as phlebotomy of three veins, that is, the basilic, median cubital (likely what "fusca" refers to here, given the context), and cephalic veins, because it draws blood from all those smaller veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed excessively in scarification, because the area is denuded of flesh, and underneath are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping glass with which one cups over the hips and it benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that it be a large cupping glass, and that it be of brass, because the area requires strong suction; and thin cupping glasses are quickly broken and should be scarified with scarification.
But cupping of the legs diminishes pain subtly, for it draws blood from the whole body, and it benefits old pains of the joints and of the womb, and of the bladder; and it provokes menstruation, and benefits swellings and edema; and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and of the two jugular veins.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause syncope in many men.
And of the jugular cuppings, the benefit is similar to the benefit of cuppings of the two legs.
And the quality of the placement of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw by tempered suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them lightly, and remove them carefully, so that the humors come to the place by equal admixture. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the appearance of the blood's redness.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and consider the disposition of the humors.
Because that man who is of tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And command the cupper to amplify the scarification and probe a little, and temper the suction with facility.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary that you scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened to the blood and its aqueous humor. But in the second [scarification], to the acquired scrutiny of the extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let a third scarification be made so that it achieves the desired result.
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want the extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with a deep scarification.
And the term scarification in which there is only the depth of the [scarification] point.  (Or:  And the term "scarification" refers only to the depth of the point [of the instrument]).
And if the one whose blood is thick, dark, and viscous, and whose pores are constricted, the places for the cupping glasses should be anointed with emollient, opening, and resolving ointments; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, violet oil, sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or blood oil, and similar ones.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then let the oil be amaranth oil, or balsamita oil, or aloe oil, or anise oil, and similar to them.
But if the patient has large pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be avoided, and for such a person the areas for cupping should be washed with rose water, or cold water of nightshade, or poppy water, or borrage water, and similar things.
And the one whose blood is very humid, wash his cupping areas with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar things.
And for the one whose superfluities are thick, wash the cupping areas with old wine, or with sanzuc water (which is marjoram), or with a decoction of anise, or chamomile, and similar things.
And the cupping should be done before a bath; however, it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and he should sleep after the cupping.
What is required to regulate the patient (or perhaps, "the one to be cupped") before cupping or phlebotomy?
You should consider at the beginning: if the one to be cupped or bled is choleric, and has sharp, inflamed blood, then he should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, endive in sour wine, lettuce, succory, julep, and similar things.
And give him foods such as chicken, the meat of domestic birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and similar things.
And for one whose complexion is cold, he should drink syrup of almonds, or syrup of succory seeds, sumac, and very fragrant wine, mid-way between old and new; and he should be advised to use moderation in drinking and eating, and give him foods such as chicken, hempseed, peas, small birds, and pigeons.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and phlebotomy, his drink should be greater in quantity than his food.
And it should be given to drink at certain hours to some men theriac alpharac, or medicines of musk, that is, nutmeg, or theriac before cupping or before phlebotomy or afterwards, so that the principal members are strengthened and the blood is thinned; and they should not take those [medicines] hot.
But the cuppings that are without scarification should be cuppings that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen, and on the gout, and on the navel, in the areas of the knees, and the hip joint, because these parts do not tolerate scarification on them.
And it is not desired for those [cuppings] except the drawing of blood from a member, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in the illness of a blood flow from the nose; and we use this so that cold, hidden flatulence is dissolved from the member, just as we place it on the belly and the navel; for this rarefies the member and warms it and diminishes the pain because it resolves flatulence.
And it is placed on boils when they occur with obstruction or pus, for by the virtue of strong attraction it quickly opens the obstruction and removes the pus from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of enclosed flatulence in them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger; that is, with hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this are boiled.
Method of cupping administered with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, according to how we have formed it; and the length of the horn should be half a palm; and let it be on the side of it, in the place that is in the circumference, half of it; the hole should be small enough for a needle to enter it.
Let it be made of brass, copper, or tin, with thick edges, smooth, even, and polished so that it does not injure the limb due to its placement on it.
And let there be a transverse bar of brass or iron in the middle of it, on which a candle is placed with the fire.
And let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and thin people are different from the cupping glasses for young people and those with large bodies.
And the way to place this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to extend the wick with a properly-sized wood fire in front of a small wax candle, and place that on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the part below, so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be on the hole we have mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glasses and do not need to remove the finger and allow the vapor to escape through that hole, then the cupping glass will immediately be detached.
And then ignite the wick according to its manner, and repeat if you need to.
But in cupping, which is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar or hole, and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and it is placed on the limb only.
And this is its form: And this cupping glass, the larger it is, the better it is, because it can receive a large quantity of water.

Leech application is not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where the placement of cupping glasses is not possible due to their small size, such as the finger and gum and similar; or on a limb devoid of flesh, such as the finger and nose and similar.
And the quality of using those is that you should find leeches in clean, fresh waters of a pool.
And then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and that no substance remains in their bellies; and then let the body be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and with cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it reddens; and then let them be placed upon it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if it is possible to draw blood from the area with a cupping glass, it is more beneficial afterwards.
And if not, then wash the area with sour wine and then with warm water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged after the application of leeches, and it is oozing, then dip a cloth in cold water, and place it over the area until the oozing stops.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, over it until the bleeding stops; or place halves of roasted beans on the area, and leave them there until the beans adhere to the area and the bleeding stops.
And it is necessary, if needed, to repeat the application of leeches, when it is possible to change those already applied.
And if leeches are contraindicated or unavailable, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood flows; and then apply leeches, for they immediately attach themselves when they sense blood.
And when you want them to detach, sprinkle a little crushed aloe, or salt, or ash on them, and they will detach immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is about the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
O, son, know that foolish and common physicians boast of this chapter, those who never looked at the books of the Ancients nor read a single letter from them.
And for this reason, this manner of knowledge is kept private in our land; and I have never forgotten this in any way at all; and I have not acquired this except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study to understand them, until I extracted the knowledge from them.
And I have also dedicated myself to experimentation and study throughout my life.
And I have described in this chapter everything that my knowledge contains and on which I have experimented; since I have shortened it for you and freed it from lengthy elaboration; and I have shortened it for the sake of brevity; and I have shown it clearly; and I have shown many forms of instruments that are used in it, as is done by way of explanation, just as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter One.
Summary and collections on the subject of bone fractures, those which are necessary to preface before we begin the discussion of broken limbs and their various types. Therefore, I will state in the prologue of this chapter the main points of the discourse; and the chapters on which the practitioner must initially focus and base their understanding of the truth; that is, you and those who are prepared to know or learn this noble art besides you.
