And therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall occurs, then you should initially treat it with phlebotomy or purging, or both together, if no cause forbids it, such as weakness, or if the injured person is a child, or an elderly person in a weakened state, or if the time is one of extreme heat or extreme cold.
And then let the patient be content with a diet of cool herbs and the meat of birds and rabbits; and forbid fatty meats and filling foods and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are certain that the inflammation is reduced, and you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to their previous regimen, to which they are accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed nutritious foods, rich and thick and heavy foods in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, rice, heads [presumably heads of grain or similar], peas, cow stomachs, eggs, fresh fish, thick wine, and similar things. With this regimen, the consolidation of the fracture is made more easily and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in young people and the elderly, cannot be rejoined and consolidated according to their nature because of the desiccation of their limbs.
However, nature joins and applies to the broken bone from every part some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and binds it until they are together conjoined and tied together until they come to the end of strength and firmness, like fire at the beginning, until nothing prevents it from its work.
And for this reason it is necessary that you give the patient foods in which there is heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other limbs are different from one another.
And I come with a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinct from the others.
Many kinds of fractures in themselves. And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there is an arched fracture, and also those which happen in the stars; and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the sagitta (arrow) and stars and additions proceeding and non-proceeding, just as it is done in the quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to each type belongs its own art or method in its restoration, as its recollection will be distinct in its place(s).
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this is a bone fracture known:  its crookedness, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and also when we grasp it with the hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is this made evident by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
Perhaps there is a crookedness or small fissure; however, you should not move it with your hand and with total compression; rather, place upon it those medicines of which mention is made near that time which is suitable for the place; and then it should be tightened with a subtle bandage.
And know that if the place is arched and is not split in two, or something else does not happen to it in that place, as is observed in any part of its comparison, then you must begin at your hour the rectification of it before an abscess forms there.
And if a hot abscess forms there, then leave it for a few days until the abscess subsides; and then dress it according to any method you can, gently and skillfully.
And know that the restoration and healing of it is easier than of a bone in which a similar injury has occurred.
And then bind it as the instructions will dictate.
But if the bone is injured, then extension of that broken limb from two sides is necessary, if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords or with one cord and your hand.
And be in a position where you can place the limb in a dressing according to its natural shape; while the broken parts of the limb are extended, then you must force those additions back into place with skill and by any method you can.
And take care, according to your strength, that you do not cause the patient pain or injury through your operation; and force yourself, according to your ability, to apply one bone to its companion, according to their best fit.
And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch that with your hand, and if you see anything amiss, rectify it and adjust it to the extent of your ability.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish physicians do, who often cause hot abscesses through their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen through their operations many times.
And the quality of the binding of the calmed limb is that which I recommend; so that you know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their largeness and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the wrist and similar to those, then it is necessary that the wrapping be of very fine soft cloth.
But those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, it is necessary that in those there be a wide and firm wrapping, because the wide binding connects the large limb and binds it on all sides with even pressure, so that no blemish enters it.
Then begin, after your equalization evacuation, to place a bandage suitable for this, over the place of that fracture with three or four wrappings, according to what the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then come with that to the highest part of the place of the fracture, and tighten less than you did your binding with which you bound the place of the fracture. And then extend yourself with a wrapping of the place of the fracture a little, and soften the binding little by little, until you take from the place a large part of it.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with a wrapping, and then come with the wrappings to the lower part of the fracture; and let your operation be in the tightening of the wrapping and in the softening of it according to what we have said in the first wrapping above.
And then place between the layers of bandage, linen or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is padded, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, place nothing there.
And then place another bandage over it, and then wrap over these bandages well-made splints immediately, if in the place there is no inflammation or hot abscess, and then place over it that which dries the abscess and which removes the inflammation; and leave that for some days; and then you tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be of half a cane, smooth and well-formed wisely; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint which is placed over the fracture be thicker and a little smoother than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to what is required for the limb in size and smallness; and then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then bind over it with thread decently as we have said of tightening, and it is that your tightening be according to the location of the fracture, the more it is distanced from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the operation of some fools placing their threads of twisted ropes, and it is a great error when the tightening falls out of equality.
And likewise, very fine threads are not convenient, because you will not achieve with those what you want through tightening, and it is not necessary that there be less than a finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is harmed by the ends of the splints near the binding in those places, then place under those some carded flax or wool until that does not cause him harm.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption occurs in the bone, and I will come to a recollection of that in the following chapter according to its singularity.
And know that not every broken limb is bound with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that, when the limb is large, it is not necessary to place splints on it except after about five days or seven or more, according to your certainty of the occurrence of a hot abscess.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients restored [fractures], which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
Method of a common, abbreviated plaster for the restoration of fractures, suitable for many complexions and especially for women and children, because it is something upon which neither heat nor cold prevails: and it is that you take the powder of the mill, which is the bran that hangs from the movement of the millstone on the walls of the mill.
And make it like this, except that you sift it with egg white, and apply the confection neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of a plaster restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of labdanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and megrach (which is the root of wild pomegranate), of each one dram; of myrrh and aloes, half a dram; of aloechil, two drams; let all be crushed and sifted, and mixed with aloe water or with egg white if the patient's complexion is warm; and then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores slightly broken bones, and it benefits many people.
Another method of a plaster which benefits fractures and sprains: take megrach and marshmallow root, one dram each; of myrrh and aloes, half a dram each; of acacia, six drams; of Armenian bole, two drams.
Let all be crushed in a mortar with much pounding, and be sifted, and be mixed with water or with egg white and be administered.
A method for a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and if pain occurs in those and in fissures and fractures: let soiled wool be taken and be submerged in sour wine and cooked oil and be placed on the area.
However, in this plaster there is no restorative virtue, rather it draws out hot abscesses and expels pain effectively.
Another method of a plaster that restores a broken bone: take fresh fig leaves and wild poppy leaves; pound everything and make a plaster from them.
A method for a brief plaster administered for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remaining abscess: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, and violet flowers, barley flour, of each an equal part; let all be crushed and be mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then mix that with moist coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method for a stronger plaster in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb: take roots of marshmallow and linseed, and of pomegranate, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of each an equal part; let all be crushed and be mixed with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the restoration of its color.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration except a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it be of medium consistency between thick and thin.
But in Gaul it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the substances which are drying with a little heat are thus, such as myrrh, incense, and similar to those substances that we have mentioned.
Method for a plaster that relieves injury and pain: take millet, chickpea flour, shaved human hair or bird feathers, and marshmallow, and salt, in equal parts; let them be crushed and sifted and mixed, and let a plaster be made.
But the amount of time the bandage should remain in place and when it should be loosened is that you consider whether no pain or itching has occurred, and if the bone is not broken or moved from its place, then you should not loosen it for many days. And if at the location there is intense pain or itching or inflammation, then you should carefully loosen or untie it at that time, and remove the plaster from it.
And take linen cloth and a moist sea sponge, and submerge it in lukewarm water, and with this wash the area until the itching ceases and the pain is calmed; and then allow the limb to rest for an hour; and then place on it soiled wool submerged in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night until you are certain of a hot abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb subsides, and its pain is lessened.
And then repeat that subtle constriction and small plaster and do not tighten it with your first constriction, but refine it with that until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess and the redness and the pain and the inflammation and all the symptoms have already subsided completely, and you need a plaster and constriction, then you repeat it as you did at the beginning, equally.
And if there is no occurrence in the limb of any of those causes that we have mentioned, then do not loosen it until after three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all of this is left for twenty days, depending on what appears to you upon loosening the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh upon it.
And then adjust the constriction more than in your first constriction, and adjust it to the thickening of the patient's flesh, as his recovery proceeds.
But if you see the place of the fracture drying and thinning more than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore moisten it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it; and let this be done every three days; and loosen the constriction a little, and by this operation nourishment will run to the limb, and it will heal easily.
But what foolish restorers of a limb fracture do sometimes, when it is not set properly at the beginning, and is set crookedly, is an error according to their operation, and a great risk, and if this were correct, the Ancients would have enlarged upon it in their books and would have operated with it.
And I have not seen any of them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable not to do it.
Chapter Two: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are made by the percussion of a sword, and the percussion breaks the bone entirely to the marrow, which is beneath the bone, just as an axe does in wood, and for this reason this type is called axial; or the sword cuts some part of the bone, and cuts its flesh, but the incision does not go through to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called *levatio absoluta* (complete elevation/lifting).
And the wound from these two fractures is either large or small, and there is a fracture that is a contusion or abrasion; and its cause is percussion with a blunt object or a fall on a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the periosteum which is beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound from these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone, as small as the thinness of a hair, and for this reason this type is called a capillary fracture.
And there is a fracture that is made either by a fall or by percussion with a stone and similar things, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter the inner parts, and is made in a concave place, just as happens to the sides of a ram when it receives a blow and part of it is driven into the inner parts.
And this happens mostly in the bodies of soft bones, such as the skulls of infants.
And in all these types of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will bring everything back to its place with repositioning.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by probing them and by investigating them with probes and by removing the corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is uncovered on the bone, and it is wiped clean, and then examined with a probe, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider first the patient's symptoms.  And then if you see by the symptoms that it signifies by manifest signs of danger, such as vomiting of bile, prostration, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, and sinking of the eyes and coldness of them, and similar symptoms in the most serious cases, without a doubt it is not easily healed.
And if you see no serious complications, you expect recovery, then you begin its treatment.
The reason is that if the injured person has come to you at the beginning, as injured, and this is in the winter months, then you must study the setting of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to every circumstance.
And if it is in the summer months, then you must endeavor to set the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone is corrupted by the periosteum, and before the complications that we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the periosteum covering the brain, and is with small, splintered pieces, then you must incise the comminuted and splintered part, as I will explain to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the injured patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as best you can according to the nature of the injury, and that it be gentle on the patient.
And if you encounter, during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you must oppose it with what is necessary, and that is to fill the area with cloths soaked in rose-colored wine and oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is settled, and you are sure of the flow of blood; and then begin with the encircling of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow incisor, and this is its form:  And then apply another incisor like this, a little wider than that one; and this is its form:
And then apply even wider incisors than the second.
And it is necessary that you have a number of different incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and should be sharp at the ends, and should be made of Indian iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisor so that you do not strike the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you apply the incisors with the drills, which are called shallow drills.
And they are not called so except because these do not reach the limit of the skull bone adjacent to it, because the drill has a round end or edge below its sharp head, similar to a small edge or circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each of which is suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that you have available for all parts of the skull a drill whose end is sharp, its length and shortness being according to the thickness of that part of the skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills: The way to perforate the circumference of the broken bone is to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the drill to another place, and place the holes such that the space between them is the size of the width of a finger or thereabouts.
Then incise with incisors the area between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as possible in order to remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument that you will have prepared for this purpose, such as small pincers and forceps.
And you must take great care that the incisor does not touch anything of the brain.
And so when you have encircled the bone by perforating it, and it is separated from the brain if it is continuous with it, and you have extracted it, you should scrape and smooth its roughness where it is attached to the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor, but it should be thinner and finer than the other incisors.
And if any small and jagged pieces of bone remain, then remove them easily with the instruments you have available; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments that we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is easier and less frightening, which Galen described and praised highly; and this is his account: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin by uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until, when you have uncovered that part and placed under it the tip of that slow and sharp incisor of which this is the form:
And it is so that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is split in two lengthwise, like a pair of tongs; so that the lenticular part is applied to the brain with its surface, the sharp part cutting into the bone.
And then strike the incisor on one side with a small mallet until it cuts through all the bone easily as it is rotated, and you are sure of the periosteum so that no accident befalls it in any way, even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
Then, if any matter adhering to the bone or periosteum remains in any part of the bone, cut it away with the lenticular end of that incisor, and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the periosteum, and what is excised or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughness and small splinters remain, then you must scrape away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, for which you have prepared a number, of various sizes, so that it is possible for you to use in every place the most appropriate and most suitable of them according to how you proceed with your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And you must proceed in your scraping with which you scrape the bone, beginning with the broadest of those rasps, and then use progressively the finer ones, and do not cease doing this continuously until you come to using the finest and most subtle of all of them.
But in each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer care according to how you proceed to their rectification.
And this is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And I stand by what we have written and shown in cases of large fractures.
And if the panniculus (skin and subcutaneous tissue) is retracted when you scrape the bone, then you must take linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; and then take another doubled or tripled cloth and immerse it in wine and rose oil and place this over the thin cloth and do this as lightly as you can, so as not to press on the wound; and then apply a broad bandage over this, and do not tighten it except enough to hold the wound in place only.
And leave it with this dressing for a day or two until you are certain of a hot abscess; and then untie it and apply medicines that counteract excessive dryness, such as flaxseed, barley flour, and powder of chamomile and birthwort, and things similar to those.
And it is so that you make a powder of these medicines to be sprinkled on the wound, once they are dry; and generally, apply to it any medicine whose property it is to cleanse and not bite (i.e., cause further irritation).
And take care, according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment remains clean; and let there be no filth of ointment or anything else; and do not allow virulence to increase in it; for when it is applied to the pannicle of the brain, it corrupts and putrefies it, and this evil causes great harm to the patient.
And it happens in the wound of the head, as the bone is purified from it, and especially when its care is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, consider then that if the patient suffers the consequences that we have mentioned, know that it is pernicious without any doubt.
And if there is no blackness except from the medicine that is placed on top, and it is by virtue of that medicine that this blackness is made, then it is necessary that you take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these you should moisten a cloth, and then place it over the wound; and then treat it with the appropriate healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two parts or one of them sometimes, because in it there are two bones, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and is not broken, and there is only abrasion, twisting, and crookedness in it.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then it is necessary that you insert your little finger into their nose, and that you equalize that fracture inside with your index finger, and with the thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and be careful that you do not cause the patient any pain through your operation.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it must be set with the end of a rod of small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same for both.
And take care to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the seventh or tenth day you should remove the heated poultice.
And then insert into the end of the nose a pledget of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or two pledgets if the fracture is in two parts, and let the pledget be of such size as to fill the opening of the nose.
And I say that some of the ancient restorers say that the pledgets should be soaked in wine and changed every day, but I have not seen this; rather, it is necessary that the pledget be soaked in egg white mixed with powdered barley; and then leave the pledget there until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a pledget, a goose feather, wrapped in linen cloth, should be inserted into the nose; and this is especially useful if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not impeded from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, use pledgets.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs,  apply a plaster to the nose with cerate or with cotton soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then you should plaster that area externally with sesame flour and powdered lentils mixed with egg white; and then place clean linen over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then it is necessary to make an incision over them, and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then close the incision, and treat it with whatever promotes granulation and consolidation, and with suitable plasters for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it with pledgets and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external, and is not broken in two and is bent inwards at the broken parts, then its diagnosis is easy.
And it is necessary, therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and push with it the crookedness of the fracture inwards, easily aligning the external parts, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; thus decently equalizing it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from two sides according to the straightness until its equalization is possible.
And if there is concussion of teeth with disruption, then bind those which you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or silk; and then place on the broken jaw a cerate [a medicated ointment], and then place over it a doubled cloth, and place on the cloth a well-made large compress, or a piece of sole leather equal to the length of the jaw; and then tie that above according to how the binding appears to you and as the application suits, until it is healed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food of sorbitious [easily digestible]  liquids.
And if you estimate that the alignment has been altered in any way, then you should examine it and unbind it on the third day; and then rectify what has been altered and apply a plaster with powdered barley, with egg white, or with bean flour, after you have removed the cerate, and place wool over the plaster.
And then, when this plaster is attached and adjusted, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it; do not unbind it until it is healed and the fracture is held firm; for many times this fracture is healed in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and that abscess is reduced.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of bone is separated or splintered; then you should carefully remove those splinters with whatever is suitable for this in the removal of them from the wound.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a fleam, according to the amount of your need.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters which is suitable for this and heals it, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken in the front part above the muscle, and this fracture is done in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that splinters occur in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the fractured clavicle and supports it, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
And then align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is neither little prominence nor concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, and let its size be according to your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the pad with your hand until you align the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle to the outer parts, because it has reached the acromion, then the patient should be supine on their neck, and you should place on the muscle a moderately sized cushion.
And the assistant presses the muscle downwards until the bone of the clavicle that is on the acromion above is elevated, and then you rectify the fracture and align it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are loose, then you should make an incision over it and easily extract those splinters.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you should carefully incise it with one of the scalpels that you have prepared for this, after having placed under the clavicle the instrument that serves as a support; and this instrument is made of wood or iron, of which this is the shape:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size or smallness of the bone.
But its length should be as long as is possible for you in the operation; let it have two ends like a spoon, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is a risk of a hot abscess, then you should close the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then dip a cloth in sharp vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it over that.
And above all, ensure the linen cloth is not dislodged from over the broken bone; and for that reason it is necessary that you see the patient each day, so that when the bandage is loosened or you see the linen cloth is already dislodged, then you correct that and tighten the binding.
And then place the patient's pillow under his neck, and place a small cushion under his testicles at night; so that with this his afflicted area is elevated, and thus the fracture of the clavicle is elevated by the muscle; or his arm should be tied to his neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless there is a noticeable swelling or abscess at the site, until twelve days have passed.
And then renew the plaster if you see the need, and adjust the binding and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the clavicle is restored and coagulated.
And it is strengthened and healed, according to the condition, in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken in its lateral part, and not broken through, but only at the edges.
And so when the place of it is broken — and it is broken in the middle of the place — and is not known except by touch, then, according to what the figure of the fracture shows, forcibly prepare it and reduce it to its natural shape in every way possible to you.
Then place on the area powdered barley mixed with egg white and lion's wool; then place a powder of doubled cloth; then place over it a thin linen bandage according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or a little more.
But if under the bandage there is a cavity in any of the parts of the shoulder blade, then you should fill that cavity with lion's wool, and then tighten the bandage evenly.
Then bind it over with a long bandage with decent constriction and firmness of the bandage until it is not loosened from its place.
And examine the binding each day; and every time that it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it, and even out the bandage if it is loosened from its place.
And let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And so when that number is complete, then you unbind it, and you should know for certain that this is for bones whose beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if it proceeds from the location of the hip bone and settles below the rib cage, then you incise over it, and remove it, and proceed as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle concerning the cauterization of an abscess if that occurs here.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this heals slowly; but its extremities heal much faster, as they are scarred and comminuted.
And among its accidents, when the middle of the foot is broken, it inclines inwards and causes great pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, and strong palpitations and vomiting of blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is apparent and not hidden from feeling.
And its restoration is that the patient should be supine on his back, and a cushion placed between his shoulders; and then press on his muscles, and adjust the bones with hands on both sides, and be skillful in the operation and in the setting, in every way possible and appropriate, until the bone's shape is restored as it should be.
Then place a plaster and lint over it, and place over that a splint of thin willow or lime wood, or similar in lightness, after you have wrapped it in cloth; and then be skillful in tying it over the broken bone so that it is not disturbed; and use a bandage, sometimes round the back, and tighten it with a suitable constriction; and then check the bandage frequently, and tighten it when it loosens.
And if necessity demands loosening it because of corrosion or itching, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you should carefully untie it, remove the plaster, and rectify what has occurred with appropriate treatment; and then replace the plaster, if it seems appropriate; and adjust the bandage until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs only occurs in the thick parts which are two; and in their extremities, in the front part, no crushing occurs, except because they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of it is not hidden from the feeling of its investigation with the fingers.
And the restoration should be thus: equalize the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until it is aligned as needed, and then plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if that is needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclined towards the inner parts, then the patient suffers vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that occurs in someone with pleurisy, and this is because the bone pierces the membrane; and the patient experiences difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And already the Ancients were ingenious in that with many inventions.
And of them it is said: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that produce inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we would avoid this so that it does not lightly cause a hot abscess, if it does not already exist; and if it already exists, then it helps with that.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on top and that it should draw strongly; and it seems reasonable, but it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the place because of the weakness.
And some of them said: the place should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is full, so that the bandage is even as it encloses that roundness; and then the patient should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, with diet and medicine.
And if a violent cause constricts the patient, which he cannot bear, and the bone pierces the skin with a lacerating puncture,  threatening the patient's life, then it is necessary to make an incision over the area, uncover the fractured rib, and then place over it the instrument that preserves the lung, the remembrance of which is mentioned previously, and that we incise the bone easily and remove it.
And then we should join the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess forms inside, then you should consider and insert powders into rose oil and place [it] over the area, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him lie supine on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and it is slow to heal, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider: for if you see his hands already softened and numbed and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend nor flex them, and when you constrict them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he is not healed, and in most dispositions, it is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels constriction in them with the fingers and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if he has something similar in the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, the same thing happens to them as happened to the hands, and when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and defecation without his will, and sometimes he lies supine on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not labor in his cure.
But if none of those things happen, the cause is better and lighter.
And his cure is that you should perforate the inflamed boil, that you place on the affected vertebra only pure rose oil, or with beaten egg whites; apply this several times a day until the inflamed boil is healed.
And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it tightly; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where he finds pain until it is healed.
And if it happens because of the attrition of a bone splinter, or some cause that is already separated from it, then it is necessary that you cut the skin over the bone and remove that bone; and then join the two lips of the wound with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment until it is healed.
But if the posterior part of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the hip joint, then it is necessary that you insert the little finger of your left hand into the rectum, and that you manipulate the broken bone with the other hand as much as is possible by gentle traction; and then apply a plaster and bandage, if you judge it necessary, and then bind it tightly.
And if you feel splinters of bone in it, then you should probe that area, and remove them, and treat the wound with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the restoration of the hip.
The hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are broken, then their fracture is only a communication in the extremities of those bones and is located lengthwise, and inclines towards the inner parts; and the patient experiences pain and throbbing in the area, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only in the extremity of it, then you align that according to what is prepared for you by equalization, aligning it until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or inclines towards the lower parts, then you have the patient lie on their stomach so that the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. And then when you have aligned it, place a plaster on it, and then place a splint of wood or bone on top of that, and tighten it with a bandage such that you do not fear a displacement of the fracture or a movement of the splint.
And align the concavity with something that fills it until it receives the bandage evenly, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is sedated, and place on it something that sedates it as has been previously described; and then return to its restoration, and bind it as is necessary.
And if it happens in the bones that they are shattered or comminuted, then you extract them.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the clavicle (collarbone).
The clavicle is that which is between the shoulder and the neck.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a wooden splint of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at both ends, and then it should be suspended from a raised position.
And place the patient on a seat; and then lay their broken arm upon the splint until it reaches the armpit, in the middle of which the curve of the splint should be fitted.
And then suspend some heavy weight from it, or let the attendant extend it downwards; and then let the physician set the fracture with his hands, until the fracture is properly realigned.
And the other method is that the patient lies supine on their back and suspend the arm from their neck with a bandage; and then instruct two attendants that one of them should hold that which is above the fracture with their hands; and the other should hold the part below; and each of them should extend it in their direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you should tighten a bandage over the fracture and another bandage over that, and each of the two attendants should extend to their respective parts.
And thus, if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the armpit and the other below the fracture near the elbow.
Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that spot and on that elbow.
Swathe that with ease and without trouble, and when it is swathed as necessary and applied with a good application, then tighten it unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day, and place on it soiled wool soaked in sharp wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you should tighten that.
And the manner of tightening it is that you place a plaster on the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your open hand on their muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm on the support so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is [necessary] that you place on the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is [necessary] that your bandage over the fracture site be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you [proceed].
And if you fear a hot abscess, then you leave the bandage and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then examine the bandage in all parts for three days so that no itching or inflammation occurs at the site;  or [it is] forbidden for food to reach the limb due to the tightness of the bandage; and therefore rectify all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage until after three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place over the splint a poultice full of wool evenly, and check every hour of the night and day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is softened; therefore, rectify all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until the [wound] begins to close; and then it is fitting that you thicken his food.
Because it is customary for the splint and his leg to be bound for forty days; and then it is fitting that they be unbound and a bath be administered and it be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is badly crushed, then you should not untie the bandage and splints from it until one month or until two months.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two forearms; of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the little finger, and the other is large and it is that which is placed below the small finger, on the lower side.
And very easily the larger forearm is broken alone, or the smaller, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small forearm is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm is broken below, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm above, then it is convenient for the physician, during its restoration, to apply slight extension easily until it is aligned.
But if it is the larger forearm that is broken, then it is necessary to apply more forceful extension.
And if both forearms are broken together, then you must apply the extension much stronger.
And it is necessary that you position the shape of your hand during its restoration and extension stretched out on a cushion, and the wrist of the hand should be at the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be lying on a flat couch.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer strain.
Then let one assistant extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should manipulate the bone until he reduces it according to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then you must forcefully reduce all pieces to their place with your skill.
And if a separated splinter appears that penetrates the skin and you have no hope of restoring it, then you should cut it off and remove it according to the method which we have described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already dedicated a special chapter to that, and therefore take care of it accordingly.
And if an abscess occurs early in your treatment, then you should apply a cloth with a cerate made of rose oil and white wax, of moderate consistency (neither too thick nor too thin), and bind the cloth tightly over it until the abscess subsides; and then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from powdered molli (possibly a type of herb or powder) with egg white, and then apply a compress; and the compress that is applied over the fracture should be thicker and firmer.
And know that the number of compresses on the arm depends on the major disposition, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
Then apply your bandage over the fracture site as firmly and tightly as possible, and if the bandage is closer to the parts above the fracture or to the parts below it, apply the bandage a little more loosely, as was previously mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is bound should be of moderate thickness, neither too fine nor too coarse, as we have already recommended.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any event occurs that requires correction, such as itching which happens in the limb, then you should bathe the limb with warm water until that itching ceases.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the fracture is already softened and the bone is rejoined and similar to the others, then correct everything with your skill.
And consider further if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandage, for then it is necessary that you loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient experiences none of the conditions we have mentioned, then the bandage should not be loosened for approximately twenty days or thereabouts.
And then let the patient's hand be suspended by its own agitating movement, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or even quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the constitution and the disposition of the body's powers.
Chapter 13.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures rarely happen in the wrist and the finger joints; instead, attrition [or wearing away] occurs many times.
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then it is necessary that the patient lie down, and a pillow be placed under his arm, according to the level, and then place his hand on that extended pillow.
And then let the assistant extend the broken bones, and the physician join them until they are joined with a good conjunction.
And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless a hot abscess occurs; and then place a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to squeeze it with his fractured palm; then let it be bound tightly with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather with cloth in which it is softened, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tighten it as needed.
But if the fracture is on the outer parts, then it is necessary that you place a bandage above and another bandage below on the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, as the hand is bent at the wrist between the fingers with ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, let it be straightened as needed and then bound to the palm and, if you wish, apply a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index finger, or in the ring finger, or in the little finger, then let it be straightened and bound to the finger next to it; or let them all be bound together consecutively, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and close to it, the disposition of a hot abscess and oppose — with your operation — what is needed when any of those things occur, as his narrative reiterates.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and this is apparent to the senses depending on whether it is broken at the front or the upper part.
And its restoration is done in this way: a bandage is tightened above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and face.
Then the physician extends the entire bandage to its appropriate part, according to the nature of the fracture, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the hip joint, then let the bandage be close to the knee on the side of the kneecap, so that the extension pulls upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then let the bandage be placed near the knee, and let the extension be towards the lower parts.
And then the physician manipulates the bone with both hands until he reduces it to its natural form and the bone is joined by a good conjunction; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs, then you leave it for some days until the heat of the abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made thus:  you should wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and fold it two or three times, removing any excess; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the hip joint.
And insert a long strip of material between the thigh and the leg near the knee on the lower parts, and let the ends of the material come from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the leg and thigh what is superfluous of the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is firm.
And then begin with a firm bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four wrappings, and the further you move from the fracture site, the looser and softer your bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the material to the lower parts until it reaches the ankle, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not loosened from its place.
And then leave the bandage there, in such a way that it does not cause corrosion, hot abscess, swelling, or anything similar to the limb; and if any of these things occur, then carefully loosen it, and rectify what has happened immediately, as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone that protrudes, then you must reposition it as much as possible; and if not, cover it and reduce it, and treat it with what has been previously mentioned until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened beyond what is achieved by applying splints, just as we have described in the treatment of the arm.
However, in the restoration done in this way, lameness does not occur to the patient; and if it is restored alone, without the other leg being supported, then it is necessary that he limps on that leg constantly.
And know that the thigh is bound for forty days, or a little more or a little less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but often causes attrition (or wearing away).
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and all this is accompanied by feeling (or sensation).
And its restoration is thus: that which is broken of its parts should be joined together with the said things until it is added and conjoined as much as possible by equalization, ease, and fabrication (or construction/setting).
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you need it; and bind it above with a suitable bandage.
And then consider all the provisions which we have recommended in the other fractures, such as a hot poultice and similar things, so that every accident is opposed with what is convenient for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is large and is called by the name of tibia [shinbone]; and the other is slender and is called fibula.
And the same types of fractures occur to each of these bones of the leg as occur to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is the same.
And if the two bones are broken together, the leg is bent in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is bent forward.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is among those things that are not easily visible to you.
Then administer extension, setting, and ligation of the splints according to that same method.
However, if the leg fracture is large with many splinters, the extension should be less and gentler, and alleviate it with your skill.
And in the leg there is some cause for operating on the bones, and when you are setting the bones and completing your entire operation, then you will take pieces of pine wood, which are placed under the bandages that are placed on the soles, and are usually made of palm branches; choose those of small thickness, and not the thin ones, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the very bottom.
Then wrap each one in two cloths according to its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side, and they should extend from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then bind the two splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle, for with this tightening the leg is prevented from bending to the right and left, and remains in a good position.
And now a small splint of wood, according to the length of the leg, has been applied; and let the leg be placed upon it so that it maintains that position without movement.
And especially it is necessary that you do this in a fracture, and when there is a wound with it properly.
And then consider the leg each day and study it with careful examination, and guard it from abscess and inflammation or other similar conditions; and if any of these conditions occurs, then attend to it with what is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the talus and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the whole talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot do occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes rarely occur, and what occurs to them is more often attrition from ill-disposition.
And if a fracture occurs to the bones of the foot and sometimes those bones are elevated above one another, then have the patient place his foot on the ground prepared as before.
Then place one foot on that which is elevated of those bones, and then press down on them, and squeeze and manipulate them until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and lint on top, and place under the sole of the foot a small board to which two padded ends are attached.
Then bind that with the foot with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things that you need.
And when three or four days have passed for him, then you untie the bandage, for you will find the bones set, whether they were fractured or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if some say they are fractured, then restore those, and manipulate them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then apply to the fractured finger a splint according to the length of the finger, and let it be a little longer than it; and place under the foot that board which I have recounted to you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if that which is fractured of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in lion cloth, and bind the board at the bottom of the foot, and let it rest on one of the ends of the board outside the sole of the foot so that it holds with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you not forget that you consider all the causes which I have told you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is fitting.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the hip of the woman is fractured, then make her lie down on her back, and gently incline her to one side a little, and let her be held from behind.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it should be made into a kind of ball in her hip; and then the woman should be lifted, and her hip raised little by little, for all this is to be manipulated until the fractured part of the hip is in place, like a ball, and the broken bone returns [to its place].
And then place powders on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and let her urinate, and reposition and return it according to the method with which you set it initially, and then return to her first position.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it is restored.
And if you wish, take an ox bladder and attach a reed cannula to its opening; and insert the whole bladder into her hip, and then blow into the cannula forcefully until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, for this will help the fracture to return [to its proper place].
And then it should be filled with cotton and remain so for some days, as we have advised, until it is healed.
And when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, then administer for its restoration and healing what we have recommended for the hip bone.
And that which is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures that occur infrequently, especially for one who has some study, use, and understanding of this my book; for that fracture signifies to you in the greatest provisions the correct way in its restoration and its binding, and therefore understand well.
But in the case of a fractured penis, take the white of an egg, and place the penis in it; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and then let it remain for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of bone fractures when they are accompanied by a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar to those, that you carefully consider and immediately phlebotomize him if the conditions for phlebotomy indicate it as we have said above.
And if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you attend to its retention so that you pulverize crushed egg whites over it, if no other cause is present.
And then begin to set the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you should leave its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, uncovered, then you must carefully reduce it and align it with your hands gently and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight fingers, and its width according to the size of the wound; and for this it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these, according to the quantity needed for this in the treatment of every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it be of little thickness so that it is not bent by the compression upon it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp end which has a margin at its edges; and let the upper part of it be of increasing thickness, and from the middle of it to the ends.
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Because it is necessary that the sharp end of it be raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And push that in at once until the bone returns, and it is held in place with some device.
And then strive to align the ends of the fracture, and if the broken end of the bone is thin and does not accept the instrument properly, then you must cut the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have recommended, then incise it with whatever is suitable from among the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds intense, persistent pain, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do what you can to give the patient great relief.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound cerate or anything else containing oil, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption there.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you may incise the opening with scissors and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and it has caused death or a corrosive or suppurating disease, that is, gangrene, in their patients. And if your constriction is light, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have recounted, and you find pain in the place, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a bandage of firm cloths instead of stitches and bind it with those.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to generate pus, then you remove the cloth which you had placed on it with wine, and then administer poultices, ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to untie the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed, and it is necessary that the limb be positioned in such a way that the pus flows from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass and the wound is not healed nor is the pus removed from it, then know that here are hidden small things, wherefore it is necessary to investigate the wound with a probe. And of those hidden things, those which are separated, remove and extract them; those which are not separated and press on the limb and cause pain, you must incise them as far as it is possible for you.
And if a swelling or ulcer occurs in the wound, that is, a stye, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you treat each of these with what is appropriate for it, of whose treatment a description has been given previously in its chapter.
And this is what my discourse should address and you should take note of: when a large bone is fractured, elevate the limb, such as the bone of the hip and the humerus and similar large limb bones, and do not attempt traction or extraction of it, for many times death occurs because of this; rather, leave it until it festers, for it will fall away by itself very quickly, within twenty or thirty days; and then treat the wound if it seems appropriate to you, and if not, leave it be.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20: Of the treatment of a swelling that occurs at the site of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of the healing of a fracture, and especially if it is near the joints, and it deforms the shape of the limb; and it quickly prevents the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have styptic properties, such as aloes, and incense, and myrrh, and cerecloth, and acacia, and similar things;  so that you take some or all of these, and mix them with styptic wine, or with egg white, or with sour wine, and apply it to the swelling on a cloth, and bind it tightly.
And leave the bandage so that you do not untie it for many days; and then untie it and apply another, until the swelling is reduced; or bind a thin sheet of lead tightly over it, because lead has the property of reducing anything that is swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already become hardened and constricted, and necessity compels its removal carefully, then incise it at its most elevated part, and excise the excess and prominent parts, or scrape it with a sharp instrument until it is reduced; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Chapter 21. Of the restoration of a fracture when it is set, and afterward the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is set, and afterward the limb remains thin and weak, this is caused by many things: one of which is excessive loosening of the ligaments and their binding not as it should be; or by excessive tightness of the bandages so that nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb; or by excessive application of embrocations; or by excessive movement not at the proper time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and to make his heart cheerful so that his blood is increased; and administer baths; and introduce joy and merriment to him.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws much nourishment to it, or continue embrocation with lukewarm water until nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Chapter 22. Of the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly and the proper operation is prevented.
When it happens to a limb already healed from crookedness and prominence at the fractured bone and swelling, and because of this there is deformity of the limb, but nevertheless the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not accept the opinion of one who thinks that limb is newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish physicians and healers who have done this in our land; and this operation is very blameworthy, leading to great fear, the sign of which is death.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed in lukewarm water in which are boiled emollient herbs, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar things; and a poultice should be made with a softening poultice such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be prepared and mixed with white clay or wax oil, and applied as a poultice; or ripe figs should be taken and crushed with pigeon dung, and similar things from the medicines that are called  "emollients for consolidation".
And sometimes it is resolved with frequent light friction with moist hands, and movement of the limb in every direction should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become severe and hardened, and there is need to treat it with iron, then it should be sawn off above the affected area and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which is superfluous to the swelling of the bone itself should be incised with fine scalpels, and relief should be provided to this with care and skill.
Chapter 23.
Concerning a discourse on dislocation.
A dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints from its place, because of which movement is impeded, and the shape of the limb is distorted, and it causes great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs to someone, then it is necessary to immediately strive that it be reduced, and not delay that reduction, because if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and easily, by this, the reduction of the dislocation becomes difficult.
And for this reason it should not be moved or stretched during the inflammation, because many times it causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, then you should attend to the phlebotomy of the patient, and then allow it to rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with that which will bring about its recovery.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what preceded in fracture; from the most superior part of the body to the most inferior part.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, it happens quickly; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are removed from their places by slight movement, and are slightly softened; or they are dislocated by complete and total dislocation, so that they are loosened at the joint, to the point that saliva flows from the patient and they cannot retain it or close their mouth, and their tongue is entangled in their speech.
But when their dislocation is slight, then in most instances it returns by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and total, then a gentle reduction must be administered, and it should not be delayed at all.
And it is so that the assistant holds the patient's head, and the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the root of the jaw dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands are outside with which he pulls; and he commands the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to be moved from each side; and the physician pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer a vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And then, when they are returned and pulled together, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on it powders of cloth with cerate, and it should be made of wax and rose oil; and then it should be bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head should be between two pillows, so that it does not lean to the right or left; and do not force them to chew anything; rather, their food should be finely minced, until, when the pains are lessened and the jaws are healed, then let them eat what they wish.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not work on opening the mouth during eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is healed and mended.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and continuous thirst occur, and very quickly the patient's belly is distended, and very quickly and purely choleric vomit is produced.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and many times he to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inwards because of its connection with the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then constrict the area with your palm with firm compression, for it will return.
And then place a plaster and powders on it and bind it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is joined to the hip and is continuous with it, is not dislocated on any side; then it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as is available to you.
