And then place a plaster, and powders, and a bandage on it, and instruct the patient to adhere to quiet and rest until it is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated in only three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated to the rear parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and protrudes outwards to the lower parts below the armpit, and especially in those who have little flesh, it is easily dislocated; but in those who have much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And it quickly happens to some men through a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is believed to be dislocated because of that.
Then you should examine this carefully before attending to him, and then proceed with his cure.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the armpit; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a manifest difference between them; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity, and below the armpit, next to the touch of the head of the muscle, something like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all movements.
If it is dislocated near the feet or upwards, and you then find a manifest swelling, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is recent and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises his hand to the upper parts; and then places his hands below his armpit, and raises the joint strongly upwards to its place, and the minister raises his hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it below because it returns lightly.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and from the beginning that the dislocation happened, many days have passed, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and a poultice be applied that softens and relaxes, such as boiled roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be applied.
And then the patient should lie supine on his back and a wool ball, of a consistency between soft and firm, be placed under his armpit; and then the physician should place his heel on the ball, and raise the head of the muscle forcefully, and pull the patient's hand so that it is not pulled downwards, and another minister should hold the patient's head, so that it is not pulled downwards, for then it immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: that you have present a man who is taller than the patient, and he stands on that side, and inserts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another minister pulls the patient's hand from below his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced easily according to another method: that a long pole is fixed in the earth, the head of which is round in shape, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the patient;  after which soft cloths are placed on top of the pole, and the patient stands along the length of the pole, and then his hand is extended downwards on the other side; and his body is pulled strongly from the other side, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if its reduction is difficult for all the reasons we have mentioned, then use this treatment: that you take a pole the length of which is two arms, and its width is four fingers, and its thickness is eased at the armpit end in this manner:
And then soft cloths are tied over the rounded end so that the pole does not injure the patient.
And then it is placed under the armpit of the patient, and the hand or arm is extended over the pole down to the lower parts, and the pole is tied above the shoulder and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a rung of a ladder according to its width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the other body is suspended from the other side, for the joint enters immediately.
And when its reduction is complete, in whichever manner is possible for you, then you should place under the armpit a ball of wool of suitable size; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered myrrh and egg white over the entire muscle, in such a way that it is wrapped around, and then bind tightly under the armpit with a suitable bandage; and the bandage should be wrapped over the plaster, and his hand should be tied to his neck, and it should be left, and the patient's hand should not be moved for seven days.
And the patient's diet should be light until the limb is strengthened, for this makes his healing easier.
And then the diet should be increased gradually over five or seven days and movement should be exercised; and therefore, if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to the dampness that affects it or another cause, then cautery with three punctures should be administered there, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and the bandage are reapplied several times, and it is not firm and hangs loose and cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that its nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are either severed or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
For the dislocation that occurs between the foot and the ankle or the back parts, reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And another bandage should be applied there, and treat it until it is healed.
And if during healing heaviness and slowness of movement occur in the limb, then the patient should use baths many times until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its former natural state.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the treatment of hip dislocation.
Know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more so to the front than to the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is apparent to the sight or touch, according to whatever way it is dislocated. And when you align the dislocated hip with its socket, this is clearly evident to you, because the joint is concave and he cannot bend the leg, nor touch his thigh with his hand.
And it is necessary that you take care and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess forms, because if a hot abscess forms, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely; and especially when it is in the posterior parts, because it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and of greater pain, and often causes death with it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that his leg should be extended with two hands, and his thigh expanded, and the physician's hands should be on the hip and under it, and he should press the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then return it thus: bend the leg with your hand, until you strike the hip with the root of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous and very strong extension.
And it is necessary that two assistants extend the arm, and let two other assistants hold that extension so that it is not disturbed during the extension.
And then let the arm be revolved in every direction, after it has been wrapped in a long, doubled cloth over his hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this may assist in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with forceful pressure until it returns [to its place].
And after the reduction, it is necessary that he be placed upon a plaster which has styptic and drying properties with egg white, and it should be bound tightly with a descending bandage, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left thus for several days.
And then let it be unbound; and if the joint is formed in its place, then unbind his bandage and leave it thus.
And if you see the joint not well tightened, then reiterate the plaster and the bandage, and leave it thus for several days until it is tightened; and then unbind it.
But if there is severity in the reduction of the joint, and the patient's disposition is toward movement, then administer moist bathing and gentle friction, and compression, until he is relieved; or place on the joint alliaria, that is, a very fatty substance; and then let it be bandaged, and leave it on the bone day and night. And then remove that, and put him in a bath; and when he is sufficiently clean, then rub the joint with tempered friction, and then reiterate the alliaria, that is, a very fatty ointment, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the intervention of a bath, until it is softened.
And if you wish, place on it warm, moist cow dung and bind it tightly; and do this several times, because it is moist and returns it to its proper condition.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the wrist, that is, of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
Sometimes the wrist is dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easy, contrary to other joints; however, speed is necessary in its reduction at the time it is dislocated, before the place is inflamed or a hot abscess occurs there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table, and the assistant extends the patient's hand, and the physician places his hand or his palm on the prominence of the joint, and pulls it until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already turned towards the belly of the hand; then the patient places the back of his hand on a table during extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be inwards on the table, so that the physician's hand falls on that prominence of the joint.
And so if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then constrict it with a plaster, allowing the inflammation to subside, and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will not be able to achieve reduction after several days have passed.
However, if the joint remains in its distorted position, and the patient experiences no pain except that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything, then you should know that the nerves are pinched or crushed; and then there is no remedy except to constrict it with a cautery near the injured area, and quickly apply something to it.
Therefore, when the injury is reduced, then apply the plaster we have recommended, and let it be left and tightened for five days; and then let it be untied and left, and let his hand be exercised.
But if its movement is impeded and some heaviness occurs, then soothe it with warm water and friction several times until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in every part.
And when one of those fingers is dislocated towards the back of the palm or towards the belly of the hand, then straighten the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumbs until it returns to its place.
Then support the head of the finger, and hang it downwards from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is straightened and set on that same day.
And when it is night, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for a few days until it is tight/strong.
And do similarly if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are dislodged from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death awaits the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is expelled involuntarily, and they cannot retain it; and often some of their limbs are paralyzed, or their two legs, or their two arms, or one of them; or a vertebra is dislodged from its place, for often it is dislodged and sometimes the displacement of these is in four parts.
Therefore, that is dislodged in the posterior parts and is called paralysis.
But the cure for it is that then you consider if the paralysis occurred from a fever, for then in that case there is neither cure nor healing at all.
But in that which comes from a fall or from a blow or similar to those, the Ancients already described the methods of cure with lengthy discourse, of which the most returns with profit; but I have already abridged that, of which little excuses the excess, for my proximity to my intended explanation, with which I have set forth and formed this from diverse instruments, with which I will show this and explain that.
And of those, only that which occurs in the two properly is cured with what I recounted.
And it is so that the patient is laid on his face on a soft cushion close to a wall, and a soft mattress is placed under him so that his feet are not injured.
And then a straight pole is placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the bed near his head, and another pole near his feet at the other end of the bed.
And the attendant holds the pole, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another attendant holds the other in that manner.
And then wrap around the patient's head and below the armpits a strong linen bandage; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is near his head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around his hips and thighs, and below his legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is near his feet.
And then each attendant extends the bandage to the part of the pole, and the two poles and the bandages should not be moved from their places where they are fixed, but however, they are not fixed in the way we have said.
And the physician places his palms on the spine with strength until it returns, or places a board on it, and then adjusts his feet on the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three braccia, and dig into the wall which we have said is near the patient a place into which the end of the board fits. And then place half of the board on the spine, and the physician places his feet on the other end, and pulls strongly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open with a turner which is revolved by hand; and it is so that you place in the ground under the patient's head at the back of the neck two poles, the length of which is one arm's length, and under their head at the back of the neck similarly two poles; and let the length between each pole be one palm, and a hole is already made in each pole through which a turner fits, and each is firmly fixed in the ground so that it is not moved.
And let there be added a round pole on which is the turner, and let it be revolved in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end is a hole into which the pole is fitted, the length of which is one palm, with that which is revolved; and in the two other poles is something similar to that.
And then tighten the ropes that you have tied to the patient's feet in the hole which is under their head, and the ropes that you have tied to their thighs in the turner which is under their feet.
And then beside each turner let a minister stand who twists the turner with his hand, and the physician adjusts the vertebra as we have said.
And then, once the vertebra is returned and the location is adjusted, you should apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a cloth bandage whose width should be three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length should be sufficient to cover the area of the curvature, and the healthy part of the vertebra.
And then it should be bound with the rope we have mentioned, and give the patient light nourishment until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of the healing, then medicines should be administered that soften and smooth, with the administration of the bandage for a long time, which we have recommended; and a lead plate is applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the area behind the vertebrae of the back, and it is caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already swells, so that no attempt should be made to treat it with this medicine, for it will very quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint suffer only dislocation, and not what happens to other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its dislocation inwards is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without pain, and it is the place where the pain is often accompanied by a hot abscess, because the head of the femur has already reached there.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens outwards, is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And a sign of that to which dislocation happens forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but however he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk he cannot do so forwards; and his urine is retained and his groin is inflamed, and he walks on his heel.
And a sign of that to which dislocation happens backwards is that he cannot extend his knee, nor can he bend it before bending his hip, and his leg is shorter than the other, and his groin is softened, and the head of the femur is towards that place.
But the reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and chronic, that it has already become set with time, and you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not entirely cured; and then no presumption should be made in that matter.
But in the case where the dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types of dislocation, then take care to return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it quickly returns and needs no other treatment.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part, either with your hands or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant extend the hand of that one from above so that he places his hands below his heels; and then tighten a strong bandage around the root of the thigh, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be from the inside of the groin to the area of the fork (presumably the pubic bone), or from the back to the area of the back.
And let the extension of all these be at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type [of dislocation] that which we shall say of its proper cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inner parts, it is necessary that the patient be supine on his sound side; and then place the ligatures on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place which is below the groin; and then extend the ligature upwards from the part of the groin to the highest part of the fork (pubic area).
And then another strong attendant grasps his arms, and gently pulls the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a forceful extension, because that will return it to its place.
And this type is of easier cure, with which this limb is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is difficult for you and does not obey the treatment with this type of cure at all, then it is necessary that the patient's two feet be bound together with a strong, light ligature, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each (ligature) be the quantity of four fingers, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended from the head by a beam, and let him be indoors; and let his height from the ground be the quantity of two arms' lengths.
And then order a strong young man to reduce the head of the thigh, and let another young man support the patient, and the other young man, reducing the joint with strength and pushing, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, the patient should lie on his back as we have recommended in the case of the cold application; and let the ligature be tightened on the affected leg and on his feet.
And then two beams should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then let adjusted beams be placed in the middle of the mound placed on which wet cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the beam is between his thighs, so that during extension the lower parts are not pulled.
And then each assistant extends on his side, and the physician with his hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narration on a mound; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected joint, and then compresses that with the other hand, and with that compression he puts that extended to the lower parts to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower parts, and he is elevated above the earth; rather it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the elevation if his hip is dislocated to the outside parts, according to what we have said about his lying on two hips; and he is on his face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, there is no great harm.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a tablet on the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should be — and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those equal and the patient tightens his leg and extends it without difficulty, then you know that the member is returned as it should be — and then you adjust the two thighs and apply a plaster and bind it so that the hip is not moved in any of the parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
And then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is now healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and plaster that, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
And then untie it, and have him delay ambulation for a few days, until he is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, and inwards, and downwards more towards the back; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his leg to his thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its types is that you make the patient lie straight, so that he extends his leg, if he can; apply your two hands to the front of his leg, and place his foot between your feet; then apply his knee to your palms, and hold those between your fingers on his knee with strength, and another attendant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to his thigh easily and without effort.
And then plaster it and bind the leg to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if its reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with the bandages of which the narrative preceded in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the talus (ankle bone).
The talus is sometimes moved by a slight displacement, and sometimes dislocated completely, and its dislocation is made either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the swollen talus protrudes towards the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its displacement is a gentle reduction, and it is that it should be extended easily with the hands and pulled until it returns [to its place].
But in treating it when it is completely dislocated, you must have the patient lie down straight, and have a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind at the middle of his back.
Then you, with your right hand, take the foot from the upper part, and with your left hand, from the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left, twice, and then extend with the left; and push the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice as we have described.
Then push the foot to the leg a third time, and you will be at the ankle.
Then it will return in one or two tries according to this method, and if you see the foot straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation, for it will return.
But if the reduction is forbidden to you by what we have recounted, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and tightly fasten a strong rope to the ground, so that it falls between their thighs, and you will already have wrapped a cloth around them so that it doesn't hurt the patient.
Then let the patient's attendant hold their thighs, and let another attendant extend the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied around the ankle; and let each attendant extend against the extension of their partner, and let the rope remain between the patient's thighs, restraining it, so that their body is not pulled downwards against their extension.
Then the physician reduces the dislocation with their hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh lower down, because the dislocation easily returns.
And so when the dislocation returns and the success of its reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with bandages.
And it is necessary that you fear or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not too tight and severely injures them.
Then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
Then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and worsened, and then it will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then you must administer to its healing that which we narrated previously, in another place of treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
It is necessary that the dislocated [toe] be healed with slight extension without work; and that which is not difficult, is easier beforehand.
But if the dislocation is in some of the bones of the instep, then you must make the patient place his foot on a level place on the ground or on a table, and remain so as if he were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from the joints, and then press that with your foot with force until it returns, and you see that it is healed and no prominence appears in the place.
And then place under the sole of his foot a board that covers the whole foot, to which are attached two caps [possibly referring to some kind of splint or support]; and then tighten that with a decent and firm structure for three days; and then untie it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its recovery is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When any of those causes happen, and you engage yourself in its cure and in his restoration, many times death occurs.
And therefore it is not fitting that anyone presume upon his cure except one who is wise in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow [to act], not rash nor presumptuous; and that he use in the beginning medicines that only reduce hot abscesses, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except for one in whom you expect salvation from destruction, with alleviation of the disease, and in whom some hope appears. And then strive immediately to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before a hot abscess develops.
And then, if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen which reduces hot abscesses and treat the wound with what is suitable for it of drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the jagged ends of the bone are separated, then you must bring them together, and do in this what we have said in the simple diseases of what precedes in those locations.
And guard and avoid, according to your ability, that which enters the paths of fear as my preaching has preceded you; for this is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more healthful to your true body than those in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
